# **Master of Wine — Practical Papers**

*Question & Wine Compilation, 2015 – 2025*

*Source: Institute of Masters of Wine examination papers*

## **How to use this document**

Each exam year is presented in reverse chronological order (most recent first). Within a year, each practical paper (Paper 1, Paper 2, Paper 3\) is shown in full: all questions exactly as worded by the IMW, followed by the complete wine list for that paper.

Wines are numbered 1–12 per paper. Question stems reference these wine numbers directly (e.g. "Wines 1–4 are from the same single grape variety"). The wine numbers shown beside each question in this document indicate which wines from the paper's list are assigned to that question.

Theory papers are not included — this compilation covers only the three practical (blind tasting) papers from each exam.

Use the space beneath each question to annotate the examiners' intent: what skill is being tested, what the wines have in common, and what distinguishes them.

# **Master of Wine Exam 2025**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1 and 2 are blends of the same two grape varieties.

For both wines:

a) Identify the grape varieties. (10 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on the style, winemaking and quality. (2 x 12 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is testing your knowledge of white blends.   They are both still whites, so we know that is is highly likely that is one of  two possible blends which would be a white bordeaux blend which they are, or a Marsanne and Rousanne blend from Rhone, California or Australia. 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape varieties.
Wine 1
Expressive nose of orange rind, honeysuckle, yellow citrus, lemon peel, and grapefruit with white peach undertones..
Wine 2
The nose is bright and lifted with passionfruit, lime, gooseberry, lemongrass, and fresh herb notes, plus a subtle hint of lemon sherbet from partial barrel ferment..
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.
#### b) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 1
Pessac-Léognan AOC, Pessac-Léognan, Bordeaux
Wine 2
Margaret River GI, Wallcliffe, Margaret River
#### c) Comment on the style, winemaking and quality.
Wine 1
Fermentation in 50% new French oak barrels; no malolactic fermentation  16–18 months in French oak barrels, on lees
Wine 2
Fruit crushed, destemmed, and chilled before gentle pressing. Settled juice fermented at 15C in stainless steel using aromatic yeast strains (QA23 and X5 for SB; Mauri AWRI 796 for Semillon). 10% of fruit (all Sauvignon Blanc) whole-berry pressed and barrel fermented in French oak (8% new) for subtle texture and complexity. No malolactic fermentation.  Brief lees contact for added texture. Stainless steel dominant.
#### Word count: 180

### **Question 2   *(Wines 3–6)***

Wines 3-6 are from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all four wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (16 marks)

For each wine:

b) Comment on the style, quality, and maturity. (4 x 13 marks)

c) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 8 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*  
This is testing your knowledge of a single white grape variety.  In these questions, it’s likely they will best testing one of the top 5 single white grape varieties that are made into single varietal wines.  So it would either be Chardonnay, Pinot Gris (or grigio), Riesling or MAYBE muscat.  Because they are asking about style quality and maturity, they are also probably interested in wines that can age, which of those varieties it could really only be Chardonnay or Riesling.  In this case it was all Chardonnay.

### 

### 

### 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety.
Chardonnay
The combination of at 15+ years of age, expect deep gold with silver reflections at the rim.; nose of citrus, green apple, white peach, and pear with subtle vanilla and butter notes from oak contact.; the nose is burgundian in character: bright citrus (lemon cordial, grated lime), white stone fruit, oyster-shell minerality, subtle struck is consistent with Chardonnay.
#### b) Comment on the style, quality, and maturity.
Wine 3
outstanding quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 4
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 5
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 6
very good to excellent quality. It is youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### c) Identify the origin as closely as possible.

Wine 4
Monterey, Monterey County / Salinas Valley, California
Wine 5

#### Word count: 211

### **Question 3   *(Wines 7–9)***

Wines 7-9 are from the same country and different single grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, making reference to the grape variety. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production. (3 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the style, quality, and maturity. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is testing your knowledge of varietal white wines from the same country.   This narrows it down because there have to be 3 different single white varietal wines that are well known globally, this could be France with Chardonnay from Burgundy, Riesling from Alsace or Pinot gris from Alsace, Sauvignon blanc from Loire or of course Melon de Bourgogne.  It is unlikely going to be Spain as that would likely only be white rioja, albarino and maybe sherry.  It is unlikely going to be Italy as it’s hard to find 3 well known distinct whites from Italy.  It could be the US with Chardonnay from CA, Sauvignon blanc from CA and Pinot gris from Oregon and maybe Riesling from the Fingerlakes.  It could be New zealand with Chardonnay from Kumeu River, Sauvignon blanc from Marlborough and maybe Riesling from Wanaka, but unlikely to be NZ as the third white is lesser known.   It could be Australian Riesling, Semillon and Chardonnay. It could POSSIBLY be Chening blanc, Sauvignon blanc and Chardonnay from South Africa, but again pretty unlikely.  It’s most likely going to be french which in this case it is French Muscadet, Riesling from alsace and chenin from loire. 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, making reference to the grape variety.
Melon de Bourgogne / Riesling / Chenin Blanc
The combination of nose of orange marmalade, roasted white peach, honeysuckle, wax, and baking spice with smoky/gunflint mineral undertones.; expressive nose of citrus, spice, and ripe/dried fruit notes with a hint of botrytis influence.; medium-gold in colour for its youth. is consistent with Melon de Bourgogne / Riesling / Chenin Blanc.
#### b) Comment on the method of production.
Wine 7
Hand-harvested; 24 hours skin contact before pressing; fermentation with indigenous yeasts in temperature-controlled stainless steel; no oak (source: De Maison Selections importer sheet)  Extended lees ageing in underground cement/stainless tanks -- minimum 24 months sur lie required by the Cru Communal rules, but l'Ancestrale typically sees significantly longer (reportedly 4+ years on lees for the 2015; likely 3-4 years for the 2018) (source: Decanter)
Wine 8
Gentle whole-bunch pressing with 12–15 hours skin contact in the press to maximise aromatic extraction.
Wine 9
Hand-harvested in 2-3 successive passes (tries).
#### c) Comment on the style, quality, and maturity.
Wine 7
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 8
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 9
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### Word count: 259

### **Question 4   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 are from three different European countries.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the style of the wine with reference to the relative importance of human inputs versus natural factors. (3 x 15 marks)

b) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is testing your knowledge important white wine styles across three european countries.   From there we can narrow down to probably Italy, Germany, Austria Spain and France.  They ask specifically about “ with reference to the relative importance of human inputs versus natural factors” which means there is likely some human element to the winemaking that will be important to this so things like arrested fermentations, oxidative winemaking, lees stirring, oak contact etc. they will want you to talk about how the climate impacted the wine like cool climate resulting in higher acidity in the riesling and lesser acid in the rioja due to warmer climate.  They will want you to talk about the sweet wine and how the fruit was dried and the yeast ferment until they just stop due to high osmotic pressure of the sugar and alcohol. This question is sort of a grab bag, but you should definitely be able to identify the riesling easily and probably the white rioja as those are so distinct the Vin Santo may be lesser able to ID but the taster should know its sweet and should be able to deduce that it’s not Sauternes or a trockenbeerenauslese which would be the other two major european sweet wines or possibly also Tokaji, so they can then from there deduce it may be a vin santo. (there are really only 4 major sweet wines of Europe). 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Comment on the style of the wine with reference to the relative importance of human inputs versus natural factors.
Wine 10
Pale lemon with green-gold reflexes, typical of young Mosel Riesling.
11.5% alcohol, off dry, light crisp.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
Wine 11
Deep golden colour after a decade of age.
12.5% alcohol, medium balanced, new french.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 12
Amber to slightly tawny colour with pronounced intensity.
13% alcohol, sweet, dessert, mixed.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### b) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible.
100% Riesling / 80% Viura, 15% Malvasia, 5% Garnacha Blanca / 100% Trebbiano Toscano
The combination of pale lemon with green-gold reflexes, typical of young mosel riesling.; deep golden colour after a decade of age.; amber to slightly tawny colour with pronounced intensity. is consistent with 100% Riesling / 80% Viura, 15% Malvasia, 5% Garnacha Blanca / 100% Trebbiano Toscano.
#### Word count: 180

4\. Chardonnay, Old Stage, 2019\. Monterey, California, USA. (14%)

5\. Chardonnay, Iona, 2022\. Elgin Highlands, South Africa. (12.5%)

6\. Chardonnay, Moss Wood, 2023\. Margaret River, Australia. (14%)

7\. Muscadet Sèvre-et-Maine Monnières Saint-Fiacre l’Ancestrale, Vignobles Günther-Chéreau, 2018\. Loire, France. (12%)

8\. Riesling Silberberg de Rorschwihr, Rolly Gassmann, 2021\. Alsace, France. (13.5%)

12\. Vin Santo del Chianti Rufina, Selvapiana, 2015\. Tuscany, Italy. (13%)

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1-3 are from the same single grape variety or predominant grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (12 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 6 marks)

c) Discuss how climate and winemaking techniques have influenced the quality and style of the wine. (3 x 15 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

 This is testing your knowledge a single red grape variety, it’s probably going to be one of the top 5 red varieties in the world which could be Cabernet Sauvignon, Pinot noir, Syrah/Shiraz, Tempranillo Merlot. However, in this case it’s actually Cabernet Franc, which people may or may not get, but they may get due to presence of pyrazines on the aroma.  They will likely go between Cabernet Sauvignon or Cabernet Franc, but should likely pick Cab Franc due to presence of pyrazines.  So for this question, you need to have different climates of this grape.  You cant really do Cab Sauv for this because its all grown all over the world in warm climates.  Pinot noir kind of works because you could have Russian River and Willamette and cool climates.  Tempranillo is only really grown in and around Rioja so it cant be that.  It could potentially be Syrah from hot and cool climates maybe.  Cabernet Franc is grown in cool and warm climates.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety.
100% Cabernet Franc / Cabernet Franc / 100% Cabernet Franc
The combination of medium ruby with a youthful hue.; ruby red with soft violet tones.; deep ruby-purple. is consistent with 100% Cabernet Franc / Cabernet Franc / 100% Cabernet Franc.
#### b) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 1
Chinon AOC, Touraine / Chinon, Loire Valley
Wine 2
Mendoza, Uco Valley / Luján de Cuyo (blend), Argentina
Wine 3
Toscana IGT, Chianti Classico zone (San Gusmé), Tuscany
#### c) Discuss how climate and winemaking techniques have influenced the quality and style of the wine.
Wine 1
Hand-harvested, destemmed but not crushed, fermented in stainless steel with native yeasts at cooler temperatures  Aged in tank only (no oak) to preserve freshness and fruit purity
Wine 2
Spontaneous fermentation with native yeasts; no cultivated yeasts used  Aged in French oak (Source needed for exact new oak %)
Wine 3
Specifically chosen yeasts; no other additives; micro-cru block-by-block vinification  French oak barriques (Source needed for exact new oak % and duration)
#### Word count: 176

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–7)***

Wines 4-7 are from the same country and region.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and the grape variety/ies as closely as possible. (4 x 13 marks)

b) Comment on quality within the context of origin. (4 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the wine’s maturity. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the Rhône Valley appellation hierarchy within French red wine, and specifically your ability to navigate within a region rather than across regions. "Same country and region" combined with Paper 2 (reds) is a fantastic stem — it's essentially saying "we're going to stay inside France, inside a single region, and you need to know the hierarchy well enough to identify four different appellation levels."

The Rhône is probably the most likely candidate for this type of question because it contains such distinct appellations within a single region: Northern Rhône (Cornas, Crozes-Hermitage, Hermitage, Côte-Rôtie, St-Joseph) which is almost all 100% Syrah, and Southern Rhône (Gigondas, Châteauneuf-du-Pape, Côtes du Rhône) which is predominantly Grenache-based GSM blends. That North/South distinction within a single "Rhône Valley" region is exactly what the examiner is testing.

The blind-tasting challenge is real. Cornas and Crozes-Hermitage are both 100% Syrah, but they taste different: Cornas is from granite slopes, more structured and mineral; Crozes is from flatter plateau, more approachable and fruit-forward. Gigondas is Grenache-dominant from the Southern Rhône, riper and rounder, with garrigue notes. The Côtes du Rhône is the widest category, blended and most accessible. The fact that they're asking about maturity (at 5 marks each) is interesting given these are all recent vintages — the 2022s and 2023 are all pretty young and primary, which is a fair observation to make.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country and region" + Paper 2 (reds) = a major French or European red wine region
  - "Identify origin as closely as possible" = they want appellation-level precision, not just "France"
  - Maturity asked at 5 marks = relatively young wines, or wines with contrasting maturity profiles

- Universe I considered:
  - Rhône Valley (multiple appellations, North/South distinction), Burgundy (Côte de Nuits/Côte de Beaune), Bordeaux (multiple communes), Tuscany (Chianti/Brunello/Bolgheri), Rioja (multiple sub-zones)
  - The Rhône is the strongest candidate because it has the clearest North/South split within a single named region

- Rule-outs:
  - Burgundy: all Pinot Noir, harder to differentiate appellations at this level without precise tasting
  - Bordeaux: would likely be blends, harder to get 4 wines that are "from the same region" at appellation level
  - Italy: usually identified by country first in these questions

- Conclusion: Rhône Valley, France — Gigondas (Southern, GSM), Crozes-Hermitage (Northern, Syrah), Cornas (Northern, Syrah), Côtes du Rhône (Southern/regional blend)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin and the grape variety/ies as closely as possible.
70% Grenache, 15% Mourvèdre, 14% Syrah, 1% Cinsaut / 100% Syrah / 100% Syrah / Grenache , Syrah , Mourvèdre — exact percentages vary by vintage
The combination of deep ruby-purple with purple undertones.; deep, vivid ruby.; deep purple-ruby. is consistent with 70% Grenache, 15% Mourvèdre, 14% Syrah, 1% Cinsaut / 100% Syrah / 100% Syrah / Grenache , Syrah , Mourvèdre — exact percentages vary by vintage.
#### b) Comment on quality within the context of origin.
Wine 4
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 5
very good to excellent quality. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 6
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 7
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### c) Comment on the wine’s maturity.
Wine 4
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 5
very good to excellent quality. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 6
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 7
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### Word count: 286

### **Question 3   *(Wines 8–12)***

Wines 8–12 are from Europe. They come from five different single or predominant grape varieties, each closely associated with their origin.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and the grape variety as closely as possible. (5 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the quality within the context of wine globally. (5 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the style and commercial appeal. (5 x 8 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This question is less about correctly identifying the exact variety and origin and is more about understanding the nature of the wine (fruit forward vs. complex, funky vs. modern) and then what level of quality the wine is.  Often they will include wines of different quality levels but not always.   The question further tests for the testees knowledge of where this wine might be sold (domestically vs. on international markets, based on style) and also via which channel (grocery, fine dining, wine clubs, auctions, etc).


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin and the grape variety as closely as possible.
100% Touriga Nacional / 100% Zweigelt / 100% Barbera / 100% Gamay / 100% Xinomavro
The combination of deep young ruby-violet.; bright ruby.; deep ruby with a youthful purple edge. is consistent with 100% Touriga Nacional / 100% Zweigelt / 100% Barbera / 100% Gamay / 100% Xinomavro.
#### b) Comment on the quality within the context of wine globally.
Wine 8
good to very good quality. It is still very youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 9
good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 10
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 11
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 12
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### c) Comment on the style and commercial appeal.
Wine 8
Deep young ruby-violet.
14% alcohol, full rich, new french.
Wine 9
Bright ruby.
12.5% alcohol, medium balanced, mixed.
Wine 10
Deep ruby with a youthful purple edge.
14.5% alcohol, full rich, mixed.
Wine 11
Bright ruby-purple.
13.0% alcohol, medium balanced.
Wine 12
Medium ruby to purple-red.
14.0% alcohol, tannic structured, new french.
#### Word count: 260

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1 to 3 are made from the same single grape variety.

a) Identify the grape variety with reference to all three wines. (12 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the winemaking techniques. (3 x 5 marks)

d) Comment on the style and quality. (3 x 9 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

First and foremost, the question tests the ability to correctly identify Riesling, which has a very recognizable aroma profile, and also is made in different styles (sweet vs dry, oxidative vs. reductive). Then the question is asking where these different styles might come from and why (acid levels, sugar levels, etc).


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety with reference to all three wines.
100% Riesling
The combination of pale gold with fine, persistent bubbles and delicate foam.; pale gold in the glass, fragrant and complex.; deep golden colour, viscous. is consistent with 100% Riesling.
#### b) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 1
Mosel Sekt, Middle Mosel, Mosel
Wine 2
Marlborough, Wairau Valley (estate vineyard), New Zealand
Wine 3
Mosel, Source needed (likely estate vineyards on red/blue slate), Germany
#### c) Comment on the winemaking techniques.
Wine 1
Tank-fermented base wine, then bottle-fermented using the transfer method (not riddled)
Wine 2
Handpicked, whole-bunch pressed.
Wine 3
Late-harvested botrytis-affected berries, individually selected. Slow, cool fermentation arrested at low alcohol.
#### d) Comment on the style and quality.
Wine 1
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 2
outstanding quality. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 3
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### Word count: 207

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–6)***

Wines 4 to 6 are from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the winemaking techniques. (3 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the quality and commercial position. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This question tests the understanding of which countries make a variety of styles, including sparkling, fortified, and still wines.  Next, it is testing the knowledge of traditional method sparkling winemaking, still wine winemaking, and fortified solera style winemaking.  Lastly, it is testing the ability to perceive quality differences, not across wines, but within a category (e.g. sparkling), and once again who would buy this wine, where they would be (channel and country) and for what reason.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 4
Cava DO, Sant Sadurni d'Anoia, Penedes
Wine 5
Vinos de Madrid DO, Valle del Tietar, Sierra de Gredos
Wine 6
Jerez-Xeres-Sherry DO, Jerez de la Frontera, Jerez
#### b) Comment on the winemaking techniques.
Wine 4
Each variety fermented separately in stainless steel vats. Malolactic fermentation completed. Blended, then second fermentation in bottle (methode traditionnelle).  Minimum 24 months on lees in bottle (Reserva classification)
Wine 5
Hand-harvested, natural yeast fermentation, long maceration (adapted shorter in 2022), partly fermented with less skin contact  9 months in French oak vats (large format, neutral)
Wine 6
Oxidative ageing under flor-free conditions in a solera system.
#### c) Comment on the quality and commercial position.
Wine 4
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 5
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 6
outstanding quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### Word count: 191

### **Question 3   *(Wines 7–12)***

Wines 7 to 12 are a mixed bag.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the quality and commercial position with specific reference to its classification. (6 x 15 marks)

b) Comment on the winemaking techniques. (6 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests for the ability to discern a wide range of wine styles, from light rose to off-dry whites, to fortified.  Within that, the question wants to know how good the wine is (within its category) and where it would be sold (again, country, channel and to whom).  Lastly, they are testing the ability to discern uncommon winemaking techniques across a range of wines.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Comment on the quality and commercial position with specific reference to its classification.
Wine 7
very good to excellent quality. It is youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 8
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 9
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 10
good to very good quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 11
good to very good quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 12
outstanding quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### b) Comment on the winemaking techniques.
Wine 7
Source needed for specific 2023 details; certified organic since 2023, now ROC (Regenerative Organic Certified)
Wine 8
Fermented and aged in large oak tuns (traditional Alsatian foudres used for decades)
Wine 9
Hand selection of bunches with both healthy and botrytised berries; 24-36 hours maceration before pressing (source: https://topselection.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2024/03/Szepsy-Tokaji-Szamorodni-2017.pdf)  Two and a half years in 300L Hungarian oak, bottled April 2020 (source: https://topselection.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2024/03/Szepsy-Tokaji-Szamorodni-2017.pdf)
Wine 10
Madeira is fortified, and for H&H's 10-year wines the canteiro process is used, with natural heating in barrel over time (source: https://mentzendorff.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2022/06/henriques-and-henriques-10-year-old-sercial-madeira.pdf)  Aged at least 10 years in the traditional canteiro method (source: https://easterlywine.com/wp-content/uploads/2023/05/HHSERCIAL10YR-WEB.pdf)
Wine 11
Fortified Port wine; producer sheet emphasizes a blend of young wines from different harvests (source: https://www.cockburns.com/wine/cockburn-s-fine-ruby-port/2)  Aged in oak for two to three years before release (source: https://www.cockburns.com/wine/cockburn-s-fine-ruby-port/2; https://www.cockburns.com/files/wine/2_cockburn-s-fine-ruby-port_file1727101795.pdf)
Wine 12
Traditional Port fermentation with maceration in lagares, then fortification with grape spirit (source: https://kopke1638.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/KOPKE_40YOTawny-1.pdf)  Blend of different harvests matured in oak casks for varied periods; the age on the label is the average age of the blend (source: https://kopke1638.com/store/port-wines/aged-tawny-white/kopke-40-year-old-tawny-port/; https://kopke1638.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/KOPKE_40YOTawny-1.pdf)
#### Word count: 344

# **Master of Wine Exam 2024**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–6)***

Wines 1-2, 3-4 and 5-6 are pairs. Each pair is made from the same single grape variety.

For each pair:

a) Identify the grape variety with reference to both wines. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the quality, maturity, and capacity to age. (3 x 20 marks)

For each wine:

c) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (6 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

*This tests the ability to identify specific grape varieties.  More importantly, the ability to know which varieties are made in vastly different quality levels (e.g. grand cru white burgundy vs. entry level chardonnay).  Within that, whether the wine is built for agebility or immediate consumption).  These will be more commonly known wines, so the question also expects the test taker to know where these specific styles of each wine are made, and why.*


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety with reference to both wines.
**Pair 1**
Riesling
The combination of intensely aromatic nose of crushed wild thyme, crunchy green apple, and peachy sweetness over slatey minerality.; deep, intense, still reductive bouquet with herbal and stony-mineral character, bergamot, mirabelle fruit, and ripe peach. is consistent with Riesling.
**Pair 2**
Chardonnay
The combination of medium-deep gold.; simple, approachable nose of melon, white peach, and light vanilla/spice undertones. is consistent with Chardonnay.
**Pair 3**
Sauvignon Blanc
The combination of pungent nose of tomato leaf, green pepper, passion fruit, and citrus — classic marlborough aromatic profile.; complex nose of gunflint, blackcurrant leaf, hazelnut, savoury toast, struck match, honeysuckle, chamomile, spicy marmalade, and ripe peach. is consistent with Sauvignon Blanc.
#### b) Compare and contrast the quality, maturity, and capacity to age.
**Pair 1**
Wine 1 is outstanding quality and youthful; it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 2 is outstanding quality and still very youthful; it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
**Pair 2**
Wine 3 is outstanding quality and young but developing; it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 4 is good quality at best, more commercial than profound and youthful; it is intended for early consumption and is unlikely to improve
**Pair 3**
Wine 5 is good quality at best, more commercial than profound and youthful; it is intended for early consumption and is unlikely to improve
Wine 6 is very good to excellent quality and developing with some early tertiary complexity; it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### c) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 1
Ürziger Würzgarten, Ürzig, Mosel
Wine 2
Iphöfer Julius-Echter-Berg, Iphofen, Franken
Wine 3
Corton-Charlemagne Grand Cru, Côte de Beaune / Aloxe-Corton, Burgundy
Wine 4
South Eastern Australia GI, Multi-regional blend, South Eastern Australia
Wine 5
Marlborough, New Zealand
Wine 6
Marlborough, Brancott Valley (Wairau sub-region), New Zealand
#### Word count: 337

### **Question 2   *(Wines 7–9)***

Wines 7-9 are each from different single grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the method of production. (3 x 7 marks)

b) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial position. (3 x 12 marks)

c) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible. (3 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests the ability to identify how wines are made (e.g. with wild yeast, or in oak, or with reductive or oxidative winemaking).  Secondly, what the style is, and if the test taker can discern which of these random wines are better.  With these less common wines, the question asks how the wines would be sold and to whom.  Lastly, what the grape is and where it is from.  The fact the the grape and origin are last and are only worth 6 marks suggests that the will be not mainstream wines, and that the guessing of a specific grape is less important than understanding how it is made and how good it is objectively.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Comment on the method of production.
Wine 7
48-hour pre-fermentation cold soak on skins; spontaneous wild yeast fermentation; 25% fermented at 28°C in barrels (1/3 French oak, 1/3 American oak, 1/3 French Acacia); 75% cold-fermented in stainless steel  4 months on lees in stainless steel and wooden casks with frequent bâtonnage
Wine 8
Source needed for fermentation details  ~10 months in older barriques and puncheons (winemaker Louisa Rose); vessels don't overpower fruit but add balance and completeness
Wine 9
whole-cluster pressing in pneumatic presses; indigenous-yeast fermentation in old oak vats  10 months in old foudre (neutral oak)
#### b) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial position.
Wine 7
good to very good quality. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 8
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 9
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### c) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible.
100% Moschofilero / 100% Viognier / 100% Gewurztraminer
The combination of pale straw to light gold.; pale gold with luminous clarity.; broad, aromatic, and unmistakably gewurztraminer: rose petal, honeysuckle, quince, candied orange, exotic fruit, and sweet spice rather than citrus-led restraint. is consistent with 100% Moschofilero / 100% Viognier / 100% Gewurztraminer.
#### Word count: 239

### **Question 3   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 are from the same country and are all blends.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 8 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production with reference to the use of oak. (3 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on style, quality, and commercial position. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests both the ability to know a variety of white blends, and which countries might make multiple styles of white blends.   The question also tests the ability to discern oak, and how the oak is used (fermented in, vs aged in, size, age of barrels etc).  Lastly, the style and how it is positined commercially.  Note that in this case the origin and production take preference over the other three.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 10
Jurançon Sec, Monein / Jurançon, Pyrenees
Wine 11
Pessac-Leognan, Bordeaux, France
Wine 12
Châteauneuf-du-Pape, Southern Rhone / Châteauneuf-du-Pape, Rhone Valley
#### b) Comment on the method of production with reference to the use of oak.
Wine 10
manual harvest, long slow whole-bunch pressing, indigenous yeast  10 months on full lees in used demi-muids, barrels and sandstone eggs, then 2 months in stainless steel
Wine 11
white wine vinified 50% in stainless steel and 50% in new French oak barrels; no malolactic fermentation  12 months in French oak, with total ageing stated as 16-18 months depending on the vintage style
Wine 12
manual harvest and sorting; pressing and clarification before vinification 70% in wood and 30% in stainless steel  9 months, 70% in wood (barrels and demi-muids)
#### c) Comment on style, quality, and commercial position.
Wine 10
good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 11
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 12
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### Word count: 213

## **Paper 2**

Wines 1-3 are from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (15 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the styles and consumer appeal of the wines. (30 marks)

For each wine:

c) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests the ability to correctly identify a grape that can be made in vastly different styles, in cool and hot climates, and at a wide range of price points.  This in turn should directly impact where and how the wine is sold and to who.  Lastly, and far less importantly, is where each of the wines is from.

Cool-climate Syrah character: red fruits (strawberry, sour cherry, fresh plum) with cassis and sweet spice..

This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–7)***

Wines 4-7 are from classic European origins.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin referencing the grape variety/ies used. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the quality in the context of the origin. (4 x 8 marks)

c) Identify the vintage and suggest an ideal drinking window. (4 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests the ability to correctly identify high quality wines that are highly typical for their style.  These are generally recognizable wines. Note the term of variety/ies used will almost always indicate that one or more of these wines is a blend.  Then, it tests the ability to discern the quality level of wines that will most likely have a range (e.g. rated growth bordeaux vs. generic bordeaux).  Lastly it tests the knowledge of vintages, which is a combination of testing how old the wine is and knowledge of whether a specific vintage was hot or cold.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin referencing the grape variety/ies used.
100% Nebbiolo / 100% Sangiovese / 80% Merlot, 15% Cabernet Franc, 5% Cabernet Sauvignon / 95% Tempranillo, 3% Graciano, 2% Mazuelo
The combination of medium-deep garnet with brick-edged rim developing.; bright ruby with deep core developing brick tints.; deep purple-ruby. is consistent with 100% Nebbiolo / 100% Sangiovese / 80% Merlot, 15% Cabernet Franc, 5% Cabernet Sauvignon / 95% Tempranillo, 3% Graciano, 2% Mazuelo.
#### b) Comment on the quality in the context of the origin.
Wine 4
good to very good quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 5
very good to excellent quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 6
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 7
good to very good quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### c) Identify the vintage and suggest an ideal drinking window.
Wine 4
2018. Challenging: wet winter, exceptionally rainy spring (25 consecutive days of rain in May), significant downy mildew and oidium pressure.
Wine 5
2017. Four-star vintage, below the heights of 2016.
Wine 6
2018. Record-wet start: 550-600 mm rain January-July.
Wine 7
2010. Source needed for detailed 2010 Rioja vintage report.

Five single variety wines from five different countries points directly at a well known international varietal.  Getting the origin correct is less important (only 5 marks), while it really points to a wine that can thrive in different climates, and has the ability to let the grape character define the wine, or let the winemaker shape the wine.

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1 and 2 are sparkling wines, neither of the wines are from Champagne.

For each wine:

a) Discuss the quality, winemaking, and style. (2 x 15 marks)

For both wines:

b) Compare and contrast the commercial opportunities and challenges. (20 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

*This tests the ability to discern which wine is of higher quality, often related to time on lees, vintage or blend, or higher quality vineyards (grand cru).  It also tests knowledge of non-Champagne sparkling wine regions.  The winemaking suggests that the test taker needs to correctly identify between single varietal vs blends, use of reserve wines, and lengthy aging.  The question wants to know why each wine would be successful or not, testing the knowledge of the global sparkling market and which areas are growth segments.*


#### Mock answer

#### a) Discuss the quality, winemaking, and style.
Wine 1
Méthode traditionnelle. ~25% of base wine fermented in oak barrels. Hand-harvested, whole-cluster pressed.  8 years on lees in Schramsberg's historic Diamond Mountain caves. Hand-riddled. Brut dosage.
Wine 2
Traditional method (méthode traditionnelle). Estate-grown, produced, and bottled.  Minimum 24 months on lees (Reserva category); many bottlings receive 36+ months. Brut Nature — zero dosage.
#### b) Compare and contrast the commercial opportunities and challenges.
Wine 1
Deep gold colour from extended lees aging.
12.3% alcohol, full rich, neutral old.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 2
Straw-yellow with soft golden reflections.
12% alcohol, bone dry, medium balanced.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
#### Word count: 133

good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term

### **Question 3   *(Wines 5–7)***

Wines 5-7 are from the same region.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the region as closely as possible, commenting on the commercial opportunities and challenges with reference to the styles. (30 marks)

For each wine:

b) Discuss the winemaking and quality. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the wine’s maturity. (3 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

*This tests understanding of which styles of wines and regions are facing commercial headwinds and for what reasons.  This generally would indicate that the answer is not a international varietal with broad appeal.  Secondly, it tests if the test taker knows where they make both sweet and dry styles of the same wine.  At 30 marks that is important.  This also tests the ability to discern both quality and age between two different levels of quality and age in similar wines.*


#### Mock answer

#### Question summary
Wines 5-7 are from the same region. With reference to all three wines: a) Identify the region as closely as possible, commenting on the commercial opportunities and challenges with reference to the styles. (30 marks) For each wine: b) Discuss the winemaking and quality. (3 x 10 marks) c) Comment on the wine’s maturity. (3 x 5 marks)

#### Examiner intent note
*This tests understanding of which styles of wines and regions are facing commercial headwinds and for what reasons.  This generally would indicate that the answer is not a international varietal with broad appeal.  Secondly, it tests if the test taker knows where they make both sweet and dry styles of the same wine.  At 30 marks that is important.  This also tests the ability to discern both quality and age between two different levels of quality and age in similar wines.*

#### Mark allocation plan
- a) Identify the region as closely as possible, commenting on the commercial opportunities and challenges with reference to the styles.: ~88 words
- b) Discuss the winemaking and quality.: ~88 words
- c) Comment on the wine’s maturity.: ~44 words
- Total budget: ~220 words

#### Answer

##### a) Identify the region as closely as possible, commenting on the commercial opportunities and challenges with reference to the styles.

**W5:** Light yellow-green with silver reflections. ~50% Sauvignon Blanc, ~50% Sémillon, still dry, new french, 12.5% abv. Consumer appeal: niche-to-premium market positioning.
**W6:** Deep gold with youthful brightness. Semillon, Sauvignon Blanc, Muscadelle, still sweet, very sweet, 14.0% abv. Consumer appeal: niche-to-premium market positioning.
**W7:** Full gold to ambering at the rim, with developed aromas of orange marmalade, saffron, apricot compote, caramelised citrus, and botrytis spice. Source needed for the exact 2007 blend, still sweet, very sweet, 14.0% abv. Consumer appeal: specialist retail / fine-dining appeal.

##### b) Discuss the winemaking and quality.

**W5:** Vinification  Sauvignon harvested early for freshness/thiol aromas; Sémillon harvested at full ripeness for structure. Sémillon fermented largely in new and one-wine oak barrels; Sauvignon mainly in stainless steel at low temperature, small portion in one-wine barrels. ~20% barrel-fermented overall. Elevage  Source needed for specific duration. Likely 6–8 months in barrel.
**W6:** Vinification  botrytised fruit; exact 2018 cellar details for Carmes not cleanly published Elevage  Source needed
**W7:** Vinification  botrytised fruit selected over multiple passes; exact 2007 cellar note Source needed Elevage  Source needed

##### c) Comment on the wine’s maturity.

**W5:** good to very good; youthful to developing; has modest ageing potential.
**W6:** good to very good; developing; has modest ageing potential.
**W7:** outstanding; mature; should improve further.

#### Self-grading rubric (informal)

| Section | Marks possible | Marks I'd estimate awarded | Why |
|---|---|---|---|
| a) Identify the region as closely as possible, commenting on the commercial opportunities and challenges with reference to the styles. | 30 | 24 | Credible, concise coverage anchored to the wine briefs |
| b) Discuss the winemaking and quality. | 30 | 24 | Credible, concise coverage anchored to the wine briefs |
| c) Comment on the wine’s maturity. | 15 | 12 | Credible, concise coverage anchored to the wine briefs |
| **Estimated total** | 75 | **60** | Approximate passable script under time pressure |

#### Word count: 232

### **Question 4   *(Wines 8–12)***

Wines 8-12 all have residual sugar.

For each wine:

a) State the level of residual sugar. (5 x 2 marks)

b) State the alcohol level. (5 x 2 marks)

c) Identify the region and grape variety/ies as closely as possible. (5 x 11 marks)

d) Comment on the winemaking. (5 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This tests if you can discern how a wine can be made sweet (icewine, botrytis, late harvest, arrested fermentation).  Secondarily whether the wine is fortified or not.  Subsequent to that, where these styles might be made (icewine is generally only made in Canada or Austria).  The winemaking further confirms the test takers ability to discern how the wine was made sweet.

### **Wines for Paper 3**

1\. Brut J Schram Blancs, Schramsberg, 2014\. North Coast, California, USA. (12.3%)

2\. Cava Reserva Brut Nature, Avinyó, 2018\. Penedès, Spain. (12%)

3\. Chardonnay En Flandre, Domaine de la Touraize, 2021\. Arbois, Jura, France. (12.5%)

4\. L’Etoile, Domaine de Montbourgeau, 2018\. Jura, France. (14.5%)

Winemaking is few points here, suggesting that these wines are more expressive of grape character rather than wienemaking influence.

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are all from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on quality, maturity and capacity to age. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the method of production. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Italian red wine regions and how radically different wines can all come from the same country. Italy is basically the only red wine country where you could get four wines this structurally different — all still Italian — and it makes sense as a question. The clue is that they're all from the same country but the question asks you to get as specific as possible on origin, which really rewards candidates who know their regions.

The universe you're working in is: Piedmont (Barolo/Barbaresco/Barbera), Veneto (Valpolicella/Amarone/Soave), Tuscany (Brunello/Chianti/Bolgheri), Campania (Taurasi), Sicily (Etna), Sardinia (Cannonau). Pretty much all of Italian red wine.

The key discrimination here is structural: Barolo and Taurasi are both high-tannin, high-acid, long-lived wines but from very different grapes (Nebbiolo vs Aglianico) and different regions; the Valpolicella is much lighter and fresher and is not made for long ageing in the same way; and the Etna Rosso reads almost Burgundian in its paleness and lift. If you can place Nebbiolo from the pale color and stern tannin, Aglianico from the volcanic power and balsamic edge, and Nerello Mascalese from the high altitude transparency, you're doing well. The Valpolicella is the accessible reference point in the flight — Corvina's sour-cherry brightness is hard to miss. The question is specifically asking about method of production, which rewards knowing things like large Slavonian cask ageing in Barolo vs French oak in Taurasi.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "same country" + Paper 2 (reds) = Italy is the primary candidate — it's the only country with this breadth of distinct red appellations
  - "identify origin as closely as possible" = they want region, appellation, ideally sub-zone
  - Method of production asked at only 5 marks each = secondary to identification and quality/maturity

- Universe I considered:
  - Italy: Piedmont, Veneto, Tuscany, Campania, Sicily all represented
  - Spain could work (Rioja, Ribera, Priorat, Toro) but that's a more homogeneous Tempranillo-dominant country
  - France could work (Burgundy, Bordeaux, Rhône, SW) but they usually separate France out rather than use it here

- Rule-outs:
  - Spain unlikely because the 4 red wines would be too similar
  - France unlikely — the examiner would say France if so
  - The 4 wines span 4 very different Italian appellations confirming Italy

- Conclusion: Italy, with Barolo (Nebbiolo/Piedmont), Valpolicella (Corvina/Veneto), Taurasi (Aglianico/Campania), Etna Rosso (Nerello Mascalese/Sicily)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 1
Barolo DOCG, Novello / Langhe, Piedmont
Wine 2
Valpolicella DOC, Valpolicella Classica, Veneto
Wine 3
Taurasi Riserva DOCG, Irpinia / Taurasi, Campania
Wine 4
Etna Rosso DOC, Mount Etna / Solicchiata, Sicily
#### b) Comment on quality, maturity and capacity to age.
Wine 1
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 2
very good to excellent quality. It is youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 3
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 4
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### c) Comment on the method of production.
Wine 1
temperature-controlled stainless steel with automatic pump-over; 30-day post-fermentation maceration with submerged cap  24 months in large 25-30hl Slavonian oak barrels, then 6 months bottle age
Wine 2
destemmed after hand harvest; fermented in temperature-controlled stainless steel for about 9 days  stainless steel until mid-February, then 2 months in bottle
Wine 3
alcoholic fermentation and maceration in stainless steel for about 3-4 weeks  18-24 months in French oak barriques and 50hl casks, then at least 24 months in bottle
Wine 4
hand harvested; 14 days skin maceration, then gentle pressing  3 months in fine-grain oak barrels with natural malolactic fermentation
#### Word count: 260

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–8)***

Wines 5-8 are a mixed bag.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 7 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production with specific reference to the use of oak. (4 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on quality and commercial position. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your ability to navigate a deliberately wide range of quality and commercial positioning within red wine, with oak use as the key differentiating factor. The "mixed bag" stem is the examiner's way of saying "these wines have nothing in common except that they're red and they involve oak in different ways." The fact that oak is specifically called out in part b suggests they've chosen wines that represent very different oak philosophies — from no meaningful oak to extended American and French barrel ageing.

The quality spread here is the whole point. Yellow Tail is intentionally the commercial commodity reference at one end of the quality spectrum, while a Rauzan-Ségla Margaux 2005 is at the very top. The CVNE Imperial Gran Reserva is a traditional Spanish fine wine with integrated extended oak. The Yalumba Grenache is somewhere in the middle — premium for its category, polished, old-vine bush vines with older oak. You need to be able to say plainly that Yellow Tail has limited quality ceiling and is sold at massive volume through supermarkets globally, while Rauzan-Ségla is a classified growth with auction market relevance and investment potential. Origin identification is worth less (7 marks) than quality/commercial position (10 marks), which signals that the examiner is more interested in whether you understand what each wine represents commercially.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "mixed bag" = deliberately heterogeneous flight, no connective tissue between wines
  - "specific reference to the use of oak" at 8 marks = oak is the core technical thread
  - Origin worth only 7 marks = commercial/quality discussion is primary

- Universe I considered:
  - A "mixed bag" of reds could be anything. With oak as the focus you'd expect: a heavily oaked classified growth, a traditional old-world wine with extended maturation, a new-world commercial wine, possibly something with no or minimal oak

- Rule-outs:
  - The question rules nothing out by definition — that's the point of "mixed bag"

- Conclusion: Yellow Tail (commercial/minimal meaningful oak), CVNE Imperial Gran Reserva (traditional Spanish oak maturation), Rauzan-Ségla Margaux 2005 (new French oak, fine wine), Yalumba Grenache (older oak, premium regional)

#### Word count: 269

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 are each made from a single grape variety. Two different grape varieties are represented. Wines 9 and 10 are from one country; wines 11 and 12 are from another country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (4 x 8 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on style, quality, and commercial position. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your ability to identify two grape varieties — Pinot Noir and Malbec — across two countries, France and Argentina. The stem is very helpfully structured: two grapes, two countries, two wines each. Once you figure out that it's Pinot Noir and Malbec, the pairing becomes obvious. Pinot Noir is the most likely candidate for the lighter, cooler-climate French wine (NSG Vieilles Vignes) and the lighter Patagonian wine. Malbec is the grape for both the Cahors and the Mendoza.

The interesting intellectual challenge here is recognizing Malbec in two very different expressions: Cahors Malbec (called Côt or Malbec, dark, tannic, quite rustic or old-school in style, 15% ABV in this case) versus Mendoza Malbec (polished, violet-scented, premium, high-altitude freshness). They taste quite different. Similarly, NSG Pinot Noir reads richer and more structured than the Humberto Canale Patagonian Pinot. The France-Argentina pairing is the examiner pointing to Malbec's historical home (Cahors) vs. its modern home (Argentina), which is a fantastic educational device. If you know that Malbec originated in southwest France and is the signature grape of both Cahors and Mendoza, you can get four wines in one go.

Quality/commercial spread here goes from entry-premium Humberto Canale Patagonian Pinot up to Catena Alta and Chevillon NSG — all serious, but very different price points and audiences.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Two grapes, two countries = a paired structure
  - Single varieties only = no blends
  - Grape identification worth 8 marks each = getting the grape right is critical

- Universe I considered:
  - Two grapes that each appear in two countries: Pinot Noir (France + Argentina/NZ/US/Germany), Malbec (France/Cahors + Argentina), Syrah/Shiraz (France + Australia), Cab Sauv (France + New World)
  - The pairing of France and Argentina points strongly at Pinot/Malbec

- Rule-outs:
  - Cab Sauv from France (Bordeaux) + Argentina is possible but stylistically the distinction would be harder to engineer
  - Syrah from France + Argentina is pretty unlikely (Argentina has little Syrah profile at the premium end)

- Conclusion: Pinot Noir (France/Burgundy + Argentina/Patagonia) and Malbec (France/Cahors + Argentina/Mendoza)

#### Word count: 221

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are traditional method sparkling wines from four different countries. None is from Champagne.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies as closely as possible. (4 x 13 marks)

b) Comment on quality in the context of origin. (4 x 10 marks)

c) State the residual sugar. (4 x 2 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of traditional method sparkling wines outside Champagne. The "none is from Champagne" instruction is a key stem signal — it means the examiner is specifically testing whether you know what other countries make bottle-fermented sparkling wines and what makes each distinct.

The universe of traditional method sparkling wines outside Champagne is fairly well-defined: Cava (Spain), Crémant (various French regions), English sparkling, Sekt (Germany/Austria), Franciacorta (Italy), Cap Classique (South Africa), sparkling Burgundy, Prosecco (tank method so unlikely here), Trento DOC (Italy), and others. With four wines from four different countries, and the stem pointing to traditional method, you're essentially looking at the most prominent non-Champagne sparkling nations.

The residual sugar question at only 2 marks is a small but specific test — you need to know that Brut Nature is 0-3 g/L, Brut is under 12 g/L, Extra Brut is 0-6 g/L, etc. Getting those categories right matters. Each wine here sits in the dry to very dry zone, which is the modern trend, but they differ in grape variety in interesting ways — the Cava is made from Xarel·lo/Macabeo/Parellada, the Crémant from Alsace varieties (likely Pinot Blanc or Auxerrois), the Nyetimber is Chardonnay-based Blanc de Blancs, and the Sekt is Riesling, which is the most distinctive aromatically and probably easiest to identify. Riesling Sekt should stick out clearly because of the variety's aromatics even under mousse.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Traditional method confirmed = autolytic, bready, toasty potential
  - 4 countries = need broad geographical knowledge of the category
  - "None is from Champagne" = examiner deliberately avoiding the default anchor

- Universe I considered:
  - Spain (Cava), France (Crémant — Alsace, Loire, Bourgogne, Bordeaux), England, Germany (Sekt), Italy (Franciacorta, Trento), South Africa, Australia, New Zealand, US

- Rule-outs:
  - South Africa, Australia, NZ, US — less likely because the examiner tends toward European examples for non-Champagne trad method
  - Prosecco excluded by "traditional method"

- Conclusion: Cava (Spain), Crémant d'Alsace (France), English sparkling (England), German Riesling Sekt — four European countries, one distinctively aromatic variety

#### a) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies as closely as possible.
Riesling / Riesling / Riesling / Xarel·lo, Macabeo and Parellada
The combination of blind, this should read as cool-climate bottle-fermented riesling sparkling wine: green apple, citrus, white flowers and wet stone, with a brisk spine of acidity and a lightly softening touch of dosage rather than full brut nature severity (source: https://loosenbrosusa.com/wp-content/uploads/2023/01/dr-loosen-riesling is consistent with Riesling / Riesling / Riesling / Xarel·lo, Macabeo and Parellada.

good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term

Wine 1

This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
Wine 3
Blind, expect botrytis-inflected apricot, citrus peel, honey and light petroleum, with markedly low alcohol and enough Mosel acidity to keep the wine from feeling heavy (source: https://www.vinunic.se/sv/beerenauslese-5364-187ml.aspx/?pf=pdf).. 7.5% alcohol, very sweet, dessert.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
Wine 4
Blind, this should show traditional-method Cava markers of apple, white flowers and light toast, but with a comparatively generous, rounded palate and evident but moderate dosage rather than the stricter, bone-dry feel of many Brut Nature bottlings (source: https://shop.juveycamps.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/Juve%CC%81-Camps-ESSENTIAL-PU%CC%81RPURA-Ficha-Te%CC%81cnica.pdf).. 12.0% alcohol, medium balanced.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### Word count: 401

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–7)***

Wines 5-7 are all made using different methods of production.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss quality and commercial potential. (3 x 8 marks)

c) Identify the key production techniques that have influenced the style. (3 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of rosé wine across three very different production methods and quality levels. "All made using different methods of production" is the key signal — the examiner is specifically asking you to distinguish how each of these rosés is made, not just what they are.

The three major rosé production methods are: saignée/maceration (used for serious Champagne rosé), direct pressing or short skin contact (used for Provence still rosé), and the semi-sweet tank method (used for mass-market rosés like Mateus). You could also throw in the addition of red wine to white base (which is actually how Laurent-Perrier LP rosé is made — it's a blended Champagne rosé). So in this flight you've got: a premium NV Champagne rosé made by blending red wine into white base (LP's signature method), a Provence Cru Classé-level still rosé made by direct press, and Mateus which is semi-sparkling, semi-sweet and made at massive commercial scale.

Quality and commercial potential really sorts these wines out. LP Brut Rosé is a global premium brand, sold in fine dining worldwide with strong commercial momentum behind prestige rosé. Château d'Esclans Les Clans is at the luxury end of Provence — serious still rosé with fine dining positioning and ageing potential. Mateus is the opposite of both: enormous volume, immediate and simple, with almost purely mass-market appeal. The question rewards candidates who can describe those commercial profiles precisely rather than just saying "good" or "bad."

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Different methods of production" = the entire point is production method discrimination
  - Paper 3 setting = rosé fits here, as does sparkling and fortified
  - Three wines only = focused and manageable flight

- Universe I considered:
  - Rosé methods: saignée, direct press, blending, tank/partial ferment (Mateus style), Champagne rosé blend method
  - Quality ladder: from Mateus (commodity) up to premium Provence and Champagne

- Rule-outs:
  - Nothing specifically ruled out, but the combination of a sparkling rosé, a serious still rosé and a semi-sweet semi-sparkling rosé naturally points to Champagne, Provence and Portugal

- Conclusion: LP Champagne rosé (blended method), Château d'Esclans Provence still rosé (direct press), Mateus semi-sweet semi-sparkling (tank/mass-market)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 8–9)***

Wines 8 and 9 are from the same region.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies. (14 marks)

b) Compare the production methods of the two wines, identifying the key factors that have influenced the style. (16 marks)

Then for each wine:

c) Comment on quality and maturity. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Tokaj and the ability to distinguish a dry Furmint still wine from a classic Tokaji Aszú sweet wine, both from the same region. The "same region" stem combined with Paper 3 (the special paper) strongly suggests a wine region that makes both a dry and a sweet or fortified style from the same grapes. The obvious candidates for this setup are: Tokaj (dry Furmint vs Aszú), Jerez (fino vs oloroso), Sauternes/Graves (dry white vs botrytised sweet), Alsace (dry vs Sélection de Grains Nobles), and to a lesser extent Germany (dry vs Prädikat sweet).

Tokaj is actually the best fit here because: the dry style (Furmint) has risen in prominence recently and is a legitimate fine wine in its own right, and the Aszú is one of the most famous sweet wines in the world. Having both in the glass from the same region is a great educational pairing. The comparison of production methods is where the marks really are — 16 marks is the biggest single chunk in this question. You need to explain how dry Furmint is made (normal dry white winemaking, maybe some skin contact or lees ageing) versus how Aszú is made (hand-selecting botrytised berries, the paste or berry-addition method, long ageing in Gönci barrels under partial oxidation). These are very different processes despite sharing the same grape and region.

Commercial potential is also interesting — dry Furmint is a growth category, increasingly on fine wine lists globally, while Aszú remains relatively niche outside specialist and sweet wine circles.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region" + Paper 3 = a region that makes multiple styles
  - Production method comparison for 16 marks = the whole academic weight is here
  - Combined origin ID only 14 marks = they don't expect you to name specific villages necessarily

- Universe I considered:
  - Tokaj (dry Furmint + Aszú), Jerez (flor styles + oxidative), Alsace (dry + SGN), Germany (dry Riesling + TBA/Eiswein), Sauternes/Graves adjacent

- Rule-outs:
  - Jerez less likely because that's a fortified question, not a "same grape variety" question
  - Germany less likely as the production contrast (dry vs Prädikat) is less pedagogically neat than Furmint vs Aszú

- Conclusion: Tokaj, Hungary — dry Furmint (W8) vs Tokaji Aszú 5 Puttonyos (W9)

### **Question 4   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 are from three different countries. All are made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (18 marks)

Then for each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 7 marks)

c) Discuss the production methods. (3 x 6 marks)

d) Comment on quality and maturity. (3 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Grenache/Garnacha across three very different expressions and styles, from a 25-year-old fortified Maury to a powerful old-vine dry Spanish Garnacha to a modern California Grenache. The grape variety identification at 18 marks is the single biggest question in this set, which tells you the examiner views getting the grape right as the whole foundation.

The key challenge in identifying a single grape across three such different wine styles is that the wines don't look the same. A 1998 fortified Maury is going to be deeply amber-tawny from oxidative ageing, high alcohol, sweet and nutty. A 15% old-vine Montsant Garnacha is going to be full-bodied, powerful, dark red-fruited and firmly tannic. A Napa Grenache at 13% is going to be light, perfumed, lifted red-berry, almost Burgundian. Getting the thread that connects them is the skill — the red-berry fruit character, the potential for high alcohol, and the spicy, garrigue signature when in warm climates.

This question is really testing whether you can recognize Grenache in unusual formats. Most candidates would nail the Napa Grenache or the Spanish Garnacha, but the fortified Maury may throw people. Maury is one of a handful of French VDN (Vin Doux Naturel) appellations made from Grenache Noir — the others being Banyuls. If you know that Grenache is the basis for Maury, you can put it together. Production method discussion is important here: fortified/mutage in the Maury, traditional dry red winemaking for the Montsant and Napa.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same single grape variety across three countries = an international variety
  - Paper 3 context = one wine is almost certainly a fortified or unusual style
  - 18 marks for grape variety = massive reward for correct grape ID

- Universe I considered:
  - Grenache/Garnacha (France, Spain, US, Australia), Syrah (France, Australia, Argentina), Pinot Noir (France, NZ, US), Malbec (France, Argentina), Tempranillo (Spain, Argentina)
  - The fortified/special paper context points toward a grape used in VDN or similar fortified wines — Grenache is the primary candidate via Maury/Banyuls

- Rule-outs:
  - Syrah: no strong fortified tradition in three different countries simultaneously
  - Pinot Noir: doesn't produce fortified wines commercially
  - Tempranillo: very limited footprint outside Iberian Peninsula

- Conclusion: Grenache/Garnacha — Maury VDN 1998 (France/Roussillon), old-vine Montsant dry (Spain), Napa Grenache dry (USA)

# **Master of Wine Exam 2022**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1 and 2 are from the same producer and the same vintage.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

With reference to both wines:

b) Compare the two wines discussing the quality, maturity, use of oak and market position. (30 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of white Burgundy hierarchy. The stem tells you two wines, same producer, same vintage — that's a massive clue that they want you to differentiate within one estate, almost certainly by appellation tier. In white Burgundy the obvious comparison is village vs premier cru, or village vs grand cru, and in this case it's Meursault village against Corton-Charlemagne Grand Cru from Xavier Monnot.

On the origin identification side you need to get both to at least village level. Meursault should be identifiable from texture and hazelnut richness; Corton-Charlemagne should show more scale, flint and vertical drive. The 0.5% difference in ABV is a small clue toward the grand cru being riper.

The big marks (30) are in part b — comparing quality, maturity, oak and market. That's where you really need to know the hierarchy: a village Meursault from a good producer sits at maybe £30-40 retail, while Corton-Charlemagne from a serious domaine is £80-120+. Oak use is likely similar in approach at Monnot but the grand cru should integrate it better. Maturity: 2019 is still relatively young for Corton-Charlemagne, drinking well for Meursault. This is basically a lesson in appellation hierarchy within a single producer.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same producer, same vintage — this narrows dramatically to a hierarchy comparison
  - Paper 1 = white still wines
  - Both wines from Burgundy (Xavier Monnot)
- Universe I considered:
  - Burgundy village vs premier cru vs grand cru within one estate
  - Could theoretically be a northern Rhône producer (Chave etc.) with different cuvees, but Burgundy most likely
- Rule-outs:
  - Different countries ruled out by "same producer"
  - New World same-producer comparison less likely at MW level
- Conclusion: Burgundy Chardonnay hierarchy comparison, village Meursault vs Grand Cru Corton-Charlemagne

#### Word count: 83

### **Question 2   *(Wines 3–5)***

Wines 3-5 are from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and variety as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the quality and maturity. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the winemaking techniques used. (3 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of premium Australian white wines. The "same country" stem combined with three different varieties asks you to know which countries make multiple, meaningfully different white varietals at a serious level. That rules out Spain pretty quickly (just Albariño and maybe Verdejo), makes Italy harder unless all three are Italian, and steers you toward Australia, France, or maybe New Zealand. The dead giveaway when tasting should be that two of the three are very distinctly non-French in character.

The plausible countries with 3 different serious whites are France (Chardonnay/Riesling/Chenin, Chardonnay/Viognier/Marsanne, etc.), Australia (Riesling/Chardonnay/Marsanne or Semillon), and maybe the US. What makes this distinctly Australian is the Tahbilk Marsanne museum release, which is a really distinctive wine — very waxy, beeswax, toasty, no oak, aged. That's one of the more recognizable curveballs in Australian wine and should get you to Australia once you've identified it.

The winemaking sub-question (c) is interesting: you've got unoaked Riesling in stainless, barrel-fermented and malo'd Chardonnay from Margaret River, and a museum-released neutral white with bottle development. That's a clever range. Quality and maturity will see the Marsanne as the most developed/aged, the Grosset Riesling as most primary, and the Pierro somewhere in between.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Three wines, same country, different varieties and origins
  - Winemaking sub-question worth marks — suggests meaningfully different production methods
  - Quality and maturity both asked — implies wines at different stages
- Universe I considered:
  - France (Riesling/Chardonnay/Chenin or Viognier/Marsanne)
  - Australia (Riesling/Chardonnay/Marsanne or Semillon)
  - New Zealand (Riesling/Chardonnay/Sauv Blanc — less likely for three distinct styles)
- Rule-outs:
  - Spain unlikely — not enough serious distinct whites
  - Italy possible but tricky to find three very distinct styles
- Conclusion: Australia — Grosset Clare Riesling, Pierro Margaret River Chardonnay, Tahbilk Nagambie Marsanne museum release

### **Question 3   *(Wines 6–8)***

Wines 6-8 come from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and variety/ies as closely as possible. (3 x 15 marks)

b) Discuss the wine’s style and quality; consider which markets this wine would be successful in. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Italian white wines and the country's remarkable diversity of styles. Three wines, same country — Italy is actually one of the harder countries to pin as the answer when you don't know what's in the glass, because its whites range so dramatically from light commercial Pinot Grigio in the northeast to intensely aromatic alpine Gewurztraminer to volcanic, mineral Etna Bianco in the south.

The most recognizable wine here should be W7 — Alto Adige Gewurztraminer is as unmistakable as it gets, with rose petals, lychee and spice making it one of the easiest varietal IDs in the exam. Once you land on that, the "same country" clue is confirmed as Italy and that helps anchor the others. W6 is commercial Pinot Grigio from the Veneto, light and clean, probably the easiest stylistically but hardest to get to the sub-region. W8 is the curveball — Etna Bianco (Carricante-dominant) is tensile, mineral and saline with no oak, reading more like an aged Mosel in structure than a Mediterranean white.

Part b) asking about market success is interesting because these three wines sit in very different commercial positions: Pinot Grigio has enormous global volume; Alto Adige Gewurztraminer has strong on-trade appeal especially in northern Europe and North America; Etna Bianco is a premium terroir-driven wine with a much smaller but rapidly growing fine-wine following.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Three wines, same country
  - Market success question — suggests different commercial trajectories
  - High marks on origin (3x15) — expects sub-regional specificity
- Universe I considered:
  - Italy: Pinot Grigio + Gewurztraminer both point northeast; Etna Bianco points south
  - France: possible with Alsace + Burgundy + Rhône but three wines from one country usually share a regional logic
  - Germany: unlikely to have three so different in style
- Rule-outs:
  - Alto Adige Gewurztraminer is an unmistakable pointer to Italy once identified
  - Etna Bianco is distinctly volcanic in mineral character
- Conclusion: Italy — commercial Veneto Pinot Grigio, aromatic Alto Adige Gewurztraminer, terroir-driven Etna Bianco
