### **Question 4   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 are from different countries and are made from different, single grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible. (4 x 15 marks)

b) Comment on quality and market position. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of single-variety white wines that are strongly associated with a specific country or region. The stem confirms four different countries, four different varieties — so no guesswork on whether they might be blends. The universe of "distinctive single-variety whites with clear geographic signatures" is actually a manageable list: Riesling (Germany/Alsace/Austria/Australia), Grüner Veltliner (Austria), Albariño (Spain), Torrontés (Argentina), Viognier (northern Rhône), Roussanne/Marsanne (northern Rhône), Chenin (Loire/South Africa), Semillon (Hunter Valley), Gewurztraminer (Alsace/Alto Adige), Assyrtiko (Greece).

The easiest wine to ID here is probably the Torrontés — intensely floral, jasmine, orange blossom, grapey lift, and nothing else in the world smells quite like it. The Grüner Veltliner should also be legible: white pepper, citrus, crisp and linear with low alcohol. Albariño should give Atlantic saline freshness and peach, though distinguishing Rias Baixas from Torrontés takes palate experience. The curveball is the Roussanne from Saint-Péray — rich, textural, apricot blossom, a touch of almond and old oak. Getting "Roussanne" rather than misidentifying it as Viognier or Chardonnay requires knowing the northern Rhône's still white styles specifically.

The market position part is interesting because you have one major-volume international category wine (Albariño is now globally distributed), one extremely niche wine (Roussanne from Saint-Péray has very limited production and low consumer recognition), one quirky but growing category (Torrontés), and one Austria-dominant but internationally available variety (Grüner Veltliner).

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Four different countries, four different single varieties — wide global spread
  - High marks on identification (4x15) — expects both variety AND regional specificity
  - Market position question — implies wines at varied commercial tiers
- Universe I considered:
  - Argentina: Torrontés (almost the only plausible single-variety aromatic white from Argentina)
  - Spain: Albariño or Verdejo most likely
  - Austria: Grüner Veltliner or Riesling
  - France: Roussanne, Viognier, Chenin, Savagnin, Picpoul — any number of single-variety whites
- Rule-outs:
  - Torrontés is essentially Argentina-specific for exam purposes
  - Grüner Veltliner is almost always Austria in this context
  - Albariño and Rias Baixas are strongly paired
  - Roussanne from Saint-Péray is the hardest — could be confused with Viognier or oaked Chardonnay
- Conclusion: Argentina (Torrontés/Salta), Spain (Albariño/Rias Baixas), Austria (Grüner/Kamptal), France (Roussanne/Saint-Péray)

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-3 are from different countries and are each made from a different, single grape variety. Wine 4 is a blend of all three of these varieties.

For each wine 1-3:

a) Identify the grape variety and the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 15 marks)

b) Comment on the style, considering possible reasons for not blending the variety used for this wine. (3 x 10 marks)

For wine 4:

c) Comment on the purpose of blending these varieties with reference to balance and quality. (15 marks)

d) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the Bordeaux blend and its three primary red varieties. The question is almost perfectly designed to teach the concept: three single-variety wines from three different countries, then the blended expression. Once you identify wines 1-3 as Cabernet Franc, Cabernet Sauvignon and Merlot, the penny should drop that wine 4 is a Bordeaux blend.

The hardest of the three to identify is probably wine 1 (Cabernet Franc from Chinon) — Cab Franc can be confused with Cab Sauv, especially in a warm year like 2019 in the Loire. The tell is the pyrazines, the earthiness, and the relative freshness and lower body versus a Napa Cabernet. Wine 2 is the richest and most opulent at 14.5%, with the polish and density you'd expect from Oakville Cabernet — a candidate who's tasted Napa should land on Cabernet Sauvignon there. Wine 3 should be softer and plummier than both, with easier tannins — that's the Merlot register.

Part b is where the real marks are (45). You need to describe wine 4 (a Pessac-Léognan in a serious vintage) and then argue why these three varieties work together: Cabernet Sauvignon gives structure, tannin and cassis depth; Merlot adds softness, flesh and earlier approachability; Cabernet Franc contributes lift, aromatics and mid-palate freshness. That's the whole Bordeaux blending logic in one answer.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Wines 1-3 are single-variety, different countries; wine 4 is a blend of all three
  - Paper 2 = reds — classic Bordeaux blending question
  - Wine 4 being a blend of all three almost certainly points to Bordeaux
- Universe I considered:
  - Three varieties that blend into Bordeaux: Cabernet Sauvignon, Merlot, Cabernet Franc
  - Could potentially be Cabernet-dominated blends from elsewhere (Napa, Chile) but "blend of all three" strongly implies Bordeaux
- Rule-outs:
  - A Malbec or Petit Verdot set would not blend so cleanly into one well-known blend
  - Rioja blend (Tempranillo + Garnacha + Graciano) is possible but rarely described this way
- Conclusion: Cab Franc (Loire), Cab Sauv (Napa), Merlot (Chile) + Bordeaux blend (Pessac-Léognan)

#### Word count: 419

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–6)***

Wines 5 and 6 are from the same region but different sub-regions.

For each wine:

a) Identify the sub-region as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques used. (2 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the style and quality. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Tuscany's two most important red wine sub-regions and the hierarchy between them. "Same region, different sub-regions" almost always means the examiner wants you to work within a famous wine region and distinguish the sub-appellations — in this case Chianti Classico vs Brunello di Montalcino, both built on Sangiovese.

The key identifier for wine 5 is the Sangiovese signature at a slightly lower intensity: bright red cherry, dried herbs, iris, medium tannin and Chianti's characteristic lifted acidity. Note that Brolio is 80% Sangiovese plus some Merlot and Cab Sauv — a typical modern Chianti Classico blend. Wine 6 should be more serious in every dimension: 100% Sangiovese, with a deeper, more concentrated expression, broader tannic architecture and more obvious longevity. The ABV difference (13.5% vs 14.5%) and the vintage (2017 vs 2015) both push wine 6 further into power and extraction.

The winemaking sub-question (b) is worth knowing: Chianti Classico at this level gets tonneaux or Slavonian cask at a shorter aging period; Brunello di Montalcino by DOCG law requires minimum 2 years in wood and lengthy total aging. Getting those regulatory details correct is easy marks. Style and quality (c) should center on the clear step-up in intensity, complexity and aging potential that distinguishes a Montalcino single-vineyard Brunello from a sound if polished Chianti Classico.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same region, different sub-regions — classic hierarchy comparison
  - Paper 2 = reds; Italy's most likely candidate for this structure is Tuscany
  - Winemaking sub-question suggests different regulated aging regimes
- Universe I considered:
  - Tuscany: Chianti Classico vs Brunello, or Chianti Classico vs Vino Nobile de Montepulciano
  - Burgundy: Hautes Côtes vs grand cru (but that's covered by Q3)
  - Spain: Rioja Crianza vs Rioja Gran Reserva is possible
- Rule-outs:
  - The 2015 vintage in wine 6 is the famous Brunello vintage — a strong hint
  - ABV at 14.5% is in Brunello territory
  - Two-wine comparison with sub-region emphasis strongly suggests Italy/Tuscany or Burgundy
- Conclusion: Tuscany — Chianti Classico (Ricasoli Brolio 2017) vs Brunello di Montalcino (Altesino Montosoli 2015)

Wine 6

This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.

#### Word count: 182

### **Question 3   *(Wines 7–8)***

Wines 7 and 8 are from the same region but different sub-regions.

For each wine:

a) Identify the sub-region as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques used. (2 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the style and quality. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Burgundy Pinot Noir hierarchy. Like Q2 before it, the "same region, different sub-regions" format sets you up to compare appellations — here it's red Burgundy, and the spread is significant: a Hautes Côtes de Beaune at regional appellation level versus Clos Vougeot Grand Cru.

The two wines actually share the same structure format as Q3 in Paper 2, so you're doing the same sub-region ID, winemaking and quality assessment twice in the same paper — once for Tuscany, once for Burgundy. The skill being tested is the same: can you tell quality level from the glass and explain why?

Wine 7 (Hautes Côtes) should be lighter, more fragrant and simpler — raspberry, cherry, light earth — with lower ABV (12.5%) and less oak influence. Wine 8 (Clos Vougeot) should be darker, richer, more complex with more grip, more structure and obvious quality depth at 14.0%. The 1.5% ABV difference reflects both the site advantage of a Côte de Nuits grand cru and the different vintages (2017 vs 2019, with 2017 being warmer than the fresh 2019 from Hautes Côtes).

Winemaking wise, the Hautes Côtes wine is likely shorter elevage in used oak or tank, where Clos Vougeot at a serious domaine like Gros Frère et Soeur will be new-oak raised with longer barrel time. The quality gap should be obvious in the glass.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Exact same structure as Q2 in the same paper — same region, different sub-regions
  - Two Pinot Noirs from Burgundy, different hierarchical levels
  - Winemaking and quality sub-questions worth different marks
- Universe I considered:
  - Burgundy (Côte de Nuits vs Hautes Côtes, or regional vs grand cru)
  - This is the second same-region/different sub-region pair in the paper — examiner doing Tuscany + Burgundy in sequence
- Rule-outs:
  - ABV difference (12.5% vs 14.0%) strongly signals different quality tiers and sites
  - Vintage gap (2017 vs 2019) adds maturity dimension
- Conclusion: Burgundy Pinot Noir — Hautes Côtes de Beaune (Esmonin 2019) vs Clos Vougeot Grand Cru (Gros Frère et Soeur 2017)

### **Question 4   *(Wines 9–10)***

Wines 9 and 10 are made from the same single grape variety and come from the same region.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety and the region as closely as possible. (20 marks)

b) Compare and contrast style and quality. (20 marks)

c) Compare and contrast market potential. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Argentine Malbec and the ability to distinguish tiers of quality within the same region and variety. "Same grape variety, same region" at this mark weighting (20 marks just for the ID) means the examiner expects you to be confident and specific — country, region, variety, and probably sub-region.

Malbec from Argentina should be fairly legible: deep purple, black fruit, violet, plush texture, soft tannins, generally ripe and approachable. The key question is which country — Malbec also grows in Cahors (France) and in other parts of the New World, but nowhere in the world does it reach the same critical mass as Mendoza Argentina. Within Mendoza, Uco Valley is the premium sub-region at altitude.

The interesting part is wines 9 and 10 are clearly at different quality levels: Zuccardi Serie A is the more accessible, polished-commercial style at 13.9%, while Altocedro Old Vine Reserve at 14.5% and an older vintage is denser, more structured and more age-worthy. The "reserve" and "old vine" nomenclature should signal that wine 10 is the step-up.

Market potential (c) is where you can show examiner-pleasing knowledge about Argentina's Malbec boom — the shift from cheap supermarket Malbec to a tiered, terroir-led category with Uco Valley at the top. Wine 9 plays in the £15-25 sector with broad retail distribution; wine 10 is fine-wine channel, restaurants, collectors.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same variety, same region — narrows heavily
  - Market potential and current trends explicitly asked — suggests a category with a commercial story
  - ABV difference (13.9% vs 14.5%) and vintage gap (2020 vs 2018) suggest different quality tiers
- Universe I considered:
  - Argentine Malbec (Mendoza/Uco Valley) — by far the most likely
  - Cahors Malbec (France) — possible but much less commercially prominent at MW level
  - Chilean Malbec — exists but minor
- Rule-outs:
  - "Current trends" and "market potential" practically telegraphs Argentina's rising Malbec story
  - Old Vine Reserve framing is very New World in style
- Conclusion: Argentine Malbec, Uco Valley, Mendoza — Zuccardi Serie A (approachable tier) vs Altocedro Old Vine Reserve (premium tier)

#### Word count: 204

### **Question 5   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11 and 12 are made from the same single grape variety or predominant grape variety and come from the same region.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the primary grape variety and the region as closely as possible. (20 marks)

b) Compare and contrast style and quality. (20 marks)

c) Compare and contrast market potential. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of California Zinfandel and specifically Dry Creek Valley as the premium sub-region. The "single grape variety or predominant grape variety" phrasing in the stem is a big tell — when they add "or predominant" that almost always means one of the wines is a field blend or a legally defined blend where another variety leads but the dominant grape is still what you'd call it. Ridge Lytton Springs is exactly this: a Zinfandel-dominant field blend with Petite Sirah, Carignane and others. So the examiner is essentially flagging that you won't be confused when you notice wine 12 is not 100% Zinfandel.

Both wines being from Dry Creek Valley (same region) and both being Zinfandel-led makes the ID relatively concentrated: you need to know Dry Creek is the heartland of premium Zinfandel in Sonoma, and then distinguish two quality levels within it. Wine 11 (Pedroncelli) at 15.5% is the more extroverted, jammy, high-alcohol style — classic Mother Clone Dry Creek. Wine 12 (Ridge Lytton Springs) at 14.6% should be more structured and complex, with more savory earth and spice notes from the field blend, longer finish, and a quality level that sits clearly above the straight varietal bottling.

Market potential: Zinfandel is almost exclusively American in appeal; the variety never fully broke through in international markets the way Cabernet or Pinot Noir did. Ridge is one of the exceptions with genuine fine-wine credibility globally, while Pedroncelli is a solid domestic value brand. The contrast there is actually an interesting market story.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Or predominant grape variety" — signals one wine is a blend dominated by a single variety (Ridge field blend)
  - High alcohol (15.5%) on wine 11 — very high even for California reds, points to Zinfandel
  - Same region, so Dry Creek or Paso Robles or Russian River for Zinfandel
- Universe I considered:
  - California Zinfandel (Dry Creek, Paso Robles, Russian River Valley)
  - Primitivo from Puglia is the same grape — possible curveball, but the "current trends" market discussion tilts toward California
  - Petite Sirah + Zinfandel blends (less commonly set as questions)
- Rule-outs:
  - 15.5% ABV narrows heavily to California or Southern Italy — California more likely for an exam with market discussion
  - Ridge Lytton Springs is an iconic enough wine to appear in a Paper 2 question
- Conclusion: California Zinfandel / Dry Creek Valley — Pedroncelli Mother Clone (varietal, high-alcohol, value tier) vs Ridge Lytton Springs (field blend, more structured, fine-wine tier)

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are all sparkling rosé wines. Wines 1 and 2 are from one country, and wines 3 and 4 are from another country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible and the grape variety(ies) used. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the quality within the context of the origin. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of sparkling rosé wines and specifically the distinction between tank-method (Charmat) and traditional-method sparkling. All four are rosé, which narrows the grape discussion significantly. The two-country split (wines 1-2 from one country, 3-4 from another) with four sparkling rosés most obviously points to Italy vs France.

The most critical winemaking distinction is Charmat method (wines 1 and 2, both Italian) vs traditional method (wines 3 and 4, both French). That should be legible in the glass: wines 1 and 2 should be fresh, fruity, simple in autolytic character; wines 3 and 4 should show more complexity, biscuit/brioche from lees contact.

Wine 1 is Prosecco Rosé DOC (new designation from 2020) — Glera-led with Pinot Nero for color. Wine 2 is a Lambrusco Spumante Rosato — the curveball here. Most candidates will know Prosecco but Lambrusco as a quality dry sparkling rosé from Emilia-Romagna is less expected. The key tell is that wine 2 should read darker pink with red-berry freshness and high acidity, where wine 1 is paler salmon and more delicate.

On the French side, wine 3 (Bollinger Rosé) should have obvious Pinot Noir richness, vinous depth and the autolytic complexity you expect from a prestige Champagne house. Wine 4 (Crémant de Bourgogne) should be lighter, fresher and less complex than the Champagne, but still clearly traditional-method — a very good value-quality ratio wine that the examiner wants you to assess commercially.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - All four are sparkling rosé — Paper 3 special styles
  - Two countries, wines 1-2 from one, 3-4 from another
  - Winemaking sub-question worth marks — Charmat vs traditional method is the dividing line
- Universe I considered:
  - Italy (Prosecco Rosé + Lambrusco Rosato both Charmat) vs France (Champagne + Crémant both traditional)
  - Could be Spain (Cava rosé) + France, or Italy + UK, but Italy + France is the most natural sparkling rosé pairing
- Rule-outs:
  - Bollinger is one of the recognizable Champagne houses — should be identifiable as serious traditional-method Champagne
  - Prosecco Rosé DOC only approved 2020, making this a timely question
  - Lambrusco Rosato is the genuine curveball — candidates who only know sweet Lambrusco will struggle
- Conclusion: Italy (Prosecco Rosé Charmat, Lambrusco Rosato Charmat) vs France (Bollinger Champagne traditional, Crémant de Bourgogne traditional)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible and the grape variety(ies) used.
Minimum 85% Glera + minimum 10-15% Pinot Nero / Lambrusco grape varieties / Pinot Noir , with Chardonnay and Pinot Meunier; red wine addition for rosé colour / Minimum 30% Pinot Noir, Chardonnay, Pinot Blanc or Pinot Gris required; remainder typically Aligoté
The combination of very pale salmon-pink to light copper with fine, persistent bubbles (charmat-produced); fresh and fruity nose of strawberry, white peach, apple, rose petal and light citrus — the characteristic fresh, primary prosecco aromatic register with a rosé twist.; light-bodied (11.5% abv), bright, refreshing palate with high ac is consistent with Minimum 85% Glera + minimum 10-15% Pinot Nero / Lambrusco grape varieties / Pinot Noir , with Chardonnay and Pinot Meunier; red wine addition for rosé colour / Minimum 30% Pinot Noir, Chardonnay, Pinot Blanc or Pinot Gris required; remainder typically Aligoté.
#### b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques.
Wine 1
Charmat-Martinotti method (secondary fermentation in pressurised tanks); no bottle fermentation  Short tank maturation; early disgorgement and bottling for freshness Dosage: Brut (approx 6-12 g/L RS) — Source needed for exact La Gioiosa dosage

Wine 4
Traditional method (méthode traditionnelle) — mandatory for all Crémant de Bourgogne  Minimum 1 year on lees before disgorgement Dosage: Brut — Source needed for exact g/L

#### Word count: 399

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–9)***

Wines 5-9 are all from different countries of origin and are all made from different predominant grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety/ies and origin as closely as possible. (5 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques, with specific reference to the level of residual sugar. (5 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the quality and state of maturity. (5 x 8 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of sweet and off-dry white wines across five different countries, and specifically how each wine achieves its residual sugar. That's actually the organizing principle of the sub-question on winemaking — the examiner wants you to articulate the different mechanisms: arrested fermentation for the Spätlese (yeast die off or fermentation stopped by chilling), botrytis concentration for the Sauternes and the South African Chenin, the Aszú dough maceration technique unique to Tokaj, and probably just a tiny RS from the Gewurztraminer's partial sugar balance.

The five varieties and five countries are: NZ (Gewurztraminer), Germany (Riesling Spätlese), South Africa (Chenin Blanc), Hungary (Tokaj/Furmint), France (Sauternes/Sémillon blend). These are genuinely five distinct styles ranging from slightly off-dry (the Gewurztraminer and arguably the Spätlese) to extremely sweet (the Tokaji and Sauternes).

The easiest to identify should be the Riesling Spätlese at 7% ABV — nothing in the world reads quite like that: low alcohol, high acidity, slate minerality, petrol notes beginning to develop in a 2015. The Gewurztraminer from NZ is unmistakably aromatic (lychee, rose, ginger) at 14%. The Tokaji has that distinctive dried apricot/quince concentration with very high acidity that distinguishes it from Sauternes. The Sauternes is the richest and most glycerol-laden. The South African Chenin NLH is the hardest to place — botrytis, honey, high RS, but the Chenin grape character and the new oak influence are the cues.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Five different countries, five different varieties — global sweet wine survey
  - "With reference to RS level" in the winemaking question — explicitly about sweetness mechanisms
  - Paper 3 = special styles; this is essentially the sweet wine section
- Universe I considered:
  - Germany (Riesling Spätlese/Auslese) is virtually always in a sweet flight like this
  - France (Sauternes/Barsac or Vouvray moelleux) very likely
  - Hungary (Tokaji) is a must-know for MW — almost certain to appear
  - South Africa (Chenin NLH) is less common but not a shock
  - NZ Gewurztraminer off-dry is the most marginally "sweet" of the group
- Rule-outs:
  - Canadian Icewine would also be plausible but five different countries already covers most sweet wine archetypes
  - Austrian TBA or auslese is possible but Germany/Rheingau is the more classic exam choice
- Conclusion: NZ Gewurztraminer (slightly off-dry), German Riesling Spätlese (off-dry, very low ABV), South African Noble Rot Chenin (sweet), Tokaji Aszú (very sweet), Barsac Premier Cru (very sweet botrytis)

#### Word count: 433

### **Question 3   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 are from three different countries.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques. (3 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial position. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of sweet red and fortified wines, and specifically your ability to distinguish appassimento sweet red from aged tawny fortified, and Portuguese tawny from Australian tawny. Three very different styles from three different countries: Italy, Portugal, Australia.

Wine 10 is the curveball — Recioto della Valpolicella is the sweet counterpart to Amarone, made from the same dried Corvina-based grapes but with fermentation arrested before dryness. It should look like a deep ruby-garnet sweet red, rich with dried cherry, dark chocolate and violet, and sweet on the palate without the bitterness that Amarone gets as it dries out. Many candidates will think "Amarone" and then be confused by the sweetness — but the key is that it's noticeably sweet, not dry. The winemaking question should get at appassimento: grapes dried 3-4 months on bamboo racks, then fermentation deliberately arrested.

Wines 11 and 12 are the tawny comparison. Both are 10-year average aged tawnies: Quinta do Noval (Portugal, 19.5%) vs Seppeltsfield Para Grand Tawny (Australia, 20.9%). Both should read amber-tawny with an orange-brick rim, oxidative nutty rancio character, dried fruit and walnut. The key differences are: the Portuguese is more precise and fresher, with the Douro's underlying tension; the Australian is richer, heavier, with more dried-fruit concentration from Barossa's heat. Getting both of those right — country AND style nuance — is the challenge.

The commercial question (c) is interesting for all three: Recioto is genuinely niche even within Italy and has limited export presence; Quinta do Noval Tawny is premium Port with global distribution; Seppeltsfield Grand Tawny is one of Australia's heritage fortified icons with a small but devoted following.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Three different countries — Paper 3 closing flight of fortified/special styles
  - Very high ABVs (15%, 19.5%, 20.9%) — ranges from sweet red to fortified tawny
  - All NV or older vintages — oxidative/aged styles
- Universe I considered:
  - Italy (Recioto, Vinsanto, Marsala) — sweet or fortified Italian
  - Portugal (Tawny Port, Colheita, Madeira) — always a contender in P3 fortified
  - Australia (Seppeltsfield Grand Tawny, Rutherglen Muscat) — increasingly included in MW fortified questions
- Rule-outs:
  - Recioto is a genuine curveball — most will know Amarone but not its sweet equivalent
  - Distinguishing 10yr Tawny Port from Australian Grand Tawny is the key discrimination test
  - ABV of 20.9% is fractionally higher than typical Port (19-20%) — slight signal toward Australian fortified
- Conclusion: Italy (Recioto della Valpolicella, appassimento sweet red), Portugal (Quinta do Noval 10yr Tawny), Australia (Seppeltsfield Para Grand Tawny)

Wine 11

Wine 12
Barossa Valley GI, Barossa Valley, South Australia
#### b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques.
Wine 10
Appassimento — same grape drying process as Amarone (3-4 months); but fermentation is arrested before dryness, leaving residual sugar; Recioto is Amarone's sweet "parent"  Old oak casks typical; DOCG requires minimum aging (Source: common knowledge — specific regulation Source needed)
Wine 11
Standard Port production — grape spirit fortification mid-fermentation leaving residual sugar  Fractional blending (criadera-solera analogy) in small old oak pipes (550L); oxidative aging; "10 Year" = average age of the blended parcels (Source: Wikipedia — "age designations in 10-year increments representing average time spent in wood")
Wine 12
Fortification with neutral spirit; oxidative aging in small oak barrels  Fractional blending system in small old American and/or French oak; "10 Year Grand Tawny" = average 10 years of oxidative barrel aging; similar system to Port but different terroir, varieties and climate
#### c) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial position.
Wine 10
good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 11
very good to excellent quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 12
good to very good quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### Word count: 271

# **Master of Wine Exam 2021**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 all come from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and variety/ies used. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on quality and maturity. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the method of production. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of French white wines across very different regions and styles. The "same country" clue here is doing a lot of work — France is the only country that can plausibly produce four distinct, well-known white wine styles at this quality level. You could theoretically argue for the US (California Chardonnay, Oregon Pinot Gris, NY Riesling…), but the styles would be too similar. Italy only really gives you two or three recognizable whites. So France is pretty much the answer from the stem alone.

From there you need to navigate Burgundy, the Jura, the Rhone, and Alsace in the same flight. Corton Charlemagne should be identifiable — rich, weighty Chardonnay with oak and serious extract. Pinot Gris from Alsace is very distinctive — oily texture, low acidity, phenolic bitterness. The Vin Jaune is the real curveball: oxidative, walnut, curry, high alcohol, very specific to the Jura, and nothing else really tastes like it. The Grenache-based CdR-Villages white (Inopia Blanc) is the hardest — it reads warm, herby, could fool you into thinking it's Viognier or a southern Rhone blend.

Winemaking is where the marks pile up. The Vin Jaune has its own règles — six years and three months in a 228L cask under voile, no topping up. The Corton Charlemagne will have been barrel-fermented with malo. The Pinot Gris likely minimal intervention. The Rhone white its own warm-climate handling.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country" + Paper 1 (all white still) = France most likely
  - Quality/maturity AND method of production both asked = they want you to engage with specific winemaking of each wine
- Universe I considered:
  - France: Burgundy, Jura, Rhone, Alsace — all white, all very different styles
  - US: plausible but styles converge too much
  - Italy: not enough distinct named whites
- Rule-outs:
  - Germany: unlikely to include a Grenache-dominant white
  - Spain: no equivalent of Vin Jaune or Corton Charlemagne
  - Australia: can produce diverse whites but less likely at MW level for "same country" framing
- Conclusion: France confirmed by actual wines. The curveball is the CdR-Villages Grenache blend — easily the hardest identification in the flight.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin and variety/ies used.
Savagnin / Chardonnay / Likely a white Rhône blend — Grenache Blanc, Clairette, Roussanne, Viognier or combination / Pinot Gris
The combination of full-bodied on the palate with high alcohol (14.5%), racy acidity, pronounced oxidative nutty character and a very long, dry, umami-laden finish.; full-bodied palate with high alcohol (13.5%), very high acidity for the vintage, and the power and breadth typical of the corton hill.; full-bodied and broad on the palate,  is consistent with Savagnin / Chardonnay / Likely a white Rhône blend — Grenache Blanc, Clairette, Roussanne, Viognier or combination / Pinot Gris.
#### b) Comment on quality and maturity.
Wine 1
good to very good quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 2
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 3
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 4
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### c) Comment on the method of production.
Wine 1
Fermented dry in old oak barrels, then transferred to small 228 L unsealed ouillé casks where the voile (flor-like film yeast) develops  Minimum 6 years and 3 months under voile before bottling; no topping up; wine reduces to ~62% of original volume through evaporation
Wine 2
Source needed (typical Grand Cru Burgundy: whole-cluster press, barrel fermentation with indigenous yeasts)  Barrel-aged typically 12–18 months in a proportion of new French oak; batonnage common
Wine 3
Source needed (artisan producer likely using partial barrel fermentation)  Source needed
Wine 4
Stainless steel fermentation typical for cooperative production; no malo common  Stainless steel or neutral tank; early bottling
#### Word count: 320

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–7)***

Wines 5-7 are from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Compare and contrast quality and maturity. (36 marks)

b) Identify the variety. (15 marks)

For each wine:

c) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 8 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Chenin Blanc across different terroirs and quality levels. When you see "same single variety" on Paper 1 with the quality/maturity question weighted most heavily (36 marks vs 15 for variety ID), they want you to demonstrate range — not just name the grape. The likely candidates for a question like this are Chardonnay or Riesling because they age and vary hugely by origin. But Chenin is the right answer here, which is less obvious but entirely consistent with the examiner's habit of using Chenin when they want to show dramatic quality and style divergence.

The range is obvious once you taste it: W5 is a basic Anjou at 12.5% — clean, simple, decent fruit, not built for age. W6 is Savennières — serious, dry, mineral, potentially still youthful at 14 years old given the terroir and the 14.5% alcohol. Baumard's Clos du Papillon is one of Savennières' benchmark labels, so even without knowing the exact wine the seriousness should be apparent in the glass. W7 is South African Chenin from Citrusdal Mountains — riper, more tropical, different oak approach, but should still show that distinctive waxy/quince/lanolin Chenin character.

The variety ID is worth only 15 marks, which confirms the examiner cares more about whether you can articulate the quality gap than whether you correctly say "Chenin." Though you absolutely should — it's very identifiable.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same single variety" + quality/maturity marks heavily weighted (36) = grape with wide quality and style range
  - Origin asked separately (3x8) = three different origins, probably at least two countries
- Universe I considered:
  - Chardonnay: most likely candidate
  - Riesling: second most likely, fits the aging/style diversity
  - Chenin Blanc: fits Loire + SA narrative, used before in MW papers (see 2015 P1 Q2)
  - Pinot Gris: less likely for this kind of age diversity
- Rule-outs:
  - Sauvignon Blanc: doesn't age dramatically, less interesting for quality/maturity question
  - Muscat: doesn't produce this kind of tasting range
- Conclusion: Chenin confirmed by actual wines. 2015 P1 Q2 precedent directly relevant here — same Loire-to-SA structure.

Wine 6
outstanding quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 7
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### b) Identify the variety.
Chenin Blanc
The combination of contrast to the grand terroir expressions of savennières (w6 in this paper); this is the commercial, approachable end of loire chenin.; full-bodied (14.5% abv) with extraordinary acidity that remains vibrant despite age; very long, saline, mineral finish.; medium-full body (13% abv), mouth-watering acidity, and a conce is consistent with Chenin Blanc.

#### Word count: 178

### **Question 3   *(Wines 8–9)***

Wines 8 and 9 are from the same producer, the same grape variety and the same region of origin.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety and the region as closely as possible. (30 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the two wines, discussing quality, maturity, winemaking and market position. (20 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the Wachau classification system and what it means in the glass. The "same producer, same variety, same region" stem is really specific — it's essentially telling you the two wines will differ only in site quality and ripeness tier. That combination points pretty directly at a region with an internal quality hierarchy based on natural alcohol, which in Paper 1 white wines means either Alsace (Grand Cru vs. village) or the Wachau (Federspiel / Steinfeder / Smaragd).

The Riesling ID should be fairly straightforward — FX Pichler makes very pure, technically precise Riesling, the Wachau style is dry, no botrytis, high acidity, mineral. What the examiner really wants here is the comparison: W8 is a Federspiel (12.5%, lighter, earlier drinking, named after a falcon kept by local hunters) vs. W9 which is Smaragd (13.5%, richer, site-driven from Kellerberg, built to age). Both are 2018, so vintage isn't the variable.

The market position piece matters too — FX Pichler Smaragd commands serious prices internationally, whereas the Federspiel is a more accessible entry. They want you to articulate why the same producer in the same region can occupy two very different commercial tiers.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same producer, same variety, same region" = pure hierarchy question, style/quality within one producer's range
  - Two wines only, so the contrast must be really clear
  - Paper 1 (white still), no mention of blending
- Universe I considered:
  - Wachau Riesling: Federspiel vs Smaragd is the classic comparison
  - Alsace Grand Cru vs AOC: plausible, but less codified in terms of alcohol floors
  - Burgundy premier cru vs village: would likely involve Chardonnay, possible
  - Mosel GG/Kabinett: less likely as "same region" could work but Germany's hierarchy is more vintage-dependent
- Rule-outs:
  - Any blend-dominant region: stem says "same variety"
  - Any region without a visible internal ripeness/site hierarchy
- Conclusion: Wachau Riesling confirmed. The Federspiel/Smaragd comparison is an MW classic and FX Pichler is the benchmark producer.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety and the region as closely as possible.
Riesling
The combination of medium-light body (12.5% abv — top of federspiel range), very high acidity, bone dry, with a precise, focused, mineral palate and long saline finish.; full-bodied (13.5% abv), bone dry, with extraordinary concentration and extract; very high, ripe acidity that drives a long, multilayered finish. is consistent with Riesling.
#### b) Compare and contrast the two wines, discussing quality, maturity, winemaking and market position.
Wine 8
Temperature-controlled stainless steel fermentation; no new oak; preserving aromatic precision  Stainless steel; bottled relatively early to preserve freshness
Wine 9
Temperature-controlled stainless steel fermentation; no new oak; late-harvested at Smaragd ripeness levels (minimum 12.5% ABV, typically higher)  Stainless steel with extended lees contact
#### Word count: 125

### **Question 4   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 are from the same country.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the country. (15 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin and variety/ies as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used. (3 x 5 marks)

d) Comment on the market position. (3 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of white wines from the United States and your ability to navigate the dramatic quality and style range within American wine. The "same country" clue with three wines asks you to find the common thread across what could be very different styles. The US is a strong candidate here because you can plausibly get a cheap sweet wine (Moscato), a serious Napa/Sonoma Chardonnay, and an Oregon aromatic all under one flag without it feeling contrived. France could do this too but would likely lead with more cohesive regional signals.

W10 is the easy identification if you know Gallo Family Moscato — low alcohol (9%), slightly sweet, simple aromatic, mass-market California. W11 is Carneros Chardonnay at 14.5%, serious barrel-fermented wine from a prestigious single vineyard (Hyde). W12 is Willamette Valley Pinot Gris from Ponzi, one of Oregon's founding families — drier and more textured than an Alsatian version, but still aromatic.

The market position sub-question is really the point: these three wines land in completely different commercial spaces — supermarket/off-trade for Gallo, fine dining/specialist retail for Patz & Hall Hyde, and artisan/on-trade for Ponzi. The examiner wants you to articulate all three tiers.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country" + three wines + market position asked = wide quality range likely, probably USA
  - Winemaking asked (3x5) = there will be something interesting about at least one production method (e.g. arrested fermentation for the Moscato)
- Universe I considered:
  - USA: can span sweet/dry/still white across CA and OR easily
  - France: possible but tends toward more coherent regional groupings
  - Australia: possible (Riesling, Chardonnay, Semillon) but less obvious for a Moscato equivalent
- Rule-outs:
  - Italy: hard to span three such different styles under one country flag
  - Germany: could do Spatlese/Auslese/GG but usually from same region
- Conclusion: USA confirmed. The Gallo Moscato is the key identifier — mass-market, low ABV, sweet. Once you have that alongside a premium Carneros Chardonnay, the USA answer locks in.

Wine 10
Very pale straw, possibly with a slight spritz; effusively aromatic nose of peach, apricot, orange blossom, lychee and grape — pure Muscat/Moscato aromatic profile..
This style is aimed at broad retail distribution and by-the-glass placements.
Wine 11
Full-bodied (14.5% ABV), very rich and broad palate with creamy texture from full malolactic fermentation; well-integrated oak and an opulent, long finish.. 14.5% alcohol, full rich, new french.
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.
Wine 12
More restrained than Alsace Pinot Gris with less aromatic intensity and lower residual sugar — a food-friendly, dry expression typical of Oregon's approach to the variety.. 13.5% alcohol, medium balanced.
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.

This style is aimed at broad retail distribution and by-the-glass placements.

Full-bodied (14.5% ABV), very rich and broad palate with creamy texture from full malolactic fermentation; well-integrated oak and an opulent, long finish.. 14.5% alcohol, full rich, new french.
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.
Wine 12
More restrained than Alsace Pinot Gris with less aromatic intensity and lower residual sugar — a food-friendly, dry expression typical of Oregon's approach to the variety.. 13.5% alcohol, medium balanced.
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.
#### Word count: 410

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1-3 are all from the same region and producer. Each wine is from a different vintage.

For all three wines:

a) Identify the region as closely as possible, referencing each wine. (15 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the vintage. Discuss quality in the context of the region and the vintage. (3 x 20 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Bordeaux vintages and your ability to age and quality-tier a wine from the same producer across different years. "Same region and producer, different vintages" on Paper 2 means almost certainly Bordeaux — it's the region where vintage matters most, where the examiner can draw from a deep bench of well-documented years, and where a single classified growth at multiple ages makes pedagogical sense. You could argue Burgundy, Barolo, or Rioja, but Bordeaux is by far the most likely.

The key skill here is vintage knowledge. 2005 Bordeaux is a legendary year — deeply concentrated, tannic, long aging curve. 2015 is excellent — ripe, generous, approachable sooner than 2005. 2013 is the curveball — a notoriously difficult year in Bordeaux, cool and wet, lighter extraction, probably drinking sooner than either. At eight years old in the tasting room, the 2013 should show more evolution than the 2015 and much more than the 2005.

Ducru-Beaucaillou is a second growth from St Julien — elegant, cedary, classic left bank Cabernet Sauvignon-dominant. Knowing the producer isn't strictly required, but recognizing St Julien (relatively restraint vs Pauillac power, more elegance than Margaux) helps. The market question will want you to discuss auction value of the 2005 vs current-drinking appeal of the 2013.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region and producer, different vintages" on Paper 2 = Bordeaux almost certain
  - Three vintages asked to ID = vintage knowledge is core, they want you to rank quality
- Universe I considered:
  - Bordeaux classified growth: STRONG SIGNAL
  - Barolo: possible but less likely to use three vintages from same producer
  - Burgundy: possible, but "same region and producer" with vintage variation is most classic in Bordeaux
  - Rioja: possible (Gran Reserva vs Crianza from same producer) but vintage narrative less distinct
- Rule-outs:
  - New World: vintage variation less marked, less examiner interest in year-by-year conditions
- Conclusion: Bordeaux confirmed, St Julien second growth. 2005/2015/2013 is an ideal teaching flight — great, good, and difficult year from the same house.

#### Word count: 194

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–6)***

Wines 4-6 are each made from a different single grape variety. Each wine is from a different continent.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (3 x 5 marks)

b) Identify the country and region as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the style and commercial appeal. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of single-variety reds from three different continents and your ability to distinguish them by variety, origin, and commercial positioning. "Different variety, different continent" is a classic MW framing — it's essentially asking can you taste the difference between, say, Pinot Noir, Nebbiolo, and Grenache, and can you place each globally.

The three top-tier candidates for this format on Paper 2 are Cabernet Sauvignon, Pinot Noir, Syrah/Shiraz, Tempranillo, and Grenache — any of which could land in Europe, the Americas, and Oceania. The variety ID is only worth 15 marks total, which suggests these aren't obscure grapes; the examiner wants to know if you understand stylistic and commercial differences.

Flowers Sonoma Coast Pinot Noir is North America — cool climate, relatively restrained, elegant for California. Produttori del Barbaresco is the cooperative-driven benchmark for Nebbiolo in Piedmont — high acidity, firm tannin, tarry and floral. D'Arenberg Custodian is old-vine McLaren Vale Grenache — warm, spicy, plush, classic Australian style. The commercial contrast is stark: Barbaresco is fine wine with auction relevance; Custodian is accessible quality and value; Flowers sits in the premium-but-accessible US Pinot tier.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Different variety, different continent" = three distinct styles, probably recognizable varieties
  - Style and commercial appeal weighted at 30 marks = the commercial angle is key
- Universe I considered:
  - North America: Pinot Noir (Sonoma/Oregon), Cab (Napa/Sonoma), Zinfandel (CA)
  - Europe: Nebbiolo (Piedmont), Tempranillo (Rioja), Pinot Noir (Burgundy), Cabernet (Bordeaux)
  - Oceania/Australia: Shiraz (Barossa/McLaren Vale), Grenache (McLaren Vale), Cabernet (Coonawarra)
- Rule-outs:
  - Blends would violate "single grape variety" stem
  - South America continent would displace one of the above three continents — here it's N America, Europe, Australia
- Conclusion: Pinot/Nebbiolo/Grenache confirmed across three continents. Nebbiolo from Produttori is a great MW wine — cooperative benchmark, widely studied.

### **Question 3   *(Wines 7–8)***

Wines 7-8 are made from different single grape varieties. Each wine is from a different country in the Americas.

For both wines:

a) Compare and contrast the styles and consumer appeal. (30 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the variety and origin as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of lesser-known South and North American red varieties and their commercial contexts. "Different variety, different country in the Americas" on Paper 2 is somewhat unusual — the more common framing is different continents. Narrowing to the Americas is a hint that the answer isn't Cabernet Sauvignon from Napa and Malbec from Mendoza (too obvious), but something more specific.

The style comparison (30 marks, asked first) is really the point here. Both wines are at 14.5%, both single variety, both 2018 — so the examiner wants you to articulate the character of each. Petite Sirah is a curveball — deeply colored, very tannic, plummy, almost inky. It's not well-known outside California, and Bogle is a reliably consistent, value-priced producer. Carmenere is the classic Chile identity variety — herbaceous (capsicum, tobacco), medium body, soft tannins, very different from what many Europeans expect.

The consumer appeal question will want you to contrast the niche, tannic Petite Sirah (mainly domestic US market, BBQ and steak crowd) with Carmenere which has built an international identity as Chile's signature grape.

Variety ID is worth only 10 marks each, which signals these won't be obvious grapes — they want you to demonstrate you can articulate what's in the glass even if you can't name the exact variety with certainty.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Different variety, different country in the Americas" = not the obvious Cab/Malbec combo
  - Style comparison weighted at 30 marks = character description is primary
  - Variety ID at 10 marks each = probably not the most famous grapes
- Universe I considered:
  - USA: Zinfandel, Petite Sirah, Cabernet, Pinot Noir (CA or OR)
  - Chile: Carmenere, Cabernet Sauvignon, Merlot, País
  - Argentina: Malbec, Bonarda, Cabernet
- Rule-outs:
  - Cabernet Sauvignon: too obvious given only 10 marks for variety ID
  - Malbec/Merlot: less interesting stylistically for an MW comparison question
- Conclusion: Petite Sirah + Carmenere confirmed. Both are varietally very specific, stylistically very different, and both reward someone who knows the category rather than just guessing Cab.

### **Question 4   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-10 and wines 11-12 are two pairs. Each pair is from a different region of origin.

For each pair:

a) Identify the region as closely as possible, referencing each wine. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Compare and contrast style, quality, and method of production. (2 x 20 marks)

For each wine:

c) Comment on the commercial position. (4 x 5 marks)

d) Suggest an ideal drinking window. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of GSM/Grenache-dominant blends from the Rhone and Rioja, and specifically your ability to distinguish quality tiers within each region. The "two pairs, different regions" format is really a within-region quality comparison wrapped in a cross-region identification exercise.

The Rhone pair is Clos des Papes CdP 2015 vs Guigal CdR 2017 — one of the top estates in Châteauneuf paired with a generic southern Rhône. The quality gap should be obvious: the CdP is structured, complex, built to age; the Guigal CdR is fruit-forward, approachable, and relatively simple. The Rioja pair has the same architecture: La Montesa Crianza 2017 (accessible, less oak, Palacios Remondo's more commercial tier) vs Castillo Ygay Gran Reserva 2010 (a classic traditional-style Rioja with years of oak and bottle age).

The really interesting MW angle here is the cross-pair comparison — both regions are Grenache/Garnacha-dominant blends in the same style family, and the examiner wants to know if you can articulate what distinguishes Rhone from Rioja in the glass (the herbal/garrigue character of the south Rhone vs the vanilla/dried fruit of old-school Rioja). And then within each pair, whether you can articulate the quality and aging difference.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Two pairs, different regions" + Paper 2 = two recognizable red wine regions with internal quality differentiation
  - Style/quality/production comparison at 40 marks = this is really about articulating within-region differences
- Universe I considered:
  - Rhone (CdP/CdR): classic within-region quality contrast
  - Rioja (Gran Reserva/Crianza): another classic pairing
  - Burgundy: possible but less obvious when two separate pairs are required
  - Bordeaux: possible, but the stem doesn't say "same variety" which slightly reduces Bordeaux likelihood
- Rule-outs:
  - New World: less likely for a two-pair regional comparison
  - Italy: possible (Barolo/Barbera) but less commonly used in this paired format
- Conclusion: Rhone and Rioja confirmed. Both are Grenache/Garnacha-dominant, which makes the cross-regional comparison educationally very clean.

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1-3 are all sparkling wines.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies used. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production. (3 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on the quality and commercial position. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Champagne styles and your ability to distinguish between non-vintage, vintage, and New World traditional method sparkling wines. Opening Paper 3 with three sparkling wines is common — it's a chance to test the full sparkling spectrum from entry-level NV to vintage Champagne to premium non-Champagne fizz.

The key discriminations here are: NV vs vintage (W1 vs W2), and Champagne vs non-Champagne (W2/W1 vs W3). Moët Brut Imperial NV is the world's most-sold Champagne — fine but not profound, crisp, bready, accessible. Pol Roger 2012 vintage Champagne is a step up in both age and complexity — more autolytic development, more structure, more precise. Roederer Estate Brut Quartet is the California outpost of Louis Roederer, made in Anderson Valley where the cool climate produces something genuinely close to a Champagne quality level, but it will read fractionally riper and more fruit-forward.

The commercial question will want you to distinguish: Moët is global volume, available everywhere, mass-market luxury. Pol Roger is fine wine with a loyal following and strong fine dining trade. Roederer Estate is the classic "gateway" California sparkling — premium but more accessible than Champagne in price and fruit character.

Production: all traditional method, but the NV uses reserve wines, the vintage does not, and the California wine may use a slightly different assemblage of Pinot Noir, Chardonnay and Pinot Meunier.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "All sparkling" on Paper 3 Q1 = likely a Champagne-anchored flight with at least one non-Champagne
  - Variety and origin (3x10) = they want you to know it's Pinot Noir/Chardonnay/Meunier blends vs perhaps single variety for W3
  - Method (3x5) = all traditional method, but with differences in reserve wine use
- Universe I considered:
  - Champagne NV: very likely in a sparkling flight
  - Champagne vintage: frequently used to show aging potential
  - California traditional method: Roederer Estate is exactly the kind of wine MW examiners use
  - Cava / Prosecco: unlikely to appear at this level without something saying "tank method" or "Mediterranean"
- Rule-outs:
  - Prosecco/Cava: less likely at Q1 of Paper 3 which tends to use higher quality benchmarks
- Conclusion: Three traditional method sparkling wines confirmed. The Roederer Estate is the one where candidates might hesitate — it's good enough to look like Champagne but slightly riper.

#### Word count: 318

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–8)***

Wines 4-8 are all made using different methods of production.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the method of production with reference to residual sugar and alcohol levels. (5 x 10 marks)

b) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies used. (5 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on the style and quality within the context of the origin. (5 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is a classic Paper 3 Q2 format: five wines, each made by a completely different production method, and the examiner wants you to identify the method first and variety/origin second. The "different methods" stem on a five-wine flight basically guarantees you're going to see a range of techniques for achieving sweetness and/or high alcohol — dried grape, arrested fermentation (port), vin doux naturel, and botrytis or some equivalent.

The five methods here are: appassimento/drying (Amarone — dry, no RS, but high alcohol from concentrated sugar), interrupted fermentation via CO2-sparging or carbonation (Brachetto d'Acqui — lightly sparkling, sweet, low alcohol), mistelle/mutage (Graham's Vintage Port — grape spirit added during fermentation to arrest it), mutage-style VDN (Maury — same principle but using Grenache Noir in Roussillon), and partial fermentation/fortification with very high RS (Rutherglen Muscat — fortified, heavily oxidative, syrup-like).

The Brachetto is the curveball — 5.5% alcohol, frizzante, sweet red. It's unmistakably aromatic, floral, and low-ABV. The Rutherglen Muscat at 18.5% is the extreme opposite. The Amarone at 16% is the highest-ABV dry wine in the group and should show dried cherry, tar, and the tell-tale pruney/figgy character of appassimento.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "All different methods of production" + RS and alcohol specifically asked = each wine uses a distinct winemaking technique to achieve its alcohol/sweetness profile
  - Five wines = the examiner has enough to cover the full spectrum from dry/high-ABV to sweet/low-ABV
- Universe I considered:
  - Appassimento (Amarone/Recioto): STRONG SIGNAL
  - Port (fortified mid-fermentation): STRONG SIGNAL
  - VDN/Maury/Banyuls: PLAUSIBLE alongside Port
  - Rutherglen Muscat: classic Paper 3 inclusion, very distinctive
  - Low-alcohol sweet red (Brachetto, Lambrusco): PLAUSIBLE curveball
- Rule-outs:
  - Botrytis wines: not in this flight (Sauternes/TBA would typically appear in a sweet whites question)
  - Icewine: not represented here
- Conclusion: Five methods confirmed. The Brachetto is the hardest to place for most candidates — knowing it's carbonated/interrupted fermentation rather than mistelle is a specific technical point.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Comment on the method of production with reference to residual sugar and alcohol levels.
Wine 4
Appassimento — grapes harvested early October, dried on bamboo racks or wooden crates for 3-4 months, losing 35-45% weight; fermentation then lasts up to 50 days  Minimum 2 years in large oak (Slavonian botti or French tonneaux) before release; often longer
Wine 5
Charmat (tank) method for spumante style, or natural refermentation for frizzante; arrested fermentation preserves sweetness and low alcohol  Tank; early bottling; consumed fresh
Wine 6
Rapid extraction in granite lagares or modern robotic lagares; fortification with neutral grape spirit (aguardente) mid-fermentation when ~90 g/L RS remains, raising ABV to 20%  Typically 2 years in large old wood (lodge or at Douro estate) before bottling; thereafter all aging in bottle for 20+ years
Wine 7
Partial fermentation arrested by mutage — addition of neutral spirit mid-fermentation (VDN = vin doux naturel method, developed by Arnaldus de Villa Nova in late 13th C)  Aged in large old oak or concrete; "Grande Reserve" NV suggests extended aging/blending — potentially oxidative aging component
Wine 8
Harvested very late for maximum concentration and sugar; partial fermentation then fortification with neutral spirit  Fractional blending (solera-like) system in small old oak barrels exposed to temperature extremes; the "Classic" tier represents average 12 years of barrel aging
#### b) Identify the origin and grape variety/ies used.
Corvina Veronese , Rondinella , may include Molinara or Oseleta / Brachetto / Traditional Douro blend — Touriga Nacional, Touriga Franca, Tinta Roriz, Tinta Barroca, Tinto Cão / Minimum 75% Grenache Noir ; possible addition of Grenache Gris, Grenache Blanc, Macabeu or Carignan / Muscat Blanc à Petits Grains

#### Word count: 516

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-10 and 11-12 are two pairs. Each pair is from a different region of origin.

For each pair:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the style, quality and commercial position of the two wines. (2 x 20 marks)

For each wine:

c) Comment on the method of production. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the two great Iberian fortified wines — Sherry and Madeira — and your ability to distinguish them both between regions and within each pair's quality tiers.

The two-pair structure here is perfect for this comparison: both Jerez and Madeira produce dry and sweet styles from multiple grapes, and both involve oxidative aging. The key discriminations are: Sherry (W9/W10) vs Madeira (W11/W12), and within each region, basic/NV vs premium aged/vintage.

The Jerez pair is Lustau Oloroso Pata de Gallina (dry Oloroso, nutty, dried fig, very recognizable) vs González Byass Matusalem (a sweetened Oloroso using PX, 30-year average age, extremely concentrated, syrupy but with that characteristic acetaldehyde backbone). The Madeira pair is H&H Full Rich 3-year NV (cheaper, blended Madeira, made from Tinta Negra, the workhorse grape) vs H&H Single Harvest Boal 2000 (vintage Madeira, specific noble variety, 20+ years of estufagem and barrel aging).

The commercial contrast within each pair is sharp: Lustau Pata de Gallina is specialist retail with strong fine dining trade; Matusalem is a luxury collector item. H&H Full Rich is accessible export-market Madeira; the Single Harvest Boal is serious fine wine at auction prices.

Both regions use solera or oxidative aging, but the mechanism differs — Sherry solera vs Madeira's estufagem and canteiro. That's the key production point.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Two pairs, different regions" + Paper 3 fortified context = two classic oxidative fortified wine regions
  - Production method asked (2x10) = the examiner wants you to distinguish solera from estufagem/canteiro
  - Style/quality/commercial (2x20) = within-pair quality tiers are central
- Universe I considered:
  - Jerez: Fino, Amontillado, Oloroso, PX — the range is broad
  - Madeira: Sercial, Verdelho, Boal, Malmsey — style range from dry to sweet
  - Port: possible but Port more often appears as a standalone or paired with Madeira
  - Marsala: unlikely at this level
- Rule-outs:
  - Vin Jaune: no fortification, would not pair with Madeira in a "fortified pairs" question
  - Rutherglen: already appeared in Q2 of this paper
- Conclusion: Sherry and Madeira confirmed. The within-pair quality gradient (simple NV vs aged/vintage) is the core teaching point.

Wine 10

Wine 12

#### c) Comment on the method of production.
Wine 9
Fermented to dryness; fortified to 17-18% (flor cannot survive above ~15.5%), suppressing development of flor; placed into oxidative aging from the start  Criadera and solera (fractional blending system) in old American oak butts; no flor; full oxidative aging
Wine 10
As per Oloroso (flor suppressed, oxidative aging); then blended with PX for sweetness  30+ year average age in criadera-solera in old American oak; VORS (Vinum Optimum Rara Signatum) category = "Very Old Rare Sherry" min average 30 years
Wine 11
Partial fermentation then fortification; then heated via estufagem (artificial heating in tanks or pipes at ~45°C for 3 months) for younger commercial Madeiras  Minimum 3 years (hence "3 y.o.") in old wood (oak or chestnut)
Wine 12
Partial fermentation; fortification with grape spirit; aged via canteiro (passive natural warming) rather than estufagem — the premium aging method  Single Harvest (Colheita) requires minimum 5 years aging; premium bottlings age much longer in old wood under canteiro; this 2000 would have been aged 20+ years

# **Master of Wine Exam 2019**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are from two different countries. They may be blends or single varieties, but one variety is common to all.

With reference to all four wines:

a) Identify the common grape variety. (20 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on quality and style with reference to winemaking. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Sauvignon Blanc across different climates, countries and winemaking styles. The "two countries" clue combined with "one variety common to all" really narrows this down — you need a variety that is made meaningfully in at least two different countries and also exists as both pure varietal and as part of a blend. That points almost immediately to Sauvignon Blanc (Loire vs Marlborough, with Bordeaux blends also in play) or maybe Chardonnay, but Chardonnay doesn't really make a pale, piercing blanc de Bordeaux so you can rule that out fairly fast.

The curveball here is W3 — Pessac-Léognan Blanc is a Sauvignon Blanc/Sémillon blend, and you need to spot the Sauvignon Blanc thread running through a wine that also has oak, texture and Sémillon breadth. That's the whole point: can you identify the shared variety even when one expression is a lean, stony Loire example and another is a structured, barrel-shaped Bordeaux blanc?

W4 is also interesting because 9.5% ABV should immediately signal something odd — arrested fermentation or deliberate low-alcohol production. That tests whether you can separate grape identity from winemaking decisions, which is exactly what part c) is asking for.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Two different countries" — not a single region sweep
  - "May be blends or single varieties, but one variety is common to all" — classic Sauvignon Blanc/Bordeaux blend setup
  - Part a) is worth 20 marks for just naming the variety — that's a generous allocation, suggesting the examiner wants real discussion not just a one-word answer
- Universe I considered:
  - Sauvignon Blanc (Loire, Marlborough, white Bordeaux), Chardonnay, Riesling, Chenin Blanc
- Rule-outs:
  - Riesling: not used in blends the same way; two Riesling countries would more likely be Germany/Alsace
  - Chenin: possible but La Louvière is not Chenin; Pessac-Léognan is definitively Sauvignon Blanc/Sémillon
  - Chardonnay: no Bordeaux blanc connection
- Conclusion: Sauvignon Blanc — Sancerre (W1), Awatere/Marlborough (W2), Pessac-Léognan blend (W3), low-ABV Marlborough (W4)


#### Mock answer

#### Word count: 225

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–10)***

Wines 5-6, 7-8 and 9-10 are paired by country. Each pair is from a different country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety(ies) as closely as possible. (6 x 12 marks)

b) What are the key winemaking techniques used in the wine’s production? (6 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the quality. (6 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of paired-country white wines across Spain, Italy and Australia, and the ability to match very different styles within the same country. The pairing clue is a huge help — it means you can reason about the pair as a unit. Spain is a natural pair because you have Rioja Blanco and Albariño from Rías Baixas, two totally different styles but both recognisably Spanish if you know your whites. Italy is a slightly trickier pair because Sicilian Grillo (dry, medium-weight) is paired with Moscato d'Asti (sweet, frothy, 5.5% ABV) — that 5.5% alcohol is an immediate giveaway for Piedmont Moscato. The Australian pair is probably the most identifiable if you know Clare Valley Riesling: tight, lime-driven, high acid versus the Shaw and Smith M3 which is barrel-fermented, textured, cool-climate Adelaide Hills Chardonnay.

The winemaking question (b) is really the point here. The examiner wants you to contrast tank vs barrel, flor vs no flor, reductive vs oxidative, arrested fermentation vs full dry. The quality question (c) is 6 marks — not a huge allocation, so be decisive rather than exhaustive.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Paired-by-country structure tells you to reason in pairs, not six isolated wines
  - Six whites in Paper 1 mid-section usually means an international sweep of styles
  - Winemaking is worth more marks than quality, so variety/production technique is the priority
- Universe I considered:
  - Spain: Albariño (Rías Baixas), white Rioja (Viura/oak), Txakoli, Godello
  - Italy: Soave, Pinot Grigio, Vermentino, Grillo, Moscato d'Asti, Falanghina
  - Australia: Clare/Eden Riesling, Adelaide Hills Chardonnay, Hunter Semillon
- Rule-outs:
  - Spain is confirmed by the two distinct styles; Txakoli would be even more extreme in ABV; Godello possible but less likely at this quality tier
  - Italy: the 5.5% ABV wine is unmistakably Moscato d'Asti once you see it in a Paper 1 flight
  - Australia: Riesling/Chardonnay from SA is the most examined Australian white pairing in the MW corpus
- Conclusion: Spain (Rioja Blanco + Albariño), Italy (Grillo + Moscato d'Asti), Australia (Clare Riesling + Adelaide Hills Chardonnay)

#### Word count: 409

### **Question 3   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 are from two different Old World countries.

With reference to each wine:

a) Comment on the winemaking. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss the wine’s style, quality and commercial potential. Do not spend time thinking about the wine’s specific origin. (2 x 15 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of unconventional Old World white winemaking styles — specifically the ability to recognise skin-contact/orange wine production and oxidative ageing wine production, and to assess commercial potential for both without needing to pin the origin precisely. The question actually tells you not to chase specific origin, which is a signal that the wines are going to be hard to place exactly and that winemaking recognition and commercial thinking are the real marks here.

The two styles being tested are: (1) traditional qvevri skin-contact white (amber wine with phenolic grip, tannin, tea-like weight) — almost certainly going to be Georgian or possibly Friuli/Slovenian amphora-style; and (2) an oxidative, unfortified or lightly fortified dry Sicilian/Marsala-adjacent wine with rancio, walnut and nutty concentration. For part a) the examiner wants you to name the method: extended skin maceration vs fractional oxidative cask ageing. For part b) they want a realistic read on where these wines sit commercially — both are niche, both are artisan, neither is a supermarket wine.

This question is sort of a grab bag of "interesting alternative" whites, and the instruction to skip origin guessing is a kindness to the candidate.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Old World" — rules out Georgia only if you consider it New World, but Georgia IS Old World (it's the oldest wine culture)
  - "Do not spend time thinking about specific origin" — unusual instruction, confirms these are hard to place
  - Winemaking = 10 marks each, style/quality/commercial = 15 marks each — the commercial question is worth the most
- Universe I considered:
  - Orange/skin-contact: Georgia (qvevri), Friuli (Radikon, Gravner), Slovenian amphorae
  - Oxidative dry white: Jura (vin jaune), Sherry (fino/manzanilla), traditional Marsala/Vecchio Samperi style
- Rule-outs:
  - If the wine is not fortified but still 16.5% ABV and oxidative/nutty — that's Vecchio Samperi style (unfortified but concentrated through natural oxidative ageing)
  - Skin-contact wine with 12.5% ABV and phenolic grip is more likely Georgian qvevri than Friuli given the amber depth
- Conclusion: Georgian qvevri amber wine (W11) + oxidative unfortified Sicilian solera-adjacent wine (W12)

#### Word count: 127

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are made from Bordeaux varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety(ies). (4 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the quality in context of the origin, with reference to maturity and the potential for development. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Identify the key winemaking techniques used. (4 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Bordeaux grape varieties grown outside of Bordeaux, which is a well-established MW exam theme. The "Bordeaux varieties" clue means you know the varieties before you start — Cabernet Sauvignon, Merlot, Cabernet Franc, Petit Verdot, Malbec — but you still have to sort out which is dominant in each wine and where each wine comes from. That's the real challenge here.

The universe is big: you could have Right Bank Bordeaux (Merlot), Left Bank (Cab Sauv), Loire Cab Franc, Super Tuscan Cab Sauv, South Africa Bordeaux blend, Napa, Margaret River, etc. The way to navigate this is by style: Merlot-dominant right-bank Bordeaux will be soft and plummy with brick edge at 7 years; a Super Tuscan Cab Sauv will be dense with fine-grained tannins and more structural backbone; Loire Cab Franc (Saumur-Champigny) will be lighter, higher acid, more herbaceous; and a Stellenbosch blend will be big, warm and 14.5% ABV.

The 14.5% on W4 is an immediate flag for New World / warm climate — probably South Africa or Napa. Part b) asks specifically about maturity and development potential, so you need to know your vintages and assess from the glass.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Bordeaux varieties" — narrows the variety list immediately
  - Origin and variety both in part a) at 10 marks — significant
  - Part b) explicitly cites maturity and development — vintage knowledge matters
  - Part c) winemaking is only 5 marks — brief identification is enough
- Universe I considered:
  - France: Bordeaux right bank (Merlot), Loire Cab Franc (Chinon, Saumur-Champigny, Bourgueil), Bordeaux left bank
  - Italy: Super Tuscan Cab Sauv or Cab Franc blend
  - South Africa: Cape Bordeaux blend (Stellenbosch)
  - New World: Napa Cab, Margaret River, Coonawarra
- Rule-outs:
  - With four wines and the need for variety across the flight, you probably get one light Loire Cab Franc (W3), one right-bank Bordeaux (W1, satellite level given modest quality signals), one Super Tuscan (W2, dense Cab Sauv-led), one New World big blend (W4 at 14.5% — South Africa fits perfectly)
  - Napa possible for W4 but Vergelegen "V" is an iconic Cape Bordeaux and an examiner favourite
- Conclusion: Lussac-Saint-Émilion Merlot (W1), Super Tuscan Cab Sauv (W2), Saumur-Champigny Cab Franc (W3), Stellenbosch Cab Sauv-led blend (W4)
