#### Word count: 376

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–8)***

Wines 5-8 come from four different countries in North and South America.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and variety as closely as possible. (4 x 15 marks)

b) Comment on the quality and the likely market position. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the major red grape varieties of North and South America — it's a continental sweep where each country gets one wine and each wine is a different variety. That's a strong structure and fairly generous because if you can identify the varieties you can largely work out the countries: Pinot Noir from a cool California spot, Malbec from Argentina, Carménère from Chile and Tannat from Uruguay. The question allocates 15 marks to origin + variety — the biggest allocation in the question — which confirms that variety ID is the primary task here.

The curveball is W8 (Tannat, Uruguay). Most tasters will recognize massively tannic dark wine and probably reach for Madiran or possibly a robust Douro but Uruguay is genuinely unexpected. If you can place it there, that's an impressive call. Realistically you might get "probably Tannat" right on variety even if you miss the country.

W7 Carménère is slightly tricky because it can be confused with Merlot or Cabernet Franc, but the hallmark herbal/graphite/green pepper character plus Colchagua Chile is well-known territory. W5 ABC Pinot Noir from Santa Maria Valley is recognizably elegant, Burgundy-adjacent California Pinot — low oak, bright acidity, 13.5% — a useful contrast to the bigger South American wines.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Four different countries in North and South America" — one wine per country, all different
  - "Variety" singular for most wines — suggests mostly single-varietal wines
  - 15 marks for origin + variety per wine — this is the headline task
- Universe I considered:
  - USA: California Pinot Noir, Zinfandel, Napa Cab, Oregon Pinot Noir
  - Argentina: Malbec (overwhelmingly dominant choice), Torrontés (white, ruled out)
  - Chile: Carménère, Cabernet Sauvignon, Syrah
  - Uruguay: Tannat (the national variety)
  - Canada: possible but rare at MW level for reds
- Rule-outs:
  - USA: Pinot Noir fits because it's lighter and cooler (13.5%) versus the big South American wines
  - Argentina: Malbec is the only real candidate
  - Chile: Carménère is the obvious choice if the wine shows dark fruit + herbal/pyrazine notes
  - Uruguay: Tannat is the only well-known Uruguayan red variety at MW level
- Conclusion: USA Pinot Noir (Santa Maria), Argentina Malbec (Mendoza), Chile Carménère (Colchagua), Uruguay Tannat (Maldonado)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin and variety as closely as possible.
Pinot Noir / Malbec / Carménère / Tannat

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 come from Europe, but not from France, Italy or Spain.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the quality and commercial potential. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Assess the current state of maturity and the potential for development. (4 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on the possible origin and grape variety(ies). (4 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This question is less about correctly identifying the exact variety and origin (only 7 marks for that) and more about showing that you understand European reds outside the big three countries. Quality and maturity are the headline tasks. The mark split is deliberately inverted — quality/commercial first, maturity second, origin last — which tells you the examiner expects these wines to be hard to place precisely.

The "not France, Italy or Spain" clue reduces your universe to: Germany, Austria, Portugal, Greece, Hungary, Romania, Georgia, England (reds are possible but unlikely), Switzerland, and the Balkans. In practice, for a well-constructed Paper 2 question, you're probably looking at Germany (Spätburgunder), Austria (Blaufränkisch or Zweigelt), Portugal (Douro or Alentejo), and one curveball from Hungary, Greece or the Balkans. 

The fact that all four wines are 2015 vintage is interesting — same year, which could mean the examiner is testing maturity comparison across four different terroirs and varieties rather than age comparison. Germany's 2015 was warm; Austria's was warm; Douro's 2015 was excellent; Hungary's was fine. All would be approaching a nice drinking window in 2019 but with different trajectories based on grape and structure.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Not France, Italy or Spain" — unusual and explicit elimination
  - Quality + commercial = 10 marks, maturity = 8 marks, origin + variety = only 7 marks — origin is least important
  - All four wines are from 2015 — consistent vintage, unusual; may emphasize style/terroir differences over age
- Universe I considered:
  - Germany: Spätburgunder (Pinot Noir) — light body, high acid, slate mineral
  - Austria: Blaufränkisch (peppery, firm tannin, medium body), Zweigelt (lighter, cherry-driven)
  - Portugal: Douro red (Touriga Nacional blend, schist mineral, robust tannins)
  - Hungary: Cab Franc from Villány, Egri Bikavér blend
  - Greece: Xinomavro, Agiorgitiko — possible but less typical exam choice
- Rule-outs:
  - Switzerland: possible but very rarely used in MW exam
  - Balkans: less common at this quality tier
  - Germany Pinot Noir signals: pale, high acid, mineral, low-extraction — not like a traditional big red
  - Douro signals: dark, schist mineral, firm robust structure, indigenous variety complexity
- Conclusion: German Spätburgunder/Pinot Noir (W9), Austrian Blaufränkisch (W10), Douro/Portugal (W11), Hungarian Cabernet Franc from Villány (W12)

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1-2 are both from the same region.

For both wines:

a) Identify the region. (10 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the method of production. (15 marks)

c) Compare and contrast the style, quality and commercial appeal of the two wines. (25 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Champagne quality tiers and the ability to contrast large-house commercial NV against high-end grower-producer vintage Champagne. The "same region" clue narrows things down fast when both wines are sparkling — the major same-region sparkling pairs you'd consider are Champagne, Cava (DO Cava), Prosecco (Valdobbiadene vs DOC), or possibly Crémant. If both wines are autolytic, chalk-mineral and have fine persistent mousse, you're almost certainly in Champagne.

The really interesting part here is what part c) is asking — 25 marks for style, quality and commercial appeal. That's a lot. The examiner wants you to be able to say: NV house Champagne is designed for broad accessibility, consistent house style, immediate drinking, massive production, priced for restaurants and entry-level occasion; versus grower Champagne Millésime from a single vintage is smaller production, extended lees ageing (Egly-Ouriet is famous for 3–4 years on lees), deeper autolytic complexity, lower dosage, finewine-collector positioning and a very different price/distribution model.

The production contrast in part b) is also clear: multi-vintage assemblage with reserve wines vs single vintage wine; different lees ageing duration; possibly different dosage levels; grower vs négoce.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region" for two sparkling wines in Paper 3 — almost always Champagne
  - 25 marks for commercial appeal — very high allocation, examiner wants detailed commercial thinking
  - NV vs vintage, large house vs grower are the canonical Champagne contrasts
- Universe I considered:
  - Champagne (most likely for two wines of this complexity)
  - Cava (two Cava DO wines theoretically possible)
  - Prosecco DOCG vs DOC (see Q2 in this same paper — examiner split the two Prosecco questions)
- Rule-outs:
  - Given Q2 is the Prosecco pair, Q1 is almost certainly Champagne
  - If both wines show fine mousse, autolysis, chalk minerality, and 12% ABV — it's Champagne
- Conclusion: Both Champagne — NV large-house (Perrier-Jouët Grand Brut) vs vintage grower (Egly-Ouriet 2007 Grand Cru Millésime)

#### Word count: 220

### **Question 2   *(Wines 3–4)***

Wines 3-4 are both from the same region.

For both wines:

a) Identify the region. (10 marks)

b) Compare and contrast the style and quality of the two wines. (30 marks)

c) Discuss the commercial appeal of the two wines. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Prosecco quality tiers — specifically DOCG Valdobbiadene versus commercial DOC Prosecco, and what the style and quality differences look like in the glass. The "same region" clue for two light, gently sparkling, off-dry wines with pear and apple aromatics is going to point pretty quickly to Prosecco. Both wines are Glera-based, both are Charmat method, both are off-dry (Extra Dry designation means about 12–17 g/L RS), both are around 11% ABV. The examiner is testing whether you can perceive quality differences within a category that many tasters dismiss as homogeneous.

The style and quality question (part b) is worth 30 marks — by far the biggest allocation in this question — which confirms that the real skill being tested is perceiving the difference between a premium millesimato DOCG wine from hillside Valdobbiadene (Ruggeri Giustino B.) and a commodity NV DOC wine designed for the US market at volume (La Marca/Gallo). That difference shows up in perlage quality, aromatic complexity, depth on the palate and length.

Part c) on commercial appeal is interesting because these two wines have totally different commercial stories: La Marca is one of the bestselling Proseccos in the US; Ruggeri Giustino B. is a quality estate wine with limited distribution. Very different channels and price points.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Two sparkling wines, same region, Paper 3 — with Q1 already being Champagne, Q2 is likely a different sparkling category
  - Style/quality is 30 marks — the dominant task
  - Commercial appeal is a separate question (10 marks) — commercial differentiation is clearly what the examiner wants
- Universe I considered:
  - Prosecco (Valdobbiadene DOCG vs DOC) — fits perfectly
  - Cava (two different Cava producers)
  - Crémant (two from same appellation)
  - English sparkling (two from same region — unlikely to be this distinct in quality tier)
- Rule-outs:
  - The low ABV (11% / 11.5%), light mousse, off-dry character and simple pear/apple aromatics point to Prosecco not Champagne
  - Q1 is already confirmed as Champagne, so Q2 likely a different sparkling region
- Conclusion: Both Prosecco — premium DOCG Valdobbiadene millesimato (Ruggeri) vs commodity DOC NV (La Marca)

#### Word count: 179

### **Question 3   *(Wines 5–8)***

Wines 5-8 are from four different countries (two Old World and two New World), and are made from four different predominant varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 6 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production. (4 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial positioning. (4 x 12 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of rosé wines from four different countries, and the ability to distinguish styles within a category that can look superficially similar in the glass. The "two Old World, two New World" clue and "four different predominant varieties" tells you each wine is a different grape variety, so you're differentiating by colour shade, aromatics, body and ABV as well as by production method.

The two Old World wines are Provence (Grenache/Cinsault/Syrah blend, pale salmon, dry, restrained herb-mineral character, classic direct press) and Rioja Rosado (100% Tempranillo, deeper pink, more body and fruit weight, slightly earthy/rustic, also direct press with short skin contact). The two New World wines are California Pinot Noir rosé (Russian River, cool-climate, pale, delicate red fruit, 12.5%) and New Zealand rosé (Marlborough/multi-regional, commercial, fruit-forward, clean 13%). 

The commercial positioning question is worth 12 marks per wine — the highest per-wine allocation — which suggests the examiner wants genuine market analysis, not just "dry and sold in restaurants." Miraval is high-profile celebrity-backed premium Provence rosé; Cune is value-for-money Spanish rosado for the export market; Rodney Strong is quality mid-tier US rosé for the domestic on-trade; Kim Crawford is commercial volume New Zealand rosé, mostly US off-trade.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Four different predominant varieties" in four different countries — no two wines share a grape
  - Two OW, two NW — structure helps you organize the flight immediately
  - Commercial positioning = 12 marks per wine — the biggest mark allocation per wine per task
  - Origin = only 6 marks — being roughly right is enough (country + region)
- Universe I considered:
  - OW rosé: Provence (Grenache-Cinsault blend), Rioja Rosado (Tempranillo), Tavel (Grenache), Navarra, Bardolino Chiaretto
  - NW rosé: California (Pinot Noir, Zinfandel, Syrah), New Zealand, South Africa, Argentina
- Rule-outs:
  - Tavel would be fuller and often has more structure than standard Provence — probably not used alongside Miraval
  - South Africa rosé is possible but less typical at this level in the MW corpus
  - Four different varieties rules out two Grenache-based OW wines being used together
- Conclusion: Provence rosé blend (W5), Rioja Tempranillo Rosado (W6), Russian River Pinot Noir rosé (W7), NZ commercial rosé (W8)

### **Question 4   *(Wines 9–10)***

Wines 9-10 come from two different countries.

For each wine:

a) Comment on the method of production. (2 x 8 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on the style, quality, and commercial positioning. (2 x 9 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of oxidative white wine styles that are made under or near yeast veils — specifically Manzanilla (aged under flor in Sanlúcar) and Jura sous voile white wine (aged in old barrels without topping, developing a partial flor cover). These are two of the only wine styles in the world where biological ageing under yeast plays a central role in the white wine's character, so putting them together in a pair is a deliberately focused test of that knowledge.

The method of production question (8 marks each) is where the most precise knowledge is needed: you need to describe flor yeast, biological ageing in the solera system, Palomino Fino grape, albariza soils and the Atlantic-humidity conditions of Sanlúcar for the Manzanilla; and for the Jura you need to describe sous voile ageing in old barrels, Savagnin or Chardonnay, and how the partial yeast veil develops oxidative complexity without the strict 6 years / 3 months requirement of vin jaune.

The commercial question is interesting — Manzanilla and Jura white wines both face commercial headwinds because they are niche, polarizing and hard to sell to the general consumer, but both have committed specialist followings. These are not wines you'd find in a supermarket and that's worth discussing.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Two different countries" — one each
  - Production method is 8 marks (equal to origin) — the method IS the story here
  - Both wines will be oxidative/nutty/waxy — unusual for Paper 3 at this position in the flight
- Universe I considered:
  - Oxidative whites under flor: Manzanilla/Fino (Sanlúcar/Jerez), Vin Jaune (Arbois/Château-Chalon), L'Étoile sous voile
  - Also possible: dry Oloroso, dry old Madeira, dry Tawny — but these are fortified and more amber-brown
- Rule-outs:
  - If wine is pale, not sweet, 15% ABV — Manzanilla/Fino is the clear call
  - If wine is golden-amber, 13% ABV, nutty/walnut/curry character — Jura sous voile or vin jaune
  - Two countries = Spain + France is the obvious pair for these two styles
- Conclusion: Manzanilla from Sanlúcar, Spain (W9) + sous voile Jura white from L'Étoile, France (W10)

Wine 10
Barrel fermentation; likely aged sous voile (without topping) to develop oxidative character without being a formal vin jaune; duration for "Cuvée Spéciale" longer than standard (Source needed for exact protocol)  Ageing in old barrels; "Cuvée Spéciale" suggests extended ageing versus basic L'Etoile (Source needed)

#### Word count: 189

### **Question 5   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 come from two different countries.

For both wines:

a) Compare and contrast the method of production. (14 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on quality and maturity. (2 x 7 marks)

d) State the level of residual sugar (g/l). (2 x 2 marks)

e) State the level of alcohol (%). (2 x 2 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of sweet wine production methods — specifically appassimento (dried grape) production versus fortified solera-style production. The question structure is very precise: they ask you to state RS and alcohol levels to the decimal, which means they want specific knowledge of these wine styles, not vague gestures at "high sugar" and "elevated alcohol."

The two wines are clearly both sweet and both Muscat-based (Zibibbo/Muscat of Alexandria for Pantelleria; Brown Muscat/Muscat Blanc à Petits Grains for Rutherglen), which is an elegant pairing — same aromatic family, totally different production approach and origin. The appassimento method for Pantelleria means drying grapes in the sun on the island, then macerating dried grapes with fresh Zibibbo, and allowing fermentation to arrest naturally — no fortification. The Rutherglen method means late harvest or semi-dried grapes with fortification by grape spirit to arrest fermentation, then long fractional blending in old oak casks.

Part a) comparing the two production methods is 14 marks for both wines combined — the biggest single allocation — so you need to go deep on the contrast between unfortified arrested fermentation vs fortification + solera ageing.

For RS and ABV: Pantelleria is roughly 130+ g/L RS and 14.5%; Rutherglen Muscat is much higher RS (200+ g/L at even base tier) and 17%. The visual and palate differences should be dramatic if you're lucky enough to have both in front of you.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - RS and ABV asked explicitly (unusual) — examiner wants specific numbers, suggesting well-defined wine styles
  - "Compare and contrast production" for 14 marks — the main task
  - "Two different countries" — Italy + Australia would be the pair here
- Universe I considered:
  - Sweet wines from appassimento: Pantelleria (Zibibbo), Amarone-style, Vin Santo
  - Fortified sweet wines: Rutherglen Muscat (Australia), Vintage Port, Madeira, PX Sherry, Muscat de Beaumes-de-Venise
  - Two wines both being Muscat-family is a deliberate choice to highlight production method differences
- Rule-outs:
  - If both are Muscat-based with floral aromatics but different colour and ABV, it narrows immediately to Pantelleria vs Rutherglen
  - Muscat de Beaumes-de-Venise is fortified + French; possible but Rutherglen has higher concentration and tawny colour
  - Vin Santo is Trebbiano/Malvasia not Muscat — different aromatics
- Conclusion: Pantelleria Passito (appassimento, Italy) vs Rutherglen Muscat (fortified solera, Australia)

# **Master of Wine Exam 2018**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are all made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all four wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (20 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Discuss quality with reference to winemaking techniques used. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Chardonnay at vastly different quality and style levels. Because the stem says same single variety, you're working through the top single-variety whites: Chardonnay, Riesling, Pinot Gris, Sauvignon Blanc. But the 20-mark variety ID combined with origin and winemaking questions makes this very likely Chardonnay — it's the only white grape grown at serious quality levels in all four corners of the New World and Old World simultaneously.

Once you've landed on Chardonnay, the real exercise is quality discrimination. You have a commercial mass-market SE Australia wine (low acid, soft, no oak signal, 13%), a grand cru Chablis (low ABV, laser acidity, flinty mineral, bone dry, zero oak impression), a big-extraction Napa (14.9% is a dead giveaway — full malo, tropical fruit, toasty oak), and a Burgundian-aspirational Kumeu River (14%, lees complexity, but southern hemisphere fruit). The ABV spread from 12.5% to 14.9% is the most obvious structural range across any four-wine Chardonnay group you're likely to see. The quality ordering is also pretty clear: Chablis GC and Kumeu at the top, Napa premium but very different in style, [yellow tail] as the commercial anchor.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same single variety, Paper 1 (white still), 4 wines, strong marks on variety ID
  - Quality/winemaking sub-question pushes toward a variety that can be made at very different quality levels
- Universe I considered:
  - Chardonnay, Riesling, Pinot Gris, Sauvignon Blanc
- Rule-outs:
  - Riesling: rarely made at commercial commodity scale in SE Australia
  - Pinot Gris/Grigio: Kumeu River not a notable Pinot Gris producer; Napa not famous for it
  - Sauvignon Blanc: Napa not a key region; no classic GC Sauvignon
- Conclusion: Chardonnay — confirmed by the actual wines

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–10)***

Wines 5-10 are all made from different single varieties and are from different countries.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (6 x 7 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (6 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss quality with reference to winemaking techniques used. (6 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of six different single-variety whites from six different countries, which means it's deliberately going beyond the top three or four international varieties. You're going to need to identify some that are genuinely distinctive in the glass. The 42-mark variety/origin block is the bulk of the marks, so getting the variety right is critical even for the lesser-known ones.

The easier identifications are Grüner Veltliner (white pepper, bone dry, Austria — the diagnostic combo) and Torrontés (explosively floral, lychee and rose, Argentina — nothing else smells quite like it). Chenin from South Africa is recognizable if you know the tropical-fruit-heavier Cape style versus the Loire — 13.5% is a big clue that this is not Vouvray. Hunter Valley Semillon at 10% ABV is basically its own category: bone-dry but honeyed and toasty with age, no oak, insanely high acid. Pieropan Soave should show bitter almond and volcanic mineral. The genuinely tricky one is the Lopez de Heredia Gravonia — it's oxidative, amber-coloured, and made from Viura, which barely anyone knows. That one is testing whether you can identify extended wood aging in a Spanish white and make the leap to Rioja Blanco. Pretty unlikely most candidates get that one correct without specific knowledge of the Lopez de Heredia house style.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Six different varieties, six different countries — deliberately spans the globe
  - 7 marks per variety ID suggests the examiner accepts some will be missed
  - Winemaking worth 10 marks each — major emphasis on process
- Universe I considered:
  - All major single-variety whites globally: Chardonnay, Riesling, Pinot Gris, SB, Chenin, Semillon, GV, Viognier, Torrontés, Viura/Macabeo, Garganega
- Rule-outs:
  - Already used Chardonnay in Q1, so probably none here
  - Riesling would likely come from Germany/Austria — but GV already covers Austria
- Conclusion: GV (Austria), Torrontés (Argentina), Chenin (South Africa), Hunter Semillon (Australia), Garganega/Soave (Italy), Viura/Rioja Blanco (Spain)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 are made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (10 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on winemaking, quality and state of maturity. (2 x 12 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of German Riesling across the full style spectrum — specifically, whether you understand that Riesling is not a single style. You have two wines from the same variety made in completely different ways at completely different quality levels and sweetness levels. With same variety and only two wines and marks on maturity/quality, the examiner is almost certainly pushing you toward a grape that can be made bone dry and very sweet, and that has serious aging potential. In Germany that's basically only Riesling.

The two wines set up the most classic contrast in German wine: GG Trocken (bone dry, 13% ABV, full power, 2016 vintage — still young) versus Auslese (9% ABV, sweet, honeyed, already 11 years old by exam date 2018 and fully evolved). Riesling is not hard to identify once you know it — the petrol/kerosene note developing with age on the Auslese is basically diagnostic. The challenge is identifying Rheingau versus Mosel. Rheingau tends to more body and power, Mosel/Ruwer tends to more delicacy and finesse. The 13% GG is noticeably fuller than what you'd expect from a Mosel, and the 9% Auslese is obviously from the lighter, more delicate Ruwer tributary. Sub-region precision here would be impressive but just getting Germany + Riesling is the floor for marks.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same single variety, 2 wines, heavy marks on maturity/quality (24 marks total)
  - One wine will likely be sweet or off-dry, one dry — otherwise why use only two wines?
- Universe I considered:
  - Riesling (Germany, Alsace, Austria, Australia), Chenin (for dry/sweet range), Semillon
- Rule-outs:
  - Chenin already used in Q2 (South Africa); wouldn't repeat
  - The ABV contrast (13% vs 9%) makes Riesling almost certain — only German Riesling regularly sits at 9% bone dry is unusual, 9% = sweet/off-dry
- Conclusion: Riesling, one bone-dry Rheingau GG, one Mosel Auslese

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–5)***

Wines 1-5 all come from classic Western European origins.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible making reference to the grape variety(ies) used. (5 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss quality within the context of the region of origin. (5 x 8 marks)

c) What has the winemaker done to maximise quality and regional typicity during the winemaking process? (5 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the classic Western European red wine canon. The stem says "classic Western European origins" which immediately narrows you to France, Italy, and Spain as the most likely countries, possibly with Germany or Portugal at the margins. But the mark split — 10 marks for origin AND variety — tells you these are identifiable wines where the examiner expects you to name the grape. So no weird Southern Italian IGT blends here.

What's interesting about this flight is the spread of structural types: you have a 2000 Bordeaux (Merlot-dominant, fully mature, tertiary), a village-level Chambolle Pinot (relatively delicate and perfumed, 13%), a big Brunello Riserva (high tannin, high acid, Sangiovese, 14.5%), a Northern Rhône Syrah with the La Landonne smoke/meat/dark fruit character, and a mature Rioja Gran Reserva with American oak coconut-vanilla. Each of these is highly distinctive if you know the styles. The 2000 Bordeaux vintage adds maturity dimension — that wine is 18 years old by exam date, should show cedar/tobacco/dried fruit evolution. Gran Reserva Rioja at 14% with years of oak is also distinctly itself. The hardest differentiation is probably between the Côte Rôtie (pure Syrah) and the Brunello (pure Sangiovese) if you're unsure — but the tannin and acid structure plus the savory/iron quality of Brunello versus the smoked meat/violet of Syrah should be distinguishable.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Classic Western European" = top French appellations, Italian DOCG, Spanish DOCa most likely
  - 5 wines, 10 marks for origin+variety each — high confidence identifications expected
- Universe I considered:
  - Bordeaux, Burgundy, Rhône, Champagne, Rioja, Barolo/Brunello, Tuscany IGT
- Rule-outs:
  - Germany unlikely for Paper 2 (reds)
  - Portugal possible but less "classic"
  - Champagne unlikely in Paper 2
- Conclusion: Brunello (Sangiovese/Tuscany), Chambolle Pinot (Burgundy), Côte Rôtie Syrah (N. Rhône), St Emilion Merlot (Bordeaux Right Bank), Rioja Tempranillo (Spain)

### **Question 2   *(Wines 6–8)***

Wines 6-8 are made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (15 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Discuss quality with reference to winemaking techniques used. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Pinot Noir across New World regions. With three wines from the same single variety, 15 marks on variety ID, and quality/winemaking at 30 marks total, the examiner wants you to really know this grape. For Paper 2 reds, same variety with three wines: the top candidates are Pinot Noir, Cabernet Sauvignon, Syrah, or Tempranillo. But the range of fruit profiles and structural weights you'd get from these three wines — with no particularly tannic or extracted wine in the set — is most consistent with Pinot Noir.

The three wines here cover the canonical New World Pinot triangle: California Russian River (ripe, plush, dark cherry, 14.1%), Central Otago (vivid red cherry, schist-mineral edge, tighter structure), and Willamette Valley (between the two — cooler than California, riper than Burgundy, cranberry and spice). The ABV spread is relatively tight (13.5-14.1%), which means you're reading the fruit and structure more than the alcohol to differentiate. Russian River will be the ripest and most opulent. Central Otago will be the most lifted and mineral. Willamette sits in the middle. The examiner probably chose this trio specifically to test whether you can articulate regional differences within New World Pinot rather than just naming the grape.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same variety, 3 wines, 15-mark variety ID — high value on getting the grape right
  - Coming after a 5-wine Western European question — contrast in style is deliberate
- Universe I considered:
  - Pinot Noir (multi-region), Cabernet Sauvignon, Syrah, Tempranillo
- Rule-outs:
  - Cabernet: ABVs could fit but three different quality expressions across regions would show more tannin
  - Syrah: would likely show more savory/peppery character
  - Tempranillo: not grown across these three New World regions meaningfully
- Conclusion: Pinot Noir — Russian River (CA), Central Otago (NZ), Willamette Valley (OR)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–10)***

Wines 9 and 10 are made from the same single grape variety and come from the same region.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety and origin, as closely as possible. (20 marks)

For each wine:

b) Discuss the wine’s quality within the context of the region of origin. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on the winemaking techniques used. (2 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Syrah from New Zealand, which is a pretty specific ask. Same variety, same region — so you only have to name one variety and one region for 20 marks. The exercise then becomes quality and winemaking differentiation within that regional identity. What two-wine same variety/same region combinations work as an exam question? You need one that shows genuine quality or style contrast. Hawke's Bay Syrah works perfectly: a lighter, more fragrant entry-level expression (Te Mata, 12.5%) versus a flagship wine with more extraction and oak (Trinity Hill Homage, 13.5%).

The 12.5% ABV on wine 9 is a significant signal — that's on the low end for a red wine and suggests either a cool climate, a difficult vintage, or a deliberate style choice. New Zealand Syrah from Hawke's Bay is known for its cool-climate elegance, black pepper, violet, and savory character that resembles Northern Rhône more than warm-climate Australian Shiraz. If you're expecting plum jam and 14.5% you'd be wrong. The Homage is a more ambitious wine — Gimblett Gravels sourcing, more oak, more structure, more concentration. Together these two test whether you can read quality differences within the same regional identity and discuss how winemaking choices (extraction, oak, yield) impact the result.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Rule-outs:
  - Would not repeat Pinot (already done in Q2)
  - The ABV contrast between two wines from same region points to different producers/quality tiers rather than same producer
- Conclusion: Syrah, Hawke's Bay New Zealand — one entry-level fragrant style, one flagship

That's a very serious Cabernet in the mold of a Napa icon but with Chilean precision and a more restrained style. Origin identification here is the key challenge: distinguishing Lodi (San Joaquin Valley, California) from premium Chile (Maipo/Puente Alto) requires knowing both regions exist as distinct entities.

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–6)***

Wines 1-6 are presented as pairs, 1 and 2, 3 and 4, and 5 and 6\. Each pair is made by a different single producer.

For each pair:

a) Identify the origin, as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the key winemaking techniques used. (3 x 12 marks)

c) Identify the vintage(s) and consider its/their key characteristics. (3 x 10 marks)

d) Discuss the quality and maturity of the wines. (3 x 18 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of three iconic producers across three completely different wine categories — vintage Champagne, classified Sauternes/Barsac, and Vintage Port — with a paired vintage comparison within each category. The structure is clever: same producer, same wine, two different vintages. So for each pair you have to identify the origin AND read the maturity signals to sequence the wines by age.

The Champagne pair is probably the most identifiable — Dom Pérignon's consistent bead, autolytic complexity, and Pinot Noir/Chardonnay balance should be recognizable. The 1999 (19 years at exam date) will show much deeper color, toast and truffle development versus the 2009 (9 years, still relatively fresh and primary). The Sauternes pair at 14% and 13.5% ABV are both full sweet wines — amber versus golden color is the immediate visual maturity signal. The 2002 will be marmalade-and-toffee evolved; the 2013 will be primary apricot and honey. The Port pair is simplest in some ways: 20.5% ABV on both is unambiguous — that's fortified. The 1985 (33 years) will be tawny-amber with dried fruit and leather complexity; the 2009 (9 years) will be opaque purple with fresh dark berry and grippy tannin still very much present.

The vintage identification sub-questions only carry 3 marks each — the examiner isn't expecting precision, just a reasonable decade assessment and maturity commentary.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Three pairs, same producer each pair — tests producer recognition at the top level
  - Origin, winemaking, vintage/maturity — classic Paper 3 structure
  - 20.5% ABV on both W5/W6 = fortified, unambiguous
- Universe I considered:
  - For sparkling pair: Champagne prestige cuvée vs Champagne prestige cuvée (or non-Champagne sparkling)
  - For sweet pair: Sauternes/Barsac vs Tokaji vs TBA — but Coutet as premier cru Barsac is very specific
  - For fortified pair: Vintage Port is the most age-worthy fortified in the world — 1985 paired with 2009 is a textbook exercise
- Conclusion: Dom Pérignon vintages (Champagne), Château Coutet vintages (Barsac), Taylor's Vintage Port vintages (Douro)

#### Word count: 546

### **Question 2   *(Wines 7–9)***

Wines 7-9 all come from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible making reference to the grape variety(ies) used. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss the quality in the context of the region of origin. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on maturity. (3 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of French white wines from three completely different appellations and grape varieties. The stem says same country — and with three whites in Paper 3, France is the dominant candidate (Italy or Spain could work but France offers the most variety of distinct white wine styles). You need to get origin and variety for 10 marks each, which means they need to be identifiable.

The three wines are Pessac-Léognan Blanc (Sauvignon Blanc/Sémillon blend, full-bodied, toasty, 14% ABV, deep gold at 8 years), CdP Blanc (Grenache Blanc-led southern Rhône blend, aromatic, opulent, low acid, 13.5%), and a village Burgundy Chardonnay (cooler, crisper, Saint-Romain being a lesser-known side-valley appellation). The Pessac-Léognan is probably the most distinctive if you know the style — the nutty, barrel-fermented, evolved white Bordeaux character is quite unlike Burgundy. The CdP Blanc is the curveball: aromatic, full, dry but seemingly rich — most candidates will know CdP as a red appellation, so the blanc style is a test of depth. The Saint-Romain is the most classically Burgundian of the three but at village level without any premier cru designation, the complexity won't be overwhelming. Maturity sub-question (3 marks each) favors the 2010 Chevalier (oldest, most evolved, 8 years) versus the 2015 Saint-Romain (youngest, should be at peak or just past it).

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same country, three whites in Paper 3, origin+variety each — France most likely
  - Maturity discussion at 5 marks each — spans different vintages (2010, 2014, 2015)
- Universe I considered:
  - France: Burgundy, Bordeaux Blanc, Loire, Alsace, Rhône, Jura — could be any three
  - Italy: unlikely to have three very different distinct whites
  - Germany: possible but would be predominantly Riesling
- Rule-outs:
  - Riesling already covered in P1 Q3
  - Loire Chenin already used in earlier exams — possibly
- Conclusion: France — Pessac-Léognan Sauvignon/Sémillon, CdP Blanc (southern Rhône blend), Saint-Romain Chardonnay (Burgundy)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 come from different countries and different single grape varieties (minimum 90%) that are associated with the Rhône Valley.

For each wine:

a) Identify the predominant grape variety used. (3 x 5 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on the style, quality and commercial potential. (3 x 12 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Rhône-associated grape varieties when they're grown outside the Rhône Valley itself. The stem is unusually specific — it tells you the varieties are all associated with the Rhône, and that they're each from a different country. So you're looking at the main Rhône grapes: Syrah, Grenache, Mourvèdre, Cinsault, Viognier, Marsanne, Roussanne. But grown elsewhere in the world.

The three wines are Cinsault from Lodi California (12.5% ABV — lighter than you'd expect from California, pale, fragrant), Garnacha from high-altitude inland Spain (14% at 800m in Valencia — elegant rather than extracted, herbal), and Bandol Mourvèdre from Provence (14%, full body, garrigue, structured tannin). What's clever here is that only Bandol is actually in Provence adjacent to the Rhône — so the candidate has to understand the variety's global spread. Cinsault in Lodi is a genuine curveball: most people don't associate old-vine Cinsault with California. The Mustiguillo Garnacha is from a VdlT in Valencia-La Mancha, which is also not an obvious Rhône connection.

For the commercial appeal sub-question, these three wines sit at very different market positions: Birichino Bechthold is artisan/niche ($30-40 but small production, cult following), Mustiguillo is an internationally recognised artisan producer at similar pricing, and Bandol Château de Pibarnon is a serious appellation wine with aging potential and restaurant trade demand. Not exactly mass-market wines.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Rhône Valley-associated varieties" — narrows immediately to Syrah, Grenache, Mourvèdre, Cinsault, Viognier
  - Different countries, minimum 90% each variety — emphasis on variety purity
  - Style and commercial appeal — focuses on market positioning of less mainstream wines
- Universe I considered:
  - Syrah (New World), Grenache (Spain, Australia, California), Mourvèdre (Bandol, Australia), Cinsault (South Africa, California), Viognier (various)
- Rule-outs:
  - Syrah already in P2 Q3; probably wouldn't repeat
  - Viognier possible but the lightness of one wine (12.5%) doesn't fit Viognier's typical richness
- Conclusion: Cinsault from Lodi (USA), Garnacha/Grenache from inland Spain (VdlT), Mourvèdre from Bandol (France/Provence)

# **Master of Wine Exam 2017**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1-3 are all from the same country and region.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with reference to the grape variety(ies) used. (3 x 11 marks)

b) Discuss the winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (3 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss the commercial potential. (3 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the Loire Valley, specifically that France's Loire produces a range of quite distinct still white wines from different grape varieties across different sub-regions. The stem tells you same country and region — so you need three distinct whites from one country/region. That really narrows it down. France is the most likely country because the Loire is the only wine region in the world that produces three genuinely famous, distinct, single-varietal dry whites: Muscadet (Melon de Bourgogne), Chenin Blanc (Savennières, Vouvray etc.), and Sauvignon Blanc (Pouilly-Fumé, Sancerre). You could potentially get three distinct German whites from different grapes but that's unlikely as they'd probably just be different Rieslings. You can't really do this with Spain, Italy, or the New World.

The winemaking sub-question is interesting here — Muscadet is all about sur-lie ageing (lees contact for texture), Savennières is typically barrel-fermented or oak-aged with quite serious texture and age-worthiness from Chenin, and Pouilly-Fumé is almost always stainless steel with no oak, showcasing the pure varietal aromatics. So the winemaking contrast between the three is genuinely meaningful.

Commercial potential also tests very different stories: Muscadet is the cheapest and most commodity-oriented, Pouilly-Fumé sits in the mid-to-upper tier globally, and Savennières is one of the world's great dry Chenin expressions but commercially niche.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same country AND same region (not just country) — this is key, narrows to a single wine region
  - Paper 1: all still whites
  - Three different grape varieties implied (winemaking question would be boring if all one variety)
- Universe I considered:
  - Loire (Muscadet/Chenin/Sauvignon Blanc) — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Germany (three different white varieties from one region) — unlikely, would probably all be Riesling
  - Alsace (Riesling/Pinot Gris/Gewurz/Muscat) — possible but Alsace is sub-regional not regional
  - New Zealand — unlikely, no three distinct major grapes from one NZ region
- Rule-outs:
  - Spain: too few distinct famous regional whites in one region
  - Italy: hard to find three well-known distinct whites from one region
  - Australia: possible (Barossa/Clare Riesling vs. Semillon etc.) but very unlikely
- Conclusion: Loire France — Muscadet, Chenin, Sauvignon Blanc


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with reference to the grape variety(ies) used.

#### Word count: 282

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4–5)***

Wines 4-5 are made from the same grape variety.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (16 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 6 marks)

c) Discuss the wine’s quality and maturity within the context of its origin. (2 x 6 marks)

d) What are the key winemaking techniques used in the wine’s production? (2 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Pinot Gris across different climates and origins. With two wines from the same variety, and Paper 1 being white still wines, the variety has to be something that is made as a single-varietal wine in multiple countries at a decent quality level. The top candidates are Chardonnay, Riesling, Pinot Gris/Grigio, Sauvignon Blanc, or maybe Chenin Blanc. Variety identification carries the most marks here (16), which tells you the variety is identifiable but requires some work.

The pairing of Alsace 2007 and New Zealand 2015 is a classic contrasting pair. The Alsace is nine years old at tasting — developed, spicy, honeyed, textural, higher alcohol (14%), and likely quite rich. The New Zealand is two years old — fresher, more primary fruit-forward, probably less extraction and aromatic development. Same grape, same ABV (14%), but completely different style profiles. The age difference between the two (2007 vs. 2015) makes maturity assessment a genuine test, not just a formality.

Pinot Gris is probably the most plausible answer over Chardonnay or Riesling because you could have two very distinct styles from these regions — Alsace Pinot Gris has that distinctive smoky, spiced, rich quality whereas New Zealand Pinot Gris trends cleaner and more delicate. Chardonnay would also work but you'd probably expect more oak in both. Riesling from Alsace tends to be higher acid and more linear.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same variety, two wines, Paper 1 white still
  - 16 marks on variety = identifiable but requires tasting work
  - Maturity and quality asked per wine = different ages/origins likely
- Universe I considered:
  - Pinot Gris/Grigio — STRONG SIGNAL (Alsace vs. NZ is a classic contrast)
  - Chardonnay — plausible (Alsace Chardonnay is rare though)
  - Riesling — plausible but Riesling from NZ is less common at this level
  - Sauvignon Blanc — less likely as the two styles would be obvious
- Rule-outs:
  - Chardonnay unlikely because Alsace doesn't make famous Chardonnay
  - Gewurztraminer unlikely at this format (two wines, very identifiable)
- Conclusion: Pinot Gris — old Alsace (developed, rich, spiced) vs. young NZ (fresh, primary)

#### Word count: 167

### **Question 3   *(Wines 6–7)***

Wines 6-7 are from Italy and are made from different grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible with reference to the grape variety used. (2 x 12 marks)

b) Discuss the wine’s style and quality; in which area of the trade would this wine be most successful? (2 x 13 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Italian white grape varieties. The stem confirms Italy and different varieties for the two wines. So the question is: which Italian whites are famous enough to appear on an MW exam? There are really only a handful: Soave/Garganega from the Veneto, Pinot Grigio (from Alto Adige or Veneto), Vermentino (Sardinia), Arneis from Piemonte, Gavi/Cortese, Fiano, Greco di Tufo (Campania), and Verdicchio (Marche). You're probably not going to get the more obscure southern varieties at this level without more context clues.

The quality and trade sub-question is the heavy one here (13 marks each), suggesting the examiner wants you to go beyond variety/origin and actually discuss how these wines perform commercially and what their stylistic positioning is. Soave Classico is an interesting case — generic Soave is supermarket commodity stuff, but Soave Classico from a quality producer like Inama is a serious, terroir-expressive wine. That tension between the appellation's bad reputation and the quality tier is exactly what an MW question wants you to discuss. Arneis is a niche grape with decent domestic Italian market and some export presence, but it doesn't have the same global recognition as Soave.

Both wines are 2016 from Italy — same vintage, different regions and grapes, which simplifies the vintage discussion and keeps the focus on style contrast.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Italy confirmed — narrows to Italian whites
  - Different varieties — tests knowledge of the Italian white canon
  - Style/quality/trade (13 marks each) — heavy commercial weighting
  - Both 2016 — same vintage, so contrast is varietal/regional not age
- Universe I considered:
  - Soave/Garganega — STRONG SIGNAL (classic Italy white)
  - Pinot Grigio — possible but very bland to test style/quality
  - Arneis — PLAUSIBLE (identifiable and regionally distinct)
  - Gavi/Cortese — possible
  - Fiano, Greco di Tufo — curveball territory
- Rule-outs:
  - Chardonnay-based Italian wines unlikely at MW level for an Italian white question
  - Pinot Grigio too generic for a quality/trade discussion without more clues
- Conclusion: Soave Classico (Garganega, Veneto) + Arneis (Piemonte/Roero)

#### Word count: 147

### **Question 4   *(Wines 8–10)***

Wines 8-10 are all from Europe and each is made from a different grape variety.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (3 x 8 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 8 marks)

c) Consider how the winemaker has sought to retain the wine’s sense of place. (3 x 9 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of distinctive European white varieties where the variety itself is a strong signal. Three different European whites, each from a different variety — the "sense of place" sub-question for 9 marks each tells you these wines should each read as expressing their terroir distinctly, not just as commercial varietal wines.

The three wines are actually very different aromatic profiles: Muscat is intensely grapey and floral but dry (Trimbach style); Albariño is saline, peachy, and Atlantic coastal; Viognier/Condrieu is the full-on oily, honeysuckle, apricot aromatic white from the Northern Rhône. All three are highly recognizable to someone who knows them well. The variety identification (8 marks each) is relatively accessible for these three — they're not obscure — but the origin specificity in sub-part b) is where it gets harder. You need to know that dry grapey white = Alsace Muscat rather than an Italian Moscato or Greek Muscat, that the Atlantic saline peachy white is Rias Baixas specifically, and that oily/apricot/honeysuckle at 14% is Condrieu not just generic Viognier from somewhere.

The "sense of place" question is testing whether you can connect each wine's aromatic/structural character to its specific terroir rather than just naming the variety.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Europe confirmed — limits to Old World varieties
  - Three different varieties — each wine is a distinct variety ID challenge
  - "Sense of place" sub-question — implies terroir-expressive, not commodity wines
  - 8+8+9 mark structure = balanced emphasis on variety, origin, terroir
- Universe I considered:
  - Muscat (dry, Alsace) — STRONG SIGNAL once you taste it
  - Albariño (Rias Baixas) — one of Europe's most distinctive coastal whites
  - Viognier/Condrieu — classic aromatic Northern Rhône
  - Other options: Pinot Gris, Chenin, Grüner Veltliner, Verdejo — all possible
- Rule-outs:
  - Chardonnay and Sauvignon Blanc less likely as "sense of place" becomes more generic
  - Riesling very plausible too but three aromatics together is more interesting to examine
- Conclusion: Muscat (Alsace) + Albariño (Rias Baixas) + Viognier (Condrieu)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety.
Muscat d'Alsace, typically Muscat Ottonel and Muscat d'Alexandrie / Albarino / Viognier

### **Question 5   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 are made from the same grape variety.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Identify the vintage and consider the capacity to improve in the bottle. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss the method of production with particular reference to the use of oak. (2 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Chardonnay across different Burgundian origins, and specifically your ability to distinguish the oaked, richer Côte de Beaune style from the unoaked, linear Chablis style. The sub-questions are very revealing: oak is worth 7 marks per wine, which means the examiner specifically wants you to discuss oak treatment. That's a massive clue that one wine has obvious oak and the other does not. Chablis and Meursault is the classic contrast here — Meursault Premier Cru will have barrel fermentation and French oak ageing, while a quality Chablis at this level should be stainless steel or neutral.

The variety isn't asked directly — but it's clearly Chardonnay, the only grape used in both appellations. Origin is the first and heaviest sub-question (10 marks each), meaning you need to nail not just "France" or "Burgundy" but the specific sub-appellation. The vintage identification sub-question (8 marks each) is interesting because the two wines are actually from different vintages — 2013 Meursault and 2014 Chablis. Both are relatively recent but 2013 Burgundy was a challenging cooler year while 2014 was excellent in Chablis particularly. The capacity to improve aspect tests whether you know that quality Meursault 1er Cru has real aging potential while village-level Chablis is typically consumed earlier.

This question is really about knowing Burgundy's internal geography and winemaking differentiation, not just the grape.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same variety, two wines — classic contrast pair
  - Oak sub-question (7 marks each) — one oaked, one unoaked almost certainly
  - Capacity to improve — implies at least one is age-worthy
  - Origin is heaviest sub-question — need sub-appellation specificity
- Universe I considered:
  - Chardonnay (Burgundy — Chablis vs. Côte de Beaune) — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Chardonnay (Burgundy vs. somewhere else, e.g. Macon vs. Meursault) — possible
  - Chardonnay (Old World vs. New World) — possible but oak contrast is then very obvious
  - Riesling (two styles) — possible but oak question less natural for Riesling
- Rule-outs:
  - Sauvignon Blanc rarely oaked at this level
  - Pinot Gris possible but Burgundy's intra-regional contrast is the most exam-like scenario
- Conclusion: Chardonnay — oaked Meursault 1er Cru (Côte de Beaune) vs. unoaked Chablis VV

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are from different countries. They all share a common dominant grape variety.

With reference to all four wines:

a) Identify the common dominant grape variety. (16 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 8 marks)

c) Identify the key winemaking techniques used, with reference to other grape varieties and any oak maturation. (4 x 6 marks)

d) Comment on quality and maturity. (4 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Cabernet Sauvignon across different countries and quality levels. The stem says same dominant variety, four different countries — in Paper 2 (reds), this is almost certainly one of Cabernet Sauvignon, Merlot, Syrah/Shiraz, Pinot Noir, or Tempranillo. The fact that it's from four different countries makes it most likely Cabernet Sauvignon, which is the only variety genuinely made at volume in France, Australia, USA, and South Africa simultaneously. Syrah could work but is less dominant in Bordeaux. Merlot could work but is less prominent in Australia. Pinot Noir doesn't really work — it's not a dominant variety in South Africa or Australia in the way Cabernet is.

The quality spread here is enormous: a 21-year-old 1996 Classified Growth Bordeaux, an entry-level Australian commercial blend, an American oak-aged premium Californian, and a flagship South African Bordeaux-blend. The variety identification (16 marks) is accessible, but origin at 8 marks each requires getting to country level at minimum. The winemaking at 5 marks each is quite low — so they want a brief, focused answer there, probably just oak regime and fermentation basics.

The 1996 Montrose at 12.5% vs. modern New World examples at 14–14.2% is a deliberate contrast in alcohol and style — the mature Bordeaux structure vs. primary New World fruit is the key discriminator.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Four different countries, Paper 2 (reds), same dominant variety
  - Variety worth 16 marks — identifiable but central
  - Winemaking only 5 marks each — brief answers expected
  - Four countries = needs to be truly international variety
- Universe I considered:
  - Cabernet Sauvignon — STRONG SIGNAL (France/Australia/USA/SA is a textbook Cab flight)
  - Syrah/Shiraz — possible (France/Australia but less SA and less France as dominant)
  - Merlot — possible but Australia not a primary Merlot country
  - Pinot Noir — unlikely for four-country spread at this scale
- Rule-outs:
  - Tempranillo: limited to Spain primarily
  - Grenache: less prominent in these four specific countries
- Conclusion: Cabernet Sauvignon — Bordeaux/Australia/California/South Africa

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–8)***

Wines 5-8 are from the same country but from different regions.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and variety(ies) as closely as possible. (4 x 12 marks)

b) Assess the quality within the context of the region of origin. (4 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the maturity. (4 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Italian red wine regions and their key grape varieties. Same country, four different regions — the country almost has to be Italy. France could work (Burgundy/Bordeaux/Rhône/Beaujolais) but Italy is the only country with four genuinely distinct regional reds that would each come from a different variety. Spain could theoretically work but the regions aren't as distinctive varietally as Italy's.

The four wines here represent the entire Italian red spectrum: a commercial, entry-level Montepulciano d'Abruzzo; a serious Chianti Classico Riserva from a benchmark Tuscany producer; a Barolo from one of Piemonte's established houses; and an Etna Rosso which is the curveball. The Etna Rosso (Nerello Mascalese) is the most challenging — pale colour, very high ABV (15%), high tannin and acid, which is a confusing combination. The candidate needs to know that Sicily's Mount Etna produces this paradoxical style. The other three should be more accessible to a well-prepared MW candidate.

Origin and variety together for 12 marks each means you really need both — getting "Italian red" but not the region/grape won't score well. Maturity for 6 marks means they want you to assess where each wine is in its drinking window, which tests vintage knowledge and the different aging trajectories of Montepulciano (drink young) vs. Barolo (needs more time) vs. the 2012s vs. 2014.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same country, four different regions — Italy STRONG SIGNAL
  - Origin and variety together (12 marks) — both needed
  - Maturity asked — wines likely at different stages
  - Paper 2 (reds)
- Universe I considered:
  - Italy — STRONG SIGNAL (only country with 4+ distinct regional red varieties)
  - France — plausible (Burgundy/Bordeaux/Rhône/SW France) but fewer variety differences
  - Spain — possible but less likely with 4 distinct regional varieties
- Rule-outs:
  - USA: four different regions typically same varieties (Cab/Pinot everywhere)
  - Australia: similar problem — regions but fewer variety distinctions at this scale
- Conclusion: Italy — Montepulciano (Abruzzo) + Sangiovese/Chianti Classico (Tuscany) + Nebbiolo/Barolo (Piemonte) + Nerello Mascalese/Etna (Sicily)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 are made from different grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (4 x 8 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on quality and commercial potential, within the context of the region of origin. (4 x 9 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of less-mainstream red grape varieties and your ability to assess their commercial positioning. Four different varieties, and the commercial potential question worth 9 marks each makes this less about identification and more about what happens once you've identified the wine. That said, variety and origin are both worth 8 marks each, so you still need to get those right.

Looking at this group: Cabernet Franc from the Loire (Chinon) is a distinctive, lower-alcohol, herbaceous red that's relatively identifiable once you know it. Spätburgunder from Baden is essentially Pinot Noir — but German Pinot, which is fuller-bodied than Burgundy and has that distinct German precision; the taster needs to decide whether they're calling this Pinot Noir generically or getting to Germany specifically. Pinotage from Stellenbosch is one of the most identifiable reds in the world due to its rubber/acetone note, so the variety identification should be doable if you've ever tasted it. Lagrein from Alto Adige is probably the trickiest — deep inky colour, firm tannins, but very fresh acidity; it could be confused with other dark-fruited Italian reds if you're not familiar with it.

The commercial potential question is interesting because all four of these varieties have quite different global footprints: Cab Franc from Loire has strong Somm-community buzz but limited mainstream reach; German Spätburgunder is increasingly prestigious but still niche; Pinotage is almost exclusively a South African conversation; and Lagrein barely exists outside Alto Adige.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Four different varieties — classic mixed variety red flight
  - Commercial potential worth 9 marks each — highest sub-question weight
  - Paper 2 (reds), four different origins implied by variety spread
- Universe I considered:
  - Cabernet Franc (Loire/Chinon) — identifiable variety
  - Spätburgunder (Germany/Baden) — Pinot Noir style but German
  - Pinotage (South Africa) — unmistakable variety if you know it
  - Lagrein (Alto Adige) — curveball Italian variety
- Rule-outs:
  - Mainstream Pinot Noir, Cab Sauv, Syrah less likely given "commercial potential" framing suggests niche varieties
- Conclusion: Chinon (Cab Franc/Loire) + Spätburgunder (Pinot Noir/Baden) + Pinotage (Stellenbosch) + Lagrein (Alto Adige)

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–3)***

Wines 1-3 are rosés from three different countries.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible with reference to the style. (3 x 7 marks)

b) Highlight the key winemaking techniques used. (3 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss the commercial appeal and market positioning. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the full spectrum of rosé wine styles — from commercial sweet still to dry traditional-method sparkling. Three rosés from three different countries sounds simple but the styles here are radically different: a sweet, low-alcohol Californian White Zinfandel; a dry, bone-dry Provence still rosé; and a traditional-method sparkling rosé from New Zealand.

The commercial appeal sub-question carries the most marks (10 each), which makes sense because these three wines represent completely different commercial universes. White Zinfandel is mass-market, sweet, sold in US supermarkets to entry-level consumers — it's a category unto itself. Provence dry rosé is the current global premium rosé darling, driven by lifestyle marketing and on-trade placement. And sparkling NZ rosé from Cloudy Bay (LVMH-owned) sits in the premium on-trade and gifting segment, positioned more like entry Champagne than commodity pink wine.

The winemaking sub-question (8 marks each) is interesting because all three use completely different techniques: White Zinfandel is made by brief maceration and arrested fermentation; Provence rosé uses direct press; and Cloudy Bay Pelorus is traditional method with secondary fermentation in bottle and lees ageing. That's a very broad winemaking knowledge test packed into one question.

Origin relative to style (7 marks) rewards candidates who can link each wine's palate character to its provenance.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Rosés, three different countries
  - Origin relative to style — style differences are key
  - Commercial appeal highest sub-question weight
  - Paper 3 (mixed styles) — variety of production methods likely
- Universe I considered:
  - Sweet commercial US rosé (White Zinfandel) — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Dry Provence rosé — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Sparkling NZ rosé (Cloudy Bay Pelorus) — PLAUSIBLE
  - Could also be Champagne rosé, Spanish rosado, Portuguese rosé
- Rule-outs:
  - All three dry would make origin/style overlap too similar
  - Three European rosés less likely for "different countries" diversity
- Conclusion: Sweet California (White Zinfandel) + dry Provence still + traditional method sparkling NZ

#### Word count: 231

### **Question 2   *(Wines 4\)***

Consider wine 4 to be of unknown origin.

For this wine:

a) Highlight the key winemaking techniques used. (15 marks)

b) Discuss its style, quality and possible market positioning. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of orange/skin-contact white wine as a style category, and specifically your ability to describe what's happening in the glass without relying on knowing the origin. The question explicitly removes origin as a variable — "unknown origin" — which is a huge signal that the wine is hard to place and the examiner doesn't expect you to get there. Instead, almost all the marks go to winemaking (15) and style/quality/positioning (10).

The Cullen Amber is a skin-contact white wine (sometimes called "orange wine") made from Sauvignon Blanc and Sémillon with extended maceration on skins. At 15% ABV it's unusually high for a white wine, and the amber colour, tannin structure, and oxidative complexity would be very distinctive in the glass. The examiner wants you to recognize that this is a white grape wine made with prolonged skin contact — distinguishing it from an orange wine made by some other technique, or from an oxidative white like a vin jaune or an aged Rioja blanco.

The winemaking (15 marks) question is therefore really asking: can you identify skin contact maceration as the defining technique? You should also be able to discuss the biodynamic/natural wine context, ambient yeast, neutral vessel ageing. The style/quality question (10 marks) wants you to assess where this wine sits commercially — it's a niche, avant-garde product, premium-priced, targeting the natural wine, restaurant, and fine wine collector market.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Single wine, unknown origin — unusual format, signals hard-to-place style
  - Winemaking is the primary question (15 marks)
  - Style/quality/market positioning secondary (10 marks)
  - No variety or origin asked — deliberate removal of expected categories
- Universe I considered:
  - Orange/skin-contact wine — STRONG SIGNAL (the only major white wine style that would be "unknown origin")
  - Vin jaune (Jura) — could also be unknown origin but would likely be identified
  - Oxidative white Rioja — less likely as a single mystery wine
  - Georgian amber wine — possible but less likely in 2017 exam context
- Rule-outs:
  - Normal white wine wouldn't need "unknown origin" framing
  - Fortified wines have their own question slots in P3
- Conclusion: Skin-contact/orange wine — Cullen Amber from Margaret River (but candidate not expected to identify provenance)

Wine 4

#### b) Discuss its style, quality and possible market positioning.
Wine 4
good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### Word count: 76

### **Question 3   *(Wines 5–6)***

Wines 5-6 are labelled as different single grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (2 x 8 marks)

b) State the level of residual sugar (g/l) and level of alcohol (%). (2 x 4 marks)

c) Discuss the quality and commercial potential. (2 x 13 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of aromatic white varieties, and specifically your ability to assess residual sugar levels and sweetness by taste. The RS sub-question (4 marks each) is a strong signal that the wines sit at different sugar levels and the examiner wants you to quantify them — or at least place them in a band (dry, off-dry, medium-sweet, sweet). The low marks on RS/ABV suggest they want a numerical estimate or clear category, not an essay.

The variety identification (8 marks each) is very achievable here. Riesling and Gewürztraminer are two of the most recognizable varieties in the world. Riesling's lime/petrol/high acid profile and Gewürztraminer's lychee/rose/turkish delight character are about as distinct as white grapes get. The question is probably harder on the origin side — you'd need to know that a commercial, off-dry NZ Riesling at 10% exists (Giesen is one of NZ's biggest commercial Riesling producers) and that Anderson Valley in California's Mendocino makes cool-climate late harvest styles.

The quality and commercial potential (13 marks each) is the real focus. The Giesen Riesling is a commercial, off-dry entry-level New World Riesling — accessible, supermarket tier, moderate quality. The Husch Late Harvest Gewurztraminer is a sweet wine from a small California AVA — premium tier but very niche. These are very different commercial propositions: one is a volume wine for the everyday market, the other is a specialty product for wine enthusiasts and restaurant dessert lists.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Two different single varieties — clearly identifiable aromatics likely
  - RS and ABV asked (4 marks each) — must be at different sugar levels
  - Quality/commercial potential the heaviest sub-question (13 marks each)
  - Paper 3 mixed styles
- Universe I considered:
  - Riesling (off-dry or sweet) — STRONG SIGNAL (always appears in P3 as a variety)
  - Gewürztraminer (late harvest) — STRONG SIGNAL (highly aromatic, easily identifiable)
  - Muscat — PLAUSIBLE
  - Chenin Blanc sweet style — PLAUSIBLE
- Rule-outs:
  - Non-aromatic varieties less interesting for this format
  - If variety is too obscure, RS question doesn't have enough variety context
- Conclusion: Off-dry commercial Riesling (NZ) + sweet Late Harvest Gewürztraminer (California/Anderson Valley)

#### Word count: 227

### **Question 4   *(Wines 7–8)***

Wines 7-8 are made predominately from the same grape variety.

Considering both wines together:

a) Name the dominant grape variety. (10 marks)

b) Identify the origin(s) as closely as possible. (20 marks)

c) Compare and contrast the styles with reference to the likely market positioning of each wine. (20 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Grenache — specifically the contrast between its New World (Barossa) and Old World (Châteauneuf-du-Pape) expressions in a GSM-style blend. The heavy mark allocation on origin (20) and style/market comparison (20) relative to just 10 for variety tells you that the variety itself is meant to be findable — the real exam is whether you can distinguish where each wine comes from and articulate what makes them different commercially and stylistically.

Grenache is probably the right answer and it's a good test case because it's dominant in both southern Rhône blends and Barossa GSM blends. You might also consider Syrah/Shiraz which is dominant in both regions, but Grenache-dominant blends (Châteauneuf-du-Pape typically 65–70% Grenache, Torbreck Juveniles similarly Grenache-led) are the more natural pairing. At 14.5% vs. 14%, the ABVs are very close, which removes alcohol as an easy differentiator.

The style comparison is where it gets interesting: Old World CdP has the classic garrigue (thyme, lavender, herbs), iron, leather, ripe dark fruit, and minerality from galets roulés soils. The Barossa version will be riper, more jammy, fuller in body, darker fruit, more direct and less savory. Both are full-bodied serious reds but one reads as Old World terroir-expressive and the other as New World warm-climate fruit-driven. Market positioning is also quite different: CdP has global restaurant and fine wine credibility; Barossa GSM from Torbreck sits in the premium New World segment targeting wine enthusiasts and high-end restaurants.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Predominantly from same variety" — blends, not single varietal
  - Variety only 10 marks — accessible identification
  - Origin and style/market each 20 marks — the real test
  - Two reds, Paper 3 mixed paper
- Universe I considered:
  - Grenache (CdP + Barossa) — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Syrah/Shiraz (Northern Rhône + Barossa/McLaren) — PLAUSIBLE
  - Tempranillo (Rioja + somewhere else) — less likely
  - Merlot (Pomerol + something) — possible
- Rule-outs:
  - Pinot Noir doesn't blend to "predominantly" in these contexts
  - Cabernet Sauvignon covered in Q1 of this paper
- Conclusion: Grenache — Barossa GSM (Torbreck Juveniles) vs. Châteauneuf-du-Pape (Vieux Lazaret)
