#### Word count: 249

### **Question 5   *(Wines 9–10)***

Wines 9-10 are from the same country and region.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 8 marks)

b) Consider the likely vintage. (2 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on the quality and potential to improve further in the bottle. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Burgundy Pinot Noir, and specifically your ability to distinguish quality levels within Burgundy — village vs. Premier Cru — and to assess vintage character and drinking windows. Same country and region (Burgundy), same grape (Pinot Noir), but different appellations and different vintages. Both at 13% ABV.

The origin sub-question (8 marks each) wants you to get to Burgundy at minimum, and ideally to Côte de Nuits or the specific villages. These are two different levels of the Burgundy hierarchy and the quality/potential sub-question (10 marks each) rewards you for knowing that difference — CdNV (village appellation) has a shorter aging curve, while a Nuits-St-Georges 1er Cru has real cellaring potential.

The vintage sub-question (7 marks each) is interesting because the two wines are from different vintages — 2012 and 2011. At time of exam (2017), the 2012 is five years old and the 2011 is six years old. Knowing that 2012 Burgundy was a low-yield, concentrated vintage while 2011 was a more elegant, accessible year is the specific knowledge being tested. A candidate who can talk about what each vintage was like in Burgundy, and how that affects where each wine sits in its drinking window, will score well on that sub-question.

The quality comparison also tests whether you can recognize the structural differences between village and Premier Cru Pinot Noir blind — tannin quality, complexity, length.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Same country and region, Paper 3 (so could be red still in P3)
  - Vintage sub-question — different vintages likely, tests vintage knowledge
  - Quality/potential to improve — quality ladder comparison likely
  - Both 13% ABV — same region, Pinot Noir most likely
- Universe I considered:
  - Burgundy Pinot Noir (two quality levels) — STRONG SIGNAL
  - Could be a Rhône red pair — but less likely with vintage emphasis
  - Could be Loire red pair — but less internationally recognized
- Rule-outs:
  - New World same-region pairs less likely given P3 mixed paper context
  - Italy same-region with two quality levels possible but Burgundy hierarchy is more exam-friendly
- Conclusion: Burgundy Pinot Noir — CdNV village level (2012) vs. NSG 1er Cru (2011), quality ladder comparison


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 9
Côte de Nuits Villages AOC, Côte de Nuits, Burgundy
Wine 10
Nuits-St-Georges 1er Cru AOC, Côte de Nuits, Burgundy
#### b) Consider the likely vintage.
Wine 9
2012. 2012 was a challenging but rewarding vintage in Burgundy — severe frost in April decimated yields (50%+ losses in some appellations), and hail hit some areas.
Wine 10
2011. 2011 was a good but uneven vintage in Burgundy — a warm spring and excellent July raised hopes, but cool August and some rain before harvest created pressure.
#### c) Comment on the quality and potential to improve further in the bottle.
Wine 9
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 10
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### Word count: 159

### **Question 6   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 are both fortified.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 9 marks)

b) Comment on the method of production with reference to the wine’s quality. (2 x 10 marks)

c) Who would buy this wine? (2 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of fortified wines, and specifically your ability to distinguish different fortified styles by origin and production method. Both are NV and both are aged oxidative styles — but one is Spanish dry Sherry and one is French sweet VDN. That contrast is the whole point of the question.

The Palo Cortado is the rarest and most intellectually complex Sherry style — it begins its life under flor (like a Fino or Amontillado) but then the flor dies and it develops oxidatively (like an Oloroso). It has the hazelnut/almond character of an Amontillado combined with the richness and walnut of an Oloroso. At 20% ABV it's clearly fortified. Palo Cortado is the kind of wine the examiners love because it tests specific knowledge of Sherry sub-categories.

The Maury Grande Réserve is a Vin Doux Naturel from Roussillon — Grenache Noir, mutage (arrested fermentation with grape spirit), and extended oxidative ageing in old barrels or bonbonnes. At 15.5% ABV it's significantly lower than the Sherry, and it's sweet rather than dry. The "Grande Réserve" designation signals extended aging which would show tawny/rancio character.

The method of production sub-question (10 marks each) wants solera vs. VDN mutage comparison, flor biology for the Sherry, and oxidative aging for both. The "who would buy" question (6 marks each) is a softer commercial question — these are both niche, specialist, fine wine market products, not supermarket stuff.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Both fortified — confirms P3 fortified wine pair
  - Production method relative to quality (10 marks each) — wants specific technique
  - "Who would buy" (6 marks each) — commercial/consumer profile
  - NV confirmed for both — solera or aged VDN styles
- Universe I considered:
  - Dry Sherry (Jerez) — STRONG SIGNAL (one of the world's great fortified categories)
  - VDN from Roussillon/Maury or Banyuls — STRONG SIGNAL (French counterpart)
  - Port (Vintage or Tawny) — PLAUSIBLE
  - Madeira — PLAUSIBLE
  - Australian Liqueur Muscat — POSSIBLE
- Rule-outs:
  - Port covered frequently; two non-Port fortifields more interesting
  - Madeira possible but Sherry + VDN is a natural complementary pairing
- Conclusion: Dry aged Sherry (Palo Cortado, Jerez) + sweet aged VDN (Maury, Roussillon) — the classic Spain/France oxidative fortified contrast


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 11
Jerez-Xérès-Sherry DO, Marco de Jerez, Andalucía
Wine 12
Maury AOC (Vin Doux Naturel), Agly Valley, Roussillon
#### b) Comment on the method of production with reference to the wine’s quality.
Wine 11
Base wine fermented dry; fortified to ~18% to kill flor; then aged as Palo Cortado. Note: True Palo Cortado arises when a wine that started ageing biologically (under flor, like Fino/Amontillado) spontaneously loses its flor veil and develops oxidatively — a rare and unpredictable event (this is the classic definition; some producers create PC deliberately by fortifying higher and then adding older wines)  Criadera y solera system (fractional blending between multiple barrels over multiple years); the "Viejo" (old) and "CP" designation signals an aged, complex Sherry from an established solera; likely average age >30 years (Source needed for exact average solera age)
Wine 12
Mutage — arrested fermentation by addition of grape spirit (neutral alcohol) to approximately 95 g/L potential RS retained; fortified at ~6–8% ABV during fermentation  Extended oxidative ageing in old barrels, demi-muids, or bonbonnes (glass demijohns exposed to oxygen and temperature variation) — the "Grande Réserve" designation implies significant ageing, likely 10+ years average; NV solera-style blending
#### c) Who would buy this wine?
Wine 11
Amber-mahogany colour, clear with good depth.. 20.0% alcohol, full rich, neutral old.
Wine 12
Nose of dried black fruit (prune, fig, raisin), dark chocolate, coffee, vanilla, toffee, and a distinctive "rancio" character from extended oxidative ageing; complex and rich..
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### Word count: 262

# **Master of Wine Exam 2016**

## **Paper 1**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1 and 2 are from the same country. They are both blends.

For each wine:

a) Identify the region of origin as closely as possible, comment with reference to the grape varieties used. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin. (2 x 10 marks)

c) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (2 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of white blends from France. The "same country, both blends" stem immediately rules out most of the wine world — you need a country that produces multiple distinct white blends at a recognisable quality level. France is the obvious answer: the two main white blend traditions are Bordeaux Blanc (Sauvignon Blanc/Sémillon, barrel fermented, dry) and southern Rhône white (Grenache Blanc, Clairette, Roussanne, Bourboulenc, broad and aromatic). Those are really the only two well-known French white blend styles that could appear together at this level. Australia could technically work (Semillon/Chardonnay, Marsanne/Roussanne) but it's a stretch.

So you're really just deciding: is this Bordeaux white vs Rhône white, or could one be Alsace? Alsace is essentially single-variety so that's out. Languedoc could produce Roussanne/Marsanne blends but they're not iconic enough for a P1 MW question.

In practice you'd identify wine 1 as Pessac-Léognan — barrel-fermented Sauvignon/Sémillon, herbaceous lift, waxy body, some toasty oak — and wine 2 as southern Rhône white: richer, more aromatic, broader, warm climate, garrigue register. The winemaking sub-question wants you to talk about barrel fermentation vs foudre/old oak and the role of malo (likely suppressed in the Pessac, irrelevant for the Rhône). Quality sub-question rewards knowledge of Pessac-Léognan's prestige relative to generic Bordeaux Blanc.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country" + "both blends" = France almost certainly
  - Paper 1 white wines only
  - Quality question worth 20 marks = expects nuanced hierarchy discussion
- Universe I considered:
  - French white blends: Bordeaux Blanc/Pessac, Southern Rhône white, Languedoc Marsanne-Roussanne
  - Australian white blends: less likely at this quality tier
  - Spain: not a white blend tradition at this level
- Rule-outs:
  - Alsace: single variety
  - New World: rarely known for two distinct named white blend traditions
- Conclusion: Bordeaux Blanc (Pessac-Léognan) + Southern Rhône (Châteauneuf-du-Pape Blanc)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the region of origin as closely as possible, comment with reference to the grape varieties used.
Wine 1
Pessac-Léognan AOC, Pessac-Léognan, Bordeaux
Wine 2
Châteauneuf-du-Pape AOC, Southern Rhône / Châteauneuf-du-Pape, Rhône Valley
#### b) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin.
Wine 1
good to very good quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 2
outstanding quality. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### c) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style.
Wine 1
barrel fermented in French oak (proportion new oak — Source needed); malolactic fermentation likely suppressed or partially undertaken
Wine 2
typically fermented in old demi-muids or foudres; no new oak; long lees contact likely (Source needed — exact 2014 vinification protocol)
#### Word count: 151

### **Question 2   *(Wines 3–4)***

Wines 3 and 4 are from the same country and the same grape variety.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety and origin(s) as closely as possible. (16 marks)

b) Discuss winemaking with particular reference to maturation post fermentation. (14 marks)

c) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin. (20 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of California Chardonnay at two different quality tiers. The "same country, same variety" stem combined with Paper 1 immediately narrows the universe to the top single-varietal whites made globally at different quality levels. Most likely candidates are Chardonnay (it's made everywhere in the US at wildly different qualities), Riesling (possible but both from same country is tricky), or MAYBE Pinot Gris. Chardonnay is the obvious answer because it's the one variety where you can find a commercial commodity version and a premium terroir version from the same state.

The maturation question is the real focus — it's worth 14 marks and is specifically asking about post-fermentation maturation. That's a clear signal that the examiner wants you to talk about new oak vs old oak, barrel fermentation, malolactic, bâtonnage, lees aging — the California Chardonnay winemaking toolkit. The quality question at 20 marks is the biggest section, so they want you to compare these two wines meaningfully: Clos du Bois is a large-volume commodity California Chardonnay under Constellation, whereas Kistler McCrea is a single-vineyard, allocated, Burgundy-inspired premium bottling. The examiner wants you to articulate why one is better than the other and how that maps to commercial positioning and price.

In blind tasting, both should scream California Chardonnay — full malo, oak-shaped, tropical to stone-fruit driven — but Kistler has much more intensity, concentration, precision, and a higher ABV (14.1%) that signals the elevated Sonoma Mountain site.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country + same variety" = one variety, two different quality levels within one country
  - "Maturation post fermentation" = oak regime is the main winemaking topic
  - Quality = 20 marks = major mark proportion, meaning quality differentiation is key
- Universe I considered:
  - California Chardonnay (commodity vs premium)
  - German Riesling (Spätlese vs GG — possible but winemaking less distinct)
  - White Burgundy (village vs Grand Cru — possible but "same country" less useful as a limiting clue from France)
- Rule-outs:
  - Riesling: post-fermentation maturation less of a quality differentiator
  - Burgundy: possible but the maturation/quality differentiation question fits California better
- Conclusion: California Chardonnay — commercial (Clos du Bois) vs premium single-vineyard (Kistler McCrea)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the grape variety and origin(s) as closely as possible.
Chardonnay
The combination of medium-to-full body; moderate acidity from a warm california vintage; medium-long finish.; nose: stone fruit (white peach, nectarine), toasted hazelnut and creamy french oak, lemon curd, mineral tension. is consistent with Chardonnay.
#### b) Discuss winemaking with particular reference to maturation post fermentation.
Wine 3
100% malolactic fermentation; barrel fermented and/or aged in American oak with significant new oak; sur lie aging; commercial winemaking designed for consistency at scale
Wine 4
barrel fermented in Burgundy-style French oak (new oak percentage — Source needed); 100% malolactic; extended lees aging with bâtonnage; no fining/filtration typical of estate approach
#### c) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin.
Wine 3
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 4
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### Word count: 169

### **Question 3   *(Wines 5–6)***

Wines 5 and 6 are made from the same grape variety.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety. (20 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the region of origin as closely as possible. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss style and commercial appeal. (2 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Pinot Gris across two different continents. The "same grape variety" stem from two wines on Paper 1 is really asking which single white variety could show up in dramatically different styles. The top candidates for this type of Old World vs New World same-variety question are Chardonnay, Riesling, Pinot Gris/Grigio, Sauvignon Blanc, and MAYBE Chenin Blanc. Pinot Gris is actually a great choice for this because the Alsatian version (rich, full, off-dry, smoky, high ABV) looks almost nothing like the Oregon or Northern Italian version (light, crisp, dry, aromatic but restrained).

The 20-mark variety question front-loaded is a strong signal that the variety itself is identifiable but perhaps tricky — Pinot Gris is not as obvious as Sauvignon Blanc or Riesling, and the examiner wants a decisive call. Getting the variety wrong costs the most marks here.

The style and commercial appeal sub-question is where you compare the two: Zind-Humbrecht Clos Windsbuhl is a premium, biodynamic, monopole-vineyard Alsatian wine at 14.5% — rich, complex, off-dry, long-lived, premium price tier. Ponzi Oregon is medium-bodied, dry, fresh, approachable, good everyday value. The commercial appeal discussion rewards knowing how each style is positioned in market and what consumer each targets.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Single variety — both wines same grape, different origins
  - Variety worth 20 marks = high-value identification, probably not the most famous variety
  - Style + commercial question = comparing quality positions and market fit
- Universe I considered:
  - Pinot Gris: Alsace vs Oregon or Northern Italy — clear stylistic contrast
  - Riesling: Alsace vs Germany or Austria — possible
  - Chardonnay: would likely specify "same country" too
  - Sauvignon Blanc: Loire vs NZ — possible but Sauv Blanc is very obvious to ID
- Rule-outs:
  - Chardonnay: covered in Q2 of this same paper
  - Gewürztraminer: grown in fewer places, less likely to span two countries dramatically
- Conclusion: Pinot Gris — Alsace (Zind-Humbrecht) vs Oregon (Ponzi Willamette Valley)

The combination of nose: white peach, quince, smoked bacon fat (characteristic pinot gris), honey, ginger, and the distinctive waxy-unctuous register of biodynamic zind-humbrecht.; nose: fresh pear, white stone fruit, light melon, subtle smoky/spicy notes. is consistent with Pinot Gris.
#### b) Identify the region of origin as closely as possible.

Alsace AOC, Hunawihr, Alsace

#### Word count: 151

### **Question 4   *(Wines 7–8)***

Wines 7 and 8 come from the same country.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Comment on the winemaking. (2 x 8 marks)

c) Discuss style and quality. (2 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Spain's white wine traditions. The "same country" pairing of two whites on Paper 1 means you need a country with two distinct white wine regions at a recognisable quality level. Spain is the obvious answer here, as the two well-known Spanish white styles are traditional white Rioja (barrel-aged Viura) and Albariño from Rías Baixas — and they could not taste more different. The traditional white Rioja at 6 years old (2010 tasted in 2016) would be deep gold, nutty, oxidative, waxy — almost like a mature Burgundy or a Jura white. The Albariño would be pale, crisp, aromatic, stone fruit and citrus blossom, very fresh at 2 years old.

The risk for the candidate is the aged Rioja white. Many tasters won't have encountered traditional barrel-aged Viura and might call it aged white Burgundy, Jura, or even Hunter Valley Semillon. The Albariño is actually the easier one — it's distinctive enough that most candidates should get it. The examiner is really testing whether you know that Spain produces both a serious reductive aromatic white AND a complex oxidatively-aged white tradition.

Winemaking sub-question wants: Rioja = extended barrel aging in old American/French oak, possibly malo, no temperature control vs Rías Baixas = stainless steel, no oak, cold fermentation, no malo, all about aromatic preservation. Pretty unlikely this question comes from any other country — it really only works for Spain.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same country" = two white wines from one country with distinct regional styles
  - Both whites on P1 = European country most likely
  - Quality + style discussion = contrasting characters expected
- Universe I considered:
  - Spain: White Rioja (Viura) vs Albariño (Rías Baixas) — perfect contrast
  - Italy: Soave vs Falanghina, or Gavi vs Verdicchio — possible but less iconic
  - France: covered in Q1 of this paper already
  - Germany: Mosel vs Rheingau Riesling — same grape, wouldn't use "same country" framing
- Rule-outs:
  - France: already used in Q1
  - Germany/Austria: likely same variety, less interesting contrast
- Conclusion: Spain — traditional barrel-aged White Rioja (Basilio Izquierdo) vs Rías Baixas Albariño (Fefiñanes)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 7
DOCa Rioja, Rioja, La Rioja
Wine 8
Rías Baixas DO, Rías Baixas, Galicia
#### b) Comment on the winemaking.
Wine 7
extended oak aging in old barrels — the "traditional white Rioja" style that fell out of fashion in the 1980s but has been revived by artisan producers; malo likely; lees contact  extended aging in old barrels (typical for traditional white Rioja); exact duration — Source needed

### **Question 5   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 are from four different countries and are made from four different grape varieties.

For each wine:

a) Identify the grape variety. (4 x 8 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 7 marks)

c) Comment on quality and state of maturity. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of distinctive aromatic white varieties from four different countries. Four different countries, four different varieties is a classic MW grab-bag question — the examiner is giving you maximum variety signals and testing breadth. The four candidates here are Torrontés (Argentina), Gewürztraminer (New Zealand), Vouvray/Chenin Blanc (France) and Wachau Riesling (Austria).

The trick with this question is that two wines (Torrontés and Gewürztraminer) have overlapping aromatic profiles — both show rose petal, lychee, exotic florals. The key differentiation: Torrontés is bone dry despite the perfume, has higher alcohol (13.9%), and the origin is Argentina; Gewürztraminer is slightly off-dry, oilier texture, more Turkish delight and ginger. A candidate who knows both varieties should be able to split them.

The Vouvray Chenin Blanc is distinctive for its honey-quince-wool character, high acidity, and Loire signature. The Wachau Riesling Smaragd from Emmerich Knoll at 13.5% is the biggest and fullest of the Rieslings you'd encounter — fuller-bodied than Mosel or German counterparts, dry, mineral slate and petrol notes developing at 4 years. Quality and maturity sub-question: Torrontés at 1 year is at peak (it doesn't age), Gewürztraminer at 2 years is fresh, Vouvray at 3 years is drinking well but has more to give, and the Knoll Smaragd at 4 years is just entering its window.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Four different countries + four different varieties = aromatic variety showcase
  - Quality + maturity question = variety of drinking windows expected
  - These are wines 9–12 on P1, the grab-bag slot
- Universe I considered:
  - Aromatic whites: Torrontés, Gewürztraminer, Riesling, Chenin Blanc, Pinot Gris, Muscat
  - The overlap between Torrontés and Gewürztraminer is the main difficulty
- Rule-outs:
  - Chardonnay: not aromatic enough to fit this variety-heavy lineup
  - Sauvignon Blanc: covered implicitly in Q1; less likely to appear as one of four aromatics
- Conclusion: Torrontés (Argentina), Gewürztraminer (New Zealand/Nelson), Vouvray/Chenin Blanc (Loire France), Riesling Smaragd (Wachau Austria)

## **Paper 2**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–2)***

Wines 1 and 2 come from the same region and are made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the grape variety and origin as closely as possible. (18 marks)

b) Compare the winemaking styles. (18 marks)

c) Compare quality, with particular reference to both wines’ potential evolution. (14 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Gamay and cru Beaujolais, and more specifically your ability to distinguish two winemaking philosophies within the same region. The "same region, same single variety" stem on Paper 2 reds really narrows the universe. The most likely candidates are: Beaujolais (Gamay — two crus with contrasting styles), Burgundy (Pinot Noir — different village vs premier cru), Barossa/Rhône (Grenache or Shiraz from different appellations), or Rioja (Tempranillo). 

Beaujolais is the best fit because the variety question is worth 18 marks and Gamay is not as immediately obvious as Cabernet or Pinot — the examiner is rewarding candidates who can identify it correctly. Also the winemaking comparison at 18 marks is a big clue: the examiner wants you to compare carbonic/semi-carbonic Lapierre-style natural low-intervention winemaking against the more structured, Burgundian-influenced Château des Jacques approach. That's a rich winemaking discussion that rewards deep regional knowledge.

Quality and evolution at 14 marks: Moulin-à-Vent with its granite soils and more structured tannin is capable of aging more like Burgundy, while Morgon is typically more floral and approachable earlier. Lapierre at 12% ABV vs Château des Jacques at 13% already signals the difference in style and extraction.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region, same single variety" = need a region with internal stylistic contrast
  - Winemaking comparison = a big deal here, 18 marks
  - Evolution question = wines with different aging trajectories
- Universe I considered:
  - Beaujolais (Gamay): carbonic vs traditional/Burgundian styles — very strong fit
  - Burgundy (Pinot Noir): different cru expressions possible but no clear winemaking contrast
  - Barossa (Shiraz): possible but winemaking less contrast within sub-region
- Rule-outs:
  - Burgundy: would typically show different crus, not strong winemaking contrast
  - Rioja: similar winemaking philosophy across sub-zones
- Conclusion: Gamay / Beaujolais — Lapierre Morgon (natural/carbonic) vs Château des Jacques Moulin-à-Vent (Burgundian/structured)

### **Question 2   *(Wines 3–5)***

Wines 3-5 are not from France and are all made from the same single grape variety.

With reference to all three wines:

a) Identify the grape variety as closely as possible. (15 marks)

For each wine:

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with particular reference to the climate. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on quality and consider key selling points. (3 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Pinot Noir outside France. "Not from France" is the key signal — the examiner is directly removing Burgundy from the picture to see if candidates can identify Pinot in non-Burgundian contexts. This combined with "single grape variety" and Paper 2 reds means the variety is almost certainly Pinot Noir. It's the one variety where three different non-French countries can each produce a recognisably different expression while the variety character remains central. You could potentially get Syrah/Shiraz (Germany, Australia, NZ all make it) but the climate question points more toward Pinot, which is so climate-sensitive that three different climates produce dramatically different outcomes.

The three wines here span a really useful spectrum: Dog Point Marlborough is the herbal, savoury, taut cool-climate New World expression; Meyer-Näkel Ahr is the delicate, pale, acid-driven, minerally cool Old World expression; and Tyrrell's Old Winery Australia is the commercial, approachable, softer, broader-climate version. Climate sub-question directly targets these differences — cool Ahr maritime, cool-maritime Marlborough with its savoury herbal edge, vs broader South Eastern Australia. Selling points question rewards commercial awareness: Ahr Spätburgunder has the "German red wine is improving" story, Marlborough Pinot has the NZ quality narrative, and the Tyrrell's is the value-accessible easy-drinker story.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Not from France" = explicit clue ruling out Burgundy
  - "Single variety" + climate emphasis = variety that's highly climate-sensitive
  - Three countries with different climates = Pinot Noir fits perfectly
- Universe I considered:
  - Pinot Noir: NZ, Germany (Ahr), Australia — ideal spread
  - Syrah/Shiraz: possible but climate difference less dramatic across three examples
  - Cabernet Sauvignon: not typically made in Germany/Ahr at this level
- Rule-outs:
  - Grenache: not strongly represented in NZ or Germany
  - Merlot: Germany doesn't make notable Merlot
- Conclusion: Pinot Noir — Dog Point Marlborough (NZ), Meyer-Näkel Ahr (Germany), Tyrrell's Old Winery (Australia)

### **Question 3   *(Wines 6–7)***

Wines 6 and 7 come from the same region and the same vintage.

For each wine:

a) Identify the specific origin as closely as possible, with particular reference to the dominant grape variety. (2 x 9 marks)

With reference to both wines:

b) Discuss the relative quality of both wines within the context of their specific region. (18 marks)

c) Identify the vintage. (14 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Bordeaux and specifically your ability to distinguish left-bank from right-bank expressions, and then discuss relative quality hierarchy. "Same region and same vintage" on Paper 2 reds pointing to two wines with different dominant grape varieties is a massive Bordeaux signal. The only region that routinely features two wines from clearly different sub-appellations using different dominant varieties but technically the same "region" is Bordeaux — left bank (Cabernet-dominant) vs right bank (Merlot-dominant). You could theoretically get something like northern Rhône vs southern Rhône, but that's a stretch given the very different varieties and the hint about "specific origin" being worth marks.

The relative quality discussion at 18 marks is interesting — Léoville Barton is a 2ème cru classé St Julien, whereas Berliquet is Grand Cru (not Grand Cru Classé at this time). So Léoville Barton is a higher classification and a stronger commercial position, but the examiner wants you to discuss both in their specific regional context — left-bank Médoc hierarchy vs St Emilion's own system.

The vintage identification at 14 marks is unusual and very high-value — they want you to name 2005. That's one of the great Bordeaux vintages and should be identifiable by the structure, ripeness, and concentration at 11 years of age. Both wines at 11 years would be deep, structured, complex, and still not fully open.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region, same vintage" = Bordeaux almost certainly (left bank + right bank = same "region")
  - "Dominant grape variety" = expecting Cab Sauv vs Merlot discussion
  - Vintage identification = high-mark, expects knowledge of benchmark years
  - Quality comparison = hierarchy discussion, classification system
- Universe I considered:
  - Bordeaux: left bank vs right bank — ideal for this question structure
  - Northern vs Southern Rhône: possible but varieties differ more dramatically and both are from one AOC each
- Rule-outs:
  - Burgundy: single appellation would be same village, same variety
  - Italy/Spain: no classic two-sub-region same-vintage comparison tradition at MW level
- Conclusion: Bordeaux 2005 — Léoville Barton St Julien (Cabernet-dominant left bank) vs Berliquet St Emilion (Merlot-dominant right bank)

### **Question 4   *(Wines 8–9)***

Wines 8 and 9 come from the same region.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (2 x 9 marks)

b) Discuss quality within the context of the specific region. (2 x 9 marks)

c) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (2 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the Rhône Valley and specifically your ability to distinguish northern from southern Rhône. "Same region" with two clearly different reds on Paper 2 is pointing almost certainly at the Rhône, which uniquely spans both a northern section (Syrah-based, granite, cool, perfumed) and a southern section (Grenache-dominant, garrigue, warm, blended) within the broad "Rhône Valley" designation. The same basic structure applies to France more broadly (e.g., two Languedoc AOCs) but the northern/southern Rhône contrast is the most iconic at MW level.

The key differentiation: Côte-Rôtie at 13% ABV is Syrah-dominant (can include up to 20% Viognier), concentrated but lifted and perfumed, granite-driven, expensive and prestigious. Châteauneuf-du-Pape at 15% ABV is Grenache-dominant, broad, warm, garrigue-inflected, blended from multiple varieties — a completely different style despite both being labelled Rhône. The 2% ABV difference alone is a major blind tasting signal.

Winemaking sub-question wants: Côte-Rôtie = 100% Syrah typically, steep granite slopes, destemming vs whole-bunch a key choice, new oak use (Gangloff uses around 40% new); CdP = Grenache-dominant multi-variety blend, galets roulés soils, typically large old foudres or older barrels, high natural alcohol, less new oak. Quality question wants you to place each within its appellation's hierarchy — Gangloff is a cult producer, Clos des Papes is one of CdP's most respected names.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region" = Rhône is the obvious region where two very different sub-appellations share a valley name
  - Two very different ABVs (13% vs 15%) expected from these different sub-regions
  - Quality and winemaking both worth significant marks
- Universe I considered:
  - Rhône: northern (Syrah-Côte-Rôtie) vs southern (Grenache-CdP) — perfect
  - Bordeaux: possible but already used in Q3 of same paper
  - Languedoc: could have two different AOCs but less iconic
- Rule-outs:
  - Bordeaux: already featured in Q3
  - Italy: no single "region" encompassing such stylistically distinct sub-appellations
- Conclusion: Rhône Valley — Côte-Rôtie (Gangloff) vs Châteauneuf-du-Pape (Clos des Papes), both 2011

### **Question 5   *(Wines 10–12)***

Wines 10-12 come from three different single grape varieties, each closely associated with their origin.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with particular reference to the grape variety used. (3 x 12 marks)

b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (3 x 6 marks)

c) How would you sell this wine to a potential customer? (3 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of regional signature grape varieties — grapes that are so associated with one place that variety and origin are essentially one answer. The question even says "closely associated with their origin" which is a direct hint. The universe here is varieties that are effectively defined by their home country or region: Malbec/Argentina, Carménère/Chile, Blaufränkisch/Burgenland, Zinfandel/California, Pinotage/South Africa, Mencia/Galicia, Primitivo/Puglia. On Paper 2 at the end of the flight, three different countries, three distinctive varieties — this is the grab-bag.

The "how would you sell this wine" sub-question is uncommon phrasing but essentially asking for commercial appeal — who buys it, where, at what price tier, through what channel. This rewards knowing the market narrative for each: Argentine Malbec has an easy story (altitude, concentration, great value at entry tier, high-altitude Salta adds premium/boutique narrative); Blaufränkisch is an interesting sell — Austria's signature red, gaining collector interest, pairs well with food, but less consumer recognition requiring education; Carménère is Chile's identity grape with a Bordeaux-lost-variety backstory that's a great pitch to wine-curious consumers.

Moric Blaufränkisch is the hardest identification here — Blaufränkisch is not commonly encountered blind and could be confused with Pinot Noir (pale, acidic, fine tannin) or Dolcetto. The Colomé Salta Malbec at 14.5% is dense and intense, quite different from mid-altitude Mendoza Malbec — a more challenging call. Carménère should be identifiable by its herbaceous-dark-fruit Bordeaux-adjacent profile.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Closely associated with their origin" = regional signature varieties
  - Selling question = commercial positioning and market narrative
  - Three different countries
- Universe I considered:
  - Argentina/Malbec, Chile/Carménère, Austria/Blaufränkisch: all strong fits
  - South Africa/Pinotage possible but less internationally traded
  - California/Zinfandel possible but less "closely associated" phrasing typical
- Rule-outs:
  - Cabernet Sauvignon: not closely associated with one origin
  - Syrah: similarly international
- Conclusion: Malbec/Salta Argentina (Colomé), Blaufränkisch/Burgenland Austria (Moric), Carménère/Colchagua Chile (Caliterra)

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–5)***

Wines 1-5 are all made from the same single grape variety.

For each wine:

a) Discuss winemaking, with particular reference to oak. (5 x 10 marks)

b) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (5 x 8 marks)

c) Comment on quality and commercial appeal. (5 x 7 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of Chardonnay across five radically different expressions and winemaking styles. Five wines, same variety, with oak as the primary winemaking focus — this is almost certainly Chardonnay, the only white grape that spans sparkling Champagne, Côte de Beaune grand/village Burgundy, and New World premium styles in a coherent lineup. Riesling could theoretically appear in five styles but the oak question would make no sense for Riesling. Pinot Gris at five styles would be a stretch. Chardonnay is the only sensible answer.

The oak sub-question at 50 marks total (10 per wine) is the dominant focus of this question. The examiner wants you to articulate: Selosse Initial (barrel-fermented in old oak, oxidative reserve-wine philosophy, essentially a still wine with bubbles — very unusual approach for Champagne), Pol Roger Blanc de Blancs 2006 (stainless steel fermented, traditional-method, autolytic complexity from extended lees, minimal oak), Puligny-Montrachet VV Girardin (barrel-fermented in French oak, 15% new, 14 months — classic white Burgundy oak regime), Vidal Legacy Hawkes Bay (French oak barrel fermented, full malo, New World richness), and Talmard Mâcon-Villages (minimal to no oak — unoaked or lightly handled Burgundy).

So you get the full oak spectrum: heavily oxidative-barrel-shaped (Selosse), sparkling autolysis without oak (Pol Roger), measured-oak village Burgundy (Girardin and Talmard at opposite ends), and New World barrel-fermented generous (Vidal). That's a beautifully structured question testing the full Chardonnay winemaking toolkit.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same single grape variety" + oak emphasis on Paper 3 = Chardonnay across sparkling, still, New World, Old World
  - Five wines = wide variety of styles expected
  - Quality + commercial appeal = range from entry to premium
- Universe I considered:
  - Chardonnay: the only variety that spans sparkling (Champagne) + Burgundy + NZ at MW level
  - Riesling: no oak, doesn't fit the winemaking focus
  - Pinot Gris: too few examples at this geographic spread
- Rule-outs:
  - Sauvignon Blanc: not made in Champagne blanc de blancs style
  - Viognier: not made at this scale or spread
- Conclusion: Chardonnay — Selosse NV, Pol Roger 2006, Puligny-Montrachet, Vidal Hawkes Bay, Mâcon-Villages

### **Question 2   *(Wines 6–8)***

Wines 6-8 are all fortified.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (3 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (3 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on quality and maturity. (3 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the three major European fortified wine traditions. "All fortified" on Paper 3 with three wines essentially gives you the major checklist: Sherry, Port, and Madeira are the three canonical fortified styles the MW expects you to master. All three are at 20% ABV here which is a helpful visual/analytic signal. The question is not about identifying the variety (which may or may not be obvious) but about placing the origin and explaining the distinct production method for each.

The hardest identification is probably the Palo Cortado — it's a rare sherry category that combines the aromatic finesse of an amontillado nose with the body of an oloroso palate. Many candidates would correctly identify Sherry but might struggle to name Palo Cortado specifically. Tawny Port at 20 years is very recognisable — tawny amber colour, walnut, toffee, orange peel, sweet. Sercial Madeira is perhaps the most challenging because it's dry despite being fortified, with elevated acidity and maderised character; without knowing Madeira varieties candidates might correctly identify Madeira but not the Sercial.

Winemaking sub-question is rich: Palo Cortado = solera system, oxidative aging, fortification, the mystery of the Palo Cortado classification path (starting biologically, losing flor); Tawny Port = fortification during fermentation (to retain sweetness), long oxidative cask aging (20 years average), blending to style; Sercial Madeira = canteiro aging (not estufagem at this tier), fortification, maderisation through heat and oxidation, very high natural acidity from Sercial grape.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "All fortified" = the big three: Sherry, Port, Madeira
  - 20% ABV on all three = all fully fortified
  - Quality + maturity = wines with age-indicator categories (20yr Tawny, 10yr Sercial, VOS Palo Cortado)
- Universe I considered:
  - Australia makes fortified too (Rutherglen Muscat) but less likely at this MW level
  - Vin Doux Naturel (France): possible but less iconic than the big three
  - The question practically dictates Spain + Portugal + Portugal
- Rule-outs:
  - Australian fortified: possible but Jerez/Douro/Madeira is the canonical MW trio
  - Marsala or Vin Santo: wouldn't make three fortified wines at this quality level
- Conclusion: Sherry (Palo Cortado, Cayetano del Pino), Tawny Port (Graham's 20yr), Sercial Madeira (Henriques & Henriques 10yr)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 6
Jerez-Xérès-Sherry, Jerez de la Frontera, Jerez
Wine 7
Porto, Douro, Portugal
Wine 8
Madeira, Portugal
#### b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style.
Wine 6
Fermented base wine fortified, then aged in solera under long oxidative conditions; exact classification path Source needed  More than 20 years in old American oak casks according to merchant and producer notes
Wine 7

Wine 8

#### Word count: 195

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–10)***

Wines 9 and 10 are from different countries.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with particular reference to the grape varieties used. (2 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style. (2 x 10 marks)

c) Comment on quality and maturity. (2 x 5 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of botrytis sweet wines from two different countries. Two sweet whites on Paper 3 from different countries — the universe is the classic European noble rot wines: Sauternes (France), Tokaji (Hungary), Beerenauslese or Trockenbeerenauslese (Germany), and MAYBE a late-harvest from Austria. The different countries clue and the emphasis on grape varieties used as part of the origin question point toward Sauternes (Sémillon/Sauvignon Blanc/Muscadelle blend) and Tokaji (Furmint/Hárslevelű), since both involve botrytis but from totally different grape varieties.

The 9% ABV on the Tokaji is a significant signal in blind tasting — it's unusually low for a wine, caused by the natural stopping of fermentation under osmotic pressure from the aszú sugars. The Sauternes at 13.5% is a very different beast — richer, more alcoholic, oaked, broader. These two wines are a natural comparison: both are world-class noble-rot sweet wines, both are old enough (2011 and 2001) to show maturity and complexity, but they're made from completely different grapes by completely different methods.

Winemaking sub-question: Tokaji = aszú berry selection, maceration of aszú paste into base wine, puttonyos classification system, aging in used Hungarian oak (furmint fermeted dry base + aszú paste + aging); Sauternes = selective harvesting (tries), Sémillon/Sauvignon blend, barrel fermentation and aging in new French oak, long maceration. Quality question is enhanced by the vintage context: 2011 Tokaji was a good warm vintage, 2001 Sauternes is one of the great modern Sauternes vintages.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - Two sweet whites from different countries = classic noble-rot pairing
  - "Grape varieties used" in origin question = multi-variety blends expected
  - Winemaking techniques = elaborate production methods to discuss
  - Quality + maturity = both wines have age and pedigree
- Universe I considered:
  - Sauternes + Tokaji: the canonical noble-rot pairing for MW
  - German TBA/BeeA: possible but less likely alongside Tokaji (two very similar styles)
  - Austrian Trockenbeerenauslese: possible curveball
- Rule-outs:
  - Both from Germany or Austria: wouldn't fit "different countries" usefully at MW level
  - Ice wine: not botrytis, different method
- Conclusion: Tokaji Aszú 6 Puttonyos (Hungary, Royal Tokaji) + Sauternes 1er Cru Classé (France, Château Guiraud 2001)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible, with particular reference to the grape varieties used.
Wine 9
Tokaji, Tokaj, Hungary
Wine 10
Sauternes, Bordeaux, France
#### b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at this style.
Wine 9
Individually picked Aszú berries macerated with dry base wine  Aged in used Hungarian oak until bottling in 2014
Wine 10
Selective harvest of botrytised berries; exact 2001 harvest passes Source needed  Source needed, though barrique élevage is integral to top Sauternes style
#### c) Comment on quality and maturity.
Wine 9
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 10
outstanding quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
#### Word count: 138

### **Question 4   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11 and 12 come from the same region and producer.

With reference to both wines:

a) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (20 marks)

b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at these styles. (20 marks)

c) Discuss how the styles of these wines determine their commercial appeal. (10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

**Proposed annotation:**

This is testing your knowledge of the dried-grape wine tradition in Valpolicella and specifically the appassimento method. "Same region and producer" with two wines that clearly differ in style is a beautifully structured question — the examiner is asking you to figure out the origin once (worth 20 marks, reflecting the difficulty) and then contrast the winemaking approaches to produce a dry wine (Ripasso) and a sweet wine (Recioto) from the same producer and region.

The universe for "same region, same producer, different styles" on Paper 3 could theoretically be: Valpolicella (Ripasso vs Amarone, or Ripasso vs Recioto), Alsace (same producer Vendange Tardive vs Sélection de Grains Nobles), or Douro (dry table wine vs Port). In this case, with a dry and a sweet red, Valpolicella is the most distinctive fit, because the Ripasso technique — refermentation on dried Amarone/Recioto pomace — is unique to this region and gives a very specific tasting profile.

The winemaking question at 20 marks is the heart of this: Ripasso = standard Valpolicella (Corvina-led) refermented on the lees/pomace of Recioto or Amarone, picking up extra body, tannin, and dried-grape richness while remaining dry; Recioto = full appassimento of grapes on bamboo racks (arele) for 3–4 months, then fermentation stopped with residual sugar retained, resulting in a sweet, intensely concentrated red with bitter-cherry and chocolate. Both dry and sweet emerge from the same appassimento tradition but diverge completely in sweetness level.

Commercial appeal: Ripasso is the gateway product — richer than basic Valpolicella but more approachable and affordable than Amarone, very commercially successful. Recioto is a niche sweet red for dessert and cheese pairings, less commercially prominent, but loved by collectors.

**Reasoning trace (for the user's reference):**

- Stem signals I picked up on:
  - "Same region and producer" = one shared origin identification
  - Two wines with different styles = contrast in winemaking technique is the core question
  - Paper 3 = can include sweet wines, dry reds, fortified — sweet red + dry red fits
  - 20 marks on origin = identifcation is difficult, likely Italy/Valpolicella
- Universe I considered:
  - Valpolicella: Ripasso (dry, enriched) + Recioto (sweet) — perfect contrast
  - Alsace: VT + SGN from same producer — possible but both white
  - Douro: dry red + Port — possible but different ABV/fortification
- Rule-outs:
  - Alsace: wouldn't produce dry red vs sweet red contrast
  - Champagne: one sparkling one still would be unusual from same producer
- Conclusion: Valpolicella — Ripasso Superiore (Corte Sant'Alda, dry, ripasso technique) + Recioto della Valpolicella (same producer, sweet, appassimento)


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 11
Valpolicella Ripasso Superiore DOC, Valpolicella, Veneto
Wine 12
Recioto della Valpolicella DOCG, Valpolicella, Veneto
#### b) Discuss the key winemaking techniques used to arrive at these styles.
Wine 11
Refermented on Amarone or Recioto pomace in the ripasso method  Source needed
Wine 12
Grapes dried before fermentation; fermentation stopped with residual sugar retained  Source needed
#### c) Discuss how the styles of these wines determine their commercial appeal.
Wine 11
The palate is dry but broad and glycerol, with moderate-plus alcohol, supple tannins, and a darker, richer feel than straight Valpolicella.. 13.5% alcohol, full rich, mixed.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
Wine 12
The palate is sweet and full-bodied, with dense fruit, significant alcohol, and enough acidity to keep the finish from becoming heavy.. 14.5% alcohol, very sweet, dessert, mixed.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### Word count: 158

### **Question 3   *(Wines 7–10)***

Wines 7-10 are from the same country, but from two different areas. Wines 7 and 8 come from one wine region, wines 9 and 10 from another.

For each wine:

a) Identify the origin and grape variety(ies) as closely as possible. (4 x 10 marks)

b) Discuss the method of production. (4 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on quality and maturity. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This question is looking at red wines from same country and two regions where 7 & 8 are from one region and 9 and 10 are from another.  So far its hard to deduce what the wines could be because you are gotten to “one country that has different red wine making regions” of which you could have Italy, France, Spain, The US, Australia and South Africa (more likely not South Africa as you’re probably only dealing with Stellenbosch and MAYBE the Western Cape…but unlikely).  Upon tasting the wines, its clear that they are old world in nature as tehy dont have purity of fruit, they are a little rustic and sanguine.  A couple wines are high acid, high tannin and relatively high alcohol which suggests Italy and Barolo.  Baroloa is a fairly distinctive wine and there are two Nebbiolo in this flight, that would land us in Italy. 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the origin and grape variety(ies) as closely as possible.
Sangiovese / Sangiovese Grosso ; DOCG mandates single-variety / Nebbiolo / Nebbiolo
The combination of nose shows classic chianti classico sangiovese: sour cherry, dried cranberry, leather, tobacco, earth, dried herbs, and a hint of cedar.; nose shows the characteristic brunello intensity: dried cherry, pomegranate, tobacco, tar, dried rose, forest floor, leather, with secondary savoury complexity.; medium-light ruby wi is consistent with Sangiovese / Sangiovese Grosso ; DOCG mandates single-variety / Nebbiolo / Nebbiolo.
#### b) Discuss the method of production.
Wine 7
Destemmed and crushed; fermentation 28-30°C; maceration 12-15 days in steel tanks with daily pump-overs and punch-downs (source: felsina.it)  12 months in medium-capacity Slavonian oak botti plus a small percentage of second/third-use barriques; spring bottling with minimum 3 months bottle rest before release (source: felsina.it)
Wine 8
Source needed for specific vinification details; biodynamic producers typically use ambient yeasts, long macerations  DOCG regulations require minimum 4 years total aging (3.5 years in wood for standard Brunello pre-2016 revision; at San Polino likely large Slavonian oak botti rather than small barriques given traditional/biodynamic approach)
Wine 9
Similar to Barolo process but shorter maceration and lighter extraction to maintain the lighter style; fermentation in stainless steel or wooden vats  Likely older Slavonian oak botti or large oak barrels; shorter elevage than Barolo requirements (minimum 2 years total for Langhe DOC Nebbiolo versus 3 years for Barolo DOCG)
Wine 10
Gaja uses rotary fermenters for extraction in some wines; short maceration with temperature control; this gives the polished, less rustic tannin profile associated with the "modernist" school  French barriques (small barrels) and larger barrels; Gaja pioneered small barrique use for Nebbiolo in the 1980s; DaGromis uses a proportion of French oak barriques — more new oak than traditional large Slavonian botti approach
#### c) Comment on quality and maturity.
Wine 7
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 8
good to very good quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 9
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is youthful and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 10
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
#### Word count: 402

### **Question 4   *(Wines 11–12)***

Wines 11-12 are from the same country and region, but from different (predominant) grape varieties.

For both wines:

a) Identify the country, region and respective predominant grape varieties. (20 marks)

For each wine:

b) Discuss the method of production. (2 x 5 marks)

c) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin. (2 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This question is looking for reds of the same country and region but from different predominant grape varieties. That really narrows it down. What regions around the world will have different predominant grape varieties in their red wines?  It could be Bordeaux with the right and left bank. It could be Napa (who has similar blends).  It could potentially be GSM blends from Northern Rhone or Australia.  That’s about it.  In this case its Napa and one is Merlot and the other is Cab dominant.  You’d know region because its just clean pure fruit aromas and flavors youd know varieties because the merlot dominant wine is going to be plush and plummy and relatively low in tannin where the Cab dominant wine will be more tannic and deeper and tobbacoy etc.  

**Wines for Paper 2**

1\. Monthelie, 1er Cru Clos Les Champs Fulliot, Bouchard Père et Fils, 2010\. Burgundy, France. (13.5%)

#### Mock answer

Wine 11
Nose shows Napa Merlot character: plum, black cherry, cassis, vanilla, mocha, chocolate from American/French oak, with a soft, plush quality distinct from Bordeaux Merlot..
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 12
Nose shows Napa Meritage character: blackcurrant, blackberry, cedar, dark chocolate, tobacco, coffee, graphite — classic Cabernet-dominant Bordeaux blend.. 14.0% alcohol, full rich, new french.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
#### b) Discuss the method of production.
Wine 11
Fermentation in stainless steel, temperature-controlled; pump-overs; extended maceration for colour and structure  French and American oak barrels; for the Napa Valley tier (not Bancroft Ranch), typically 16-18 months; mix of new and older barrels
Wine 12
Individual lot fermentation; temperature-controlled stainless steel; pump-overs; grape-by-grape selection  French oak barriques; approximately 18-20 months for Magnificat tier; majority new French oak
#### c) Comment on quality within the context of the region of origin.
Wine 11

Wine 12
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term

## **Paper 3**

### **Question 1   *(Wines 1–4)***

Wines 1-4 are all from Europe.

For each wine:

a) Discuss the major factors involved in the method of production, with particular reference to the role of yeast. (4 x 15 marks)

b) Comment on quality and style. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is looking 4 different wines from Europe off the bat we notice that there are two sparkling wines and two oxidative still wines.  We know that the question is asking about “with particular reference to the role of yeast” so we know that they want us to talk about yeast autolysis and we can be fairly sure that we are going to talk about flor yeast with the oxidative whites so one is probably a fino sherry and the other is probably either another fino sherry or something like Arbois as there are really only two places in europe known for aging under flor.  The bubbles might both be champagne or one could be champagne and one from England.  So this question also wants us to discuss yeast autolysis.


#### Mock answer

#### a) Discuss the major factors involved in the method of production, with particular reference to the role of yeast.
Wine 1
partial barrel fermentation in old oak; malolactic fermentation typically completed Dosage: Extra Brut — approximately 3–4 g/L residual sugar
Wine 2
traditional method likely; exact winemaking protocol not publicly confirmed
Wine 3
free-run juice only (as typical for fino quality tier); fortified to 15% before solera entry  criadera y solera system under active flor yeast; single-vineyard solera aged longer than average fino
Wine 4
fermented in old oak barrels; Domaine de la Pinte is known for ouillé (topped-up) expressions of Savagnin, not the traditional sous voile/vin jaune style — a key differentiator vs oxidative Arbois  old oak barrels, topped-up to prevent flor development; length unknown for this vintage (Source needed)
#### b) Comment on quality and style.
Wine 1
good to very good quality. It is around prime maturity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 2
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
Wine 3
good quality at best, more commercial than profound. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 4

### **Question 2   *(Wines 5–8)***

Wines 5-8 are from three different countries.

For each wine:

a) Identify the region of origin. (4 x 5 marks)

b) Comment on quality and maturity. (4 x 10 marks)

c) Discuss the method of production. (4 x 10 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is a line up for 4 fortified wines two red and two white.  They are from three different countries.  What countries make fortified wines and are well known? Portugal, Spain, Australia, France and Italy.  They ask to comment on quality and maturity so they are looking at wines with some age and some that are young and some that are cheap and some that are expensive.  They are also really interested in the production methods.  Looking at the flight we see two red and two whites, the reds being the Quinta da Cavadinha Vintage Port  and JMK Shiraz VP the whites being the Matusalem Sherry  and Colheita Madeira.  The Quinta is older than the JMK and the Matusalem is older than the Colheita which we would be able to see in the color and aromatic qualities.  It would want us to distinguish between the winemaking techniques and quality of these wines with the younger wines being of more approachable and simple character than the older wines. 


#### Mock answer

#### a) Identify the region of origin.
Wine 5
Port DOC, Cima Corgo, Douro
Wine 6
Barossa Valley GI, Barossa Valley, South Australia
Wine 7
Jerez-Xérès-Sherry DO, Jerez de la Frontera, Andalucía
Wine 8
Madeira DOC, Madeira Island, Madeira
#### b) Comment on quality and maturity.
Wine 5
very good to excellent quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 6
very good to excellent quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 7
very good to excellent quality. It is already mature in style rather than youthful and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 8
very good to excellent quality. It is mature and fully evolved and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
#### c) Discuss the method of production.
Wine 5
lagares (foot-treading) or autovinification for extraction; fortified with grape spirit (aguardente) to arrest fermentation at ~20% ABV  maximum 2.5 years in wood before bottling per regulations; then long bottle aging
Wine 6
fermented to partial dryness then fortified with grape spirit to arrest fermentation; extraction maximised for VP style  limited oak aging before release (specific duration — Source needed)
Wine 7
originally fermented dry as Oloroso without flor development; fortified to 20.5% ABV; blended with sweetening component  very old criadera y solera system; "Matusalem" name (after the Biblical Methuselah — lived to 969 years) refers to extreme age
Wine 8
fermented and fortified; then aged by canteiro method (natural warehouse heat — Justino's is identified as a canteiro producer by Wikipedia)  minimum 5 years for Colheita; released after 19 years (bottled 2015 or shortly before); canteiro aging under natural Atlantic humidity and temperature fluctuation
#### Word count: 301

### **Question 3   *(Wines 9–12)***

Wines 9-12 are from four different countries.

For each wine:

a) State alcohol level (%). (4 x 2 marks)

b) Place a tick in the appropriate box for the residual sugar. (4 x 2 marks)

c) Identify the origin as closely as possible. (4 x 5 marks)

d) From what you can detect in the glass, describe the method of production from vineyard to bottle. (4 x 10 marks)

e) Comment on quality and maturity. (4 x 6 marks)

*Notes / Examiner intent:*

This is looking at 4 different sweet wines from four different countries.  Thse are most likely going to be wines like Tokaji, Sauternes, Trokenbeerenauslese from Germany or some other sweet german riesling, maybe an ice wine from Canada and maybe vin santo from Italy it could also be sweet port potentially, but if they are all white it will likely be those, but it could also be white port. In this case, its a Spatlese form Germany, an ice wine from canada, a tokaji and a vin santo.  They want you to be able to distinguish levels of sugar between them, the ice wine is going to be insanely sweet whereas the spatlese is likely to be the lowest level of sugar of all of them.  From most to least sugar it would go ice wine, vin santo, tokaji and spatlese. 


#### Mock answer

#### a) State alcohol level (%).
Wine 9
The Mosel Spätlese profile: light-bodied, 8.5% ABV, pronounced acidity, off-dry residual sugar that counterbalances the high acidity without appearing sweet..
Wine 10
Palate: viscously sweet, extremely concentrated, residual sugar ~180–320 g/L, but electric acidity prevents cloy.. 9.5% alcohol, very sweet, dessert.
Wine 11
Palate: rich and sweet but lifted by very high acidity (the Furmint contribution); the 5-puttonyos level (~150–180 g/L residual sugar) delivers concentrated sweetness without treacle-like heaviness..
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 12
Palate: sweet but never cloying (as the producer describes it); very long finish with walnut and bitter-orange complexity from the extended barrel aging.. 13.0% alcohol, sweet, dessert, neutral old.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### b) Place a tick in the appropriate box for the residual sugar.
Wine 9
The Mosel Spätlese profile: light-bodied, 8.5% ABV, pronounced acidity, off-dry residual sugar that counterbalances the high acidity without appearing sweet.. 8.5% alcohol, off dry, light crisp.
This sits comfortably in premium independent retail and better restaurant lists.
Wine 10
Palate: viscously sweet, extremely concentrated, residual sugar ~180–320 g/L, but electric acidity prevents cloy.. 9.5% alcohol, very sweet, dessert.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 11
Palate: rich and sweet but lifted by very high acidity (the Furmint contribution); the 5-puttonyos level (~150–180 g/L residual sugar) delivers concentrated sweetness without treacle-like heaviness.. 11.5% alcohol, very sweet, dessert, neutral old.
This is more naturally placed in specialist retail and fine dining than in broad volume channels.
Wine 12
Palate: sweet but never cloying (as the producer describes it); very long finish with walnut and bitter-orange complexity from the extended barrel aging.. 13.0% alcohol, sweet, dessert, neutral old.
This is commercially viable in niche-to-premium channels rather than mass-market distribution.
#### c) Identify the origin as closely as possible.
Wine 9
Mosel QmP Spätlese, Mittelmosel / Brauneberg, Mosel
Wine 10
VQA Ontario, Niagara Peninsula, Ontario
Wine 11
Tokaji Aszú DOC (PDO), Tokaj, Tokaj-Hegyalja
Wine 12
Vin Santo del Chianti Classico DOC, Chianti Classico, Tuscany
#### d) From what you can detect in the glass, describe the method of production from vineyard to bottle.
Wine 9
fermented in Fuder (old large oak casks) or stainless steel; minimal intervention; no malo (not typical in Mosel); residual sugar left by stopping fermentation
Wine 10
grapes naturally frozen on vine at ≤−8°C; harvested and pressed while frozen; the water remains frozen, yielding concentrated juice (typically ≤35° Brix minimum by VQA regulations); slow cold fermentation
Wine 11
aszú berries individually hand-harvested; trampled into paste (göncölszekér historically); must or base wine macerated on the paste; transferred to göncii oak barrels for fermentation and aging  typically 2–3 years in old Hungarian oak casks before release (Source needed — exact for Royal Tokaji 2008)
Wine 12
grapes dried on racks for approximately 3 months ("Malvasia and Trebbiano grapes dry on racks for about three months; they are then soft-pressed and slowly fermented" — source: isoleeolena.it)  11 years in small sealed wooden vessels (caratelli) of various sizes — confirmed from producer website; wine maintains contact with madre (mother yeast) throughout aging
#### e) Comment on quality and maturity.
Wine 9
very good to excellent quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 10
outstanding quality. It is young but developing and it should improve in bottle in the medium term
Wine 11
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it should hold in the short to medium term rather than improve dramatically
Wine 12
outstanding quality. It is developing with some early tertiary complexity and it has the structure to improve further and hold in the long term
